letter : policy and politics at the labour party conference
from mr david soskin

letter : policy and politics at the labour party conference
from m molly meacher
sir : despite a significant gap in the labour party 's education and training proposal , their objective to double the number of 16-18year-olds in full-time education must be welcomed ( 28 september ) .
england 's full-time staying-on rate stand at 28 per cent according to the latest statistic available .
this compare with 79 per cent in the u , 86 per cent in sweden , 45 per cent in west germany and 69 per cent in japan .
to add the part-time education and training statistic to those figure doe not improve our relative position .
labour hope to transform the situation by increasing the number of a-levels to five for the brighter youngster and providing grant for four-year training course ( two year in a further education college followed by two with an employer ) for the rest .
these reform will only achieve their objective if accompanied by the implementation of the 1944 education act provision for compulsory part- or full-time education or training up to the age of 18 .
this clause ha never yet been activated .
the sharply reduced number of 16-18-year-olds will inevitably lead employer to bid up the wage of that age group to entice young people away from education and into unskilled or semi-skilled job .
employer must be required to release 16-18-year-olds for education and training for at least two day per week .
only then will two vitally important and complementary goal be achieved : to improve britain 's general educational and vocational competence , and hence our productive capacity ; and to reduce our gross educational inequality between the elite who receive a narrow education to a very high standard , and the rest .
letter : policy and politics at the labour party conference
from mr john pertwee
sir : labour doe not need a commitment to proportional representation in order to scoop most of the centre ground at the next general election .
all it need is to promise a high-powered investigation , such a a royal commission , into the practicality of an alternative voting system ( surely justified by the intense public interest now abounding ) .
such a promise would be the only hope of a breakthrough for the disenfranchised voter and would have them flocking to the poll for mr kinnock .
his problem will be that mr thatcher might decide to do it first , and then she will garner the vote .
letter : policy and politics at the labour party conference
from mr denis macshane
sir : if labour is to suggest the setting up of a specialist labour court ( 30 september ) , then such a momentous change in the industrial relation system deserves more discussion , and need to be taken out of the hot-house of labour party conference politics .
for three reason such a move should be welcomed .
first , it would bring britain into line with the best european practice , notably in germany where local , regional and federal labour court handle problem in a fair , dispassionate way based on law , precedent and judicial expertise in the labour field .
second , it would ensure that worker enjoyed positive right under law and reduce the emphasis of union immunity .
labour law will always reflect the balance of power in society , and a fully fledged labour court system will not remove class and fundamental employer-employee difference .
but it should be possible to create and enforce enough common rule to prevent the absurd see-sawing of industrial relation legislation we have seen since 1969 .
third , a labour division of the high court would contribute to the process of constitutional reform in britain which surely is to be a key political issue in the 1990s .
the â€˜ trade union question â€™ would cease to haunt the labour party and while that might upset some conservative it would be good for the country .

letter : ec immigration check on train
from the rev j. gilmore
sir : the content of sarah helm 's article â€˜ immigration check to be made on train â€™ ( 25 september ) are extremely worrying .
during the recent european community election the representative of ec government promoted the ideal of a border-free europe by 1992 .
now , it would appear from this article , that this ideal is shattered and a two-tiered europe is on the card .
one for those who travel by aircraft and one for those who travel by train between london and paris .
the presence of immigration official and police on board train is comparable to the scene of 1930s america where migrant labourer were turned back at state boundary .
what criterion would police and immigration official use in their search for â€˜ potential terrorist â€™ on a train that they could not use at a static checkpoint or airport ?
do terrorist or illegal person travel exclusively by train ?
this is a clear example of the extension of the prevention of terrorism act where criterion for stopping , questioning and searching are purely subjective to the official involved .
will a person 's accent , dress or skin colour indicate the potential terrorist or , for that matter , football hooligan or drug pusher ?
this is a recipe for fear and anxiety .
it may mean the detention of innocent people and indeed their imprisonment .

latecomer who continue to hope
from anne applebaum in warsaw
dozen more east german refugee arrived outside the west german embassy yesterday seeking asylum and passage to the west , including some who missed by only a few minute a train that took more than 800 of their compatriot to west germany .
many of the new arrival , who waited in the street outside the embassy , said they were convinced there would be another train for them .
the one they missed pulled out from warsaw 's east station at 2.50am yesterday carrying 809 cheering east german bound for the rhine region of west germany .
once upon a time another german government let lenin cross another germany in a sealed train on his way to russia .
more than 80 year later , lenin 's heir are allowing refugee from their communist regime escape in the opposite direction , in an east german-made train travelling across east german territory .
among those leaving wa cornelia , 22 , a young east berliner destined , she said , for west berlin via a very circuitous route .
â€˜ i still do n't know if my family know where i am or if they will suffer from my decision .
but i saw no other way out : i did n't know if i would ever have a chance again . â€™
some of the refugee had been living in polish exile for a long a seven week , while others arrived only a few hour before west german diplomat announced they could leave .
only two failed to go : a new mother and her child , who wa born in a polish hospital four day ago .
the east german government 's decision to let the refugee leave across their own country came a a surprise to many , including some west german diplomat .
some of the refugee originally refused to touch east german territory again , but were reassured when two of the west german diplomat said they would accompany the train , to ensure that the rule were followed .
during the journey , east german official will enter the train , and collect all east german passport .
west german will then issue new one .
legally , the refugee have been expelled from east germany , meaning that under current rule they can not return .
according to unofficial report , the arrangement wa designed by the west german foreign minister , han dietrich genscher , the new polish foreign minister , krzystof skubiszewski , and the soviet foreign minister , eduard shevardnadze , who met recently at the united nation in new york .
communist allow burial of montenegro 's last king
from marcus tanner in cetinje , yugoslavia
the people of montenegro yesterday welcomed home the remains of nicholas i , their first and last king , after 70 year ' exile in italy , in belated fulfilment of his last will and testament .
thousand of montenegrins , many in national costume , waited at the quayside in bar for the italian warship that would bring him home .
then they formed a slow procession behind the coffin into the mountain to the old royal capital of cetinje for his reburial in the monastery there .
what wa surely the last ever montenegrin â€˜ royal event â€™ wa masterminded by the republic 's new communist leadership , which ha abruptly stopped describing nicholas a a class enemy , to ride the same wave of popular patriotism and royal fervour which swept serbia in the recent celebration of the 600th anniversary of the battle of kosovo .
the latest biography now call him â€˜ an outstanding statesman , warrior and man of letter â€™ .
â€˜ if i die abroad in exile , let my body rest in a temporary grave until my mortal remains be transferred to our dear homeland , â€™ said the montenegrin president , branko kostic , reading the royal will and testament to the crowd in cetinje square , who carried flag and portrait of nicholas and his queen , milena .
â€˜ today the people of montenegro fulfill the wish of their first and last king guided by innate love and respect for their history , â€™ he went on .
â€˜ our tourist organisation are counting on big profit , â€™ said srdan darmanovic , a member of the montenegrin central committee .
â€˜ this will be good for the montenegrin economy too . â€™
montenegro 's young and go-ahead leadership , which came to power this year amid furious popular dissatisfaction with the â€˜ old men in grey suit â€™ , sponsored the gathering of nicholas 's surviving relative â€” the first â€˜ royal reunion â€™ organised by a communist government .
â€˜ a letter dropped through the post from the president of the socialist republic of montenegro , inviting me to the funeral , â€™ said john kennedy , a conservative party member who life in barking and is a distant relative of nicholas .
montenegro 's sudden rehabilitation of nicholas 's memory is a popular move .
â€˜ he liberated u from the turk and fought for our independence , â€™ said a small boy who waited at the quayside amid a jostling crowd of former monarch , visiting serbian bishop and television crew .
marko radoman , 103 , and a veteran of both balkan war , wa given a place at the front a one of the few with personal memory of nicholas 's reign .
for a man whom rebecca west , a contemporary balkan observer , called â€˜ repulsive â€™ and â€˜ treacherous â€™ for deserting his serbian son-in-law , king alexander , in the first world war , it is a sepia-tinted view of history .
nicholas wa known a â€˜ the uncle of europe â€™ for his success in marrying his beautiful but penniless daughter into the grander royal house of russia , serbia and italy .
this furthered his long-term ambition to rule a large â€˜ south slav â€™ kingdom when the ottoman empire finally collapsed .
one daughter , a grand duchess of russia , even gained a footnote in history by introducing an unknown monk , rasputin , to the russian tsarina alexandra .
in his minute , inaccessible capital , which today ha only 15,000 inhabitant , nicholas erected a miniature european court and an exquisitely furnished victorian palace .
he even persuaded 12 european power to turn cottage into full embassy .
it wa a fair achievement in a town which an earlier observer described a â€˜ 44 hovel â€™ .
but nicholas 's grand design collapsed in 1918 when his serbian son-in-law , alexander , deposed him and incorporated montenegro into yugoslavia .
obituary : virgil thomson
by peter dickinson
virgil thomson wa a composer and writer of originality , courage and wit formed by a unique mixture of american and french influence .
thomson wa born in kansa city in 1896 , studied at harvard , and then mostly lived in paris until 1940 .
his missouri childhood gave him access to baptist hymn and church music , but at harvard he wa introduced to the writing of gertrude stein and the music of erik satie .
these influence laid the foundation for the blend of the naive and the sophisticated which is the hallmark of thomson 's maturity .
like many young american composer he studied with nadia boulanger , but after 1925 set out to achieve the â€˜ discipline of spontaneity â€™ which he found in stein 's writing .
when he collaborated directly with her in opera the result wa of historical significance .
first came four saint in three act , produced in 1934 , and then the mother of u all ( 1947 ) , which demonstrated a new kind of plotless opera only now reaching a wider currency through the stage work of philip glass .
in four saint thomson 's informality wa given free reign since he first of all improvised the music at the piano then , when it stuck , wrote it down to a figured bass .
he said : â€˜ i had wondered whether a piece so drenched in anglican chant ( running from gilbert and sullivan to morning prayer and back ) could rise and sail . â€™
but the resulting mixture of hymn , folksy tune and recitative â€” at time of intoxicating banality â€” wa a sensation .
the opera , like gershwin 's porgy and be the following year , used an all-black cast and the staging and choreography gave it a british connection since it wa by frederick ashton .
the mother of u all show more continuity , but again much depends upon production in what is still a largely non-narrative opera .
in 1936 , three year before aaron copland , thomson started writing film score which were a model of unobtrusive clarity and pertinence , combining american material to suit documentary such a the plow that broke the plain and the river with the musique d'ameublement approach of satie .
his score for the film louisiana story wa awarded the pulitzer prize in 1948 .
another response to a visual stimulus wa thomson 's series of musical portrait of people such a picasso and aaron copland .
the first of these wa written in 1928 and they number about 150 .
just a thomson transferred stein 's literary abstraction to music , so these portrait unusually employ the painter 's technique of drawing the sitter from life : an ideal combination of thomson 's habit of immediate response with an actual subject .
the result are terse and sharply etched , like the best line drawing .
thomson could control word with the same fastidiousness a note .
he always felt that effective musical criticism began by being well written , and he never lost his liveliness .
one never quite knew what he wa going to say next .
he started by writing occasional article but in 1939 he wrote the state of music , a trenchant and provocative assessment of the machinery of the musical profession at the time .
from 1940 to 1954 he wrote regularly for the new york herald tribune and four volume of his notice have appeared in book form .
he wrote a fulsome 400-page autobiography , characteristically entitled virgil thomson on virgil thomson , and a personal survey , american music since 1910 , appeared in 1972 , followed recently by a selected edition of his letter .
a both composer and critic he touched both side of the coin : a climate of receptivity is what the artist most desire .
it is also a thing that even the most experienced reviewer can not always offer .
thomson 's judgement about music were not always a accurate a his confident expression implied : he invited discussion .
above all he restored the focus of music criticism onto the music itself .
he made no secret of his francophile enthusiasm , in reaction to the earlier domination of american music by german , and he wa one of the first writer to register the importance of jazz .
he fearlessly attacked convention , which caused problem when he pitched into established reputation .
it ha become increasingly apparent that thomson belonged to the american experimental tradition .
he anticipated some of the enthusiasm of john cage , whom he first met in 1941 .
a few year later thomson recognised cage a â€˜ the most original composer in america , if not the world â€¦ â€™ and cage repaid the compliment by writing the work section of a monograph on thomson .
but finally , in spite of their common root in satie and stein , thomson felt that cage 's aesthetic wa destructive .
thomson 's song contain some of his finest work .
there are stein setting , such a â€˜ portrait of fb â€™ , but also cycle such a five song from william blake , prayer and praise and a cantata based on poem by edward lear .
thomson wa also prolific in instrumental music on both chamber and solo scale .
for many year thomson lived in new york in his apartment at the chelsea hotel .
from there , like a buzzard in it eyrie , he would make foray round the u and abroad in spite of his advanced age .
he seemed a genial and indestructible landmark in the history of american music , in spite of defective hearing which had bothered him since the late seventy .
he had a photograph of himself on his postcard and he signed his letter following the original tailpiece â€˜ everbest â€™ .
thomson came from a generation of american composer which had to find it own way without benefit of university patronage .
in that kind of free market he thrived and survived â€” and so will the best of his work .
obituary : thomas keller
by hugh matheson
thomi keller is the outstanding figure in the history of international rowing .
he wa president of fisa ( federation internationale de societes d'aviron ) , the world governing body , for 30 year ; and most of this body 's attitude and practice reflect the force of his strict , but genial personality .
most athlete first encountered him a a voice , bellowing in multi-lingual fury at official who had broken rule or arrangement designed to make racing safer or more fair .
he wa elected to succeed gaston mullegg a president of fisa when he wa 33 and still competing in the swiss rowing team .
he did not loosen his link with the athlete when projected upwards into the presidency .
provided their behaviour wa sportsmanlike and safe , oarsman found only encouragement and humour from a president who wa always among them .
keller 's zurich upbringing made him into a skiier and sculler , and he raced for the grasshopper club .
he competed for the swiss cross-country and ski-jumping team at the student winter game and later concentrated on single scull .
in 1950 , he wa swiss scull champion and finished third in the european championship before moving to manila to work for his family 's private multinational company , eduard keller ltd , for four year .
on his return , keller wa swiss champion again in 1955 and wa bitterly disappointed when the swiss chose to boycott the 1956 olympics after the russian intervention in hungary .
fisa wa then a relatively unimportant body which organised european and other championship and participated in the running of olympic regatta .
a a rich , forceful young man , keller had been earmarked a a likely future president , even before mullegg wa killed in an aircrash in 1958 .
rowing wa transformed in the breadth and depth of participation over the 30 year of his term .
he sat on the appropriate board of the international olympic committee and wa president of the general assembly of international sport federation for 17 year .
these position were not held for his own status but to promote the cause of rowing where it needed clout .
he successfully defended rowing a an olympic sport and in 1976 introduced woman 's event , which have now expanded to six class .
his personal preference for sculling over rowing assisted the introduction of quadruple scull to world championship and olympic competition .
keller wa made a steward of henley royal regatta in 1976 , a time when the proliferation of new six-lane , still-water course around the world , which had emerged under his guidance , seemed a threat to the regatta founded in 1829 .
but it wa the spirit of henley , with huge crowd enjoying amateur sport , presented with administrative finesse , which keller took a the yardstick for the international circuit .
he loved the place and he returned every year to play an often small part a umpire of race between undistinguished club far below the olympic standard he wa obliged to administer .
he said this summer that a he gradually wound down his involvement in sport , henley would be the last thing he would give up .
during his term a president of fisa he gradually reduced his role in eduard keller ltd and used the company office to provide a world headquarters for rowing .
the sport is now obliged to find income from the more normal source of television and sponsorship , to replace his largely unacknowledged private patronage .
in spite of his gift to the sport , keller wa anxious not to abuse his status a president and never went to a rowing event unless invited .
because of this , he had never seen the oxford and cambridge boat race until this year .
he wa enthralled by the entire proceeding and remarked that only the english could make a contest between two crew which would not rank in the world 's top 10 into a bigger spectacle than the olympic final .
the final task he set himself for his presidency , which wa due to end next year , wa to weed out of the regatta calendar all the rowing course which could be made unfair by the wind .
he had recently travelled from tasmania to norway in search of the perfect 2,000 metre of flat water .
it wa a blessing that , in response to congratulation on the superb world championship regatta at bled in yugoslavia three week ago , he wa able to reply , â€˜ yes â€¦ and best of all , the condition were fair throughout â€™ .
the labour party conference : cook appeal for labour to accept electoral reform
by colin hughes and colin brown
an impassioned appeal for labour to back electoral reform wa made by a leading shadow cabinet member yesterday after the national executive decided overwhelmingly to oppose a proportional representation motion .
robin cook , a member of the national executive and the health spokesman , said that a labour government which sought to force it programme on an unwilling nation would be unsocialist .
he told a labour co-ordinating committee rally that it wa not enough for labour to win the next election .
it would also be necessary â€˜ to smash the decrepit , effete constitution that allows a minority to capture power , and then use it ruthlessly in the interest of the privileged few â€™ .
mr cook argued that 10 year of thatcherism had been forced on most people , who had never voted conservative , and that many of those who opposed pr in the labour party wanted to use the same undemocratic power â€˜ to ram socialism down the throat of a majority who did n't vote for it â€™ .
advocate of the first-past-the-post system argued that it wa the only way of delivering strong government , mr cook said .
but after 10 year of tory rule , he had had enough of that approach to politics .
labour would be acquiescing in a â€˜ democratic outrage â€™ if it continued arguing that , because the conservative had got away with being undemocratic for so long , it wa now labour 's turn .
mr cook also countered the argument that pr would build in a pull towards the centre ground , preventing labour from implementing a radical programme .
it seemed strange to make that argument , mr cook said , when it wa clear the whole policy review process wa designed to placate the centre ground .
â€˜ i can not honestly see that a system which would require u to compromise the morning after the election is really so morally inferior to a system which ha already obliged u to compromise our policy two year before polling day . â€™
his speech , however , came aftera 24-4 vote in the national executive to oppose a proportional representation motion which will be debated on thursday .
the four were mr cook , ken livingstone , john evans and clare short .
mr livingstone said he wa against pact , but 10 year of thatcherism had demonstrated the danger of giving power to elite .
however , neil kinnock , the labour leader , and roy hattersley , his deputy , both opposed the motion .
mr hattersley said giving disproportionate power to small , minority centre party would not improve democracy .
mr kinnock said it wa wrong to propose a fundamental change in the voting system for a short-term tactical vote to get rid of the thatcher government .
the only way to remove the government wa for labour to win the next general election .
mr kinnock intends to reinforce his conviction that labour is in a position to win in his keynote speech tomorrow .
party organiser want the conference to show that the party is united a never before , leaving mr kinnock free for the first time to sell the policy package to voter without worrying about split .
there is expected to be a close vote on defence , but mr kinnock is confident the new policy will be affirmed today .
the executive is recommending rejection of a motion calling for unilateral disarmament .
it is also urging the conference to oppose a motion , supported by the national union of mineworker , to commit a labour government to abolishing all nuclear power within 15 year .
there could also be controversy over the executive 's rejection of demand for the age of consent for homosexual to be reduced to 16 year .

by celia dodd
these day , no one question the importance of good book for the under-fives .
but book that appeal to adult too are just a important ; we are , after all , the one who have to do the reading .
there are few thing worse than being bludgeoned into reading a book you hate .
story-reading should be one of the great joy of parenthood , a real meeting of adult and child mind .
with the ahlbergs ' book , it very often is .
the husband-and-wife partnership behind such classic a the jolly postman , burglar bill and the baby 's catalogue win child over with ripping yarn and a wicked sense of humour .
they win their parent over with a rose-tinted vision of the world which is decidedly reassuring .
their book are usually set nostalgically in the past â€” the thirty and forty are favourite period â€” and often feature the perennial character of fairy tale and nursery rhyme .
yet they are saved from sentimentality by the wry humour of both text and picture .
the ahlbergs have been accused of not facing up to the harsh reality of life , of being too cosy and sweet , but their latest picture book , bye bye baby , published today , break the mould .
in it , a small child roams the street , talking to stranger , until he finally go off with a woman who ha been wandering about pushing an empty pram .
curious subject matter for a child 's picture book , you might think , and hardly guaranteed to win the approval of the gimlet-eyed child 's book world .
but the ahlbergs have no time for literal interpretation of their work .
how , they argue , would alice , or the wind in the willow , stand up against such scrutiny ?
the way to look at bye bye baby , they insist , is a a â€˜ light , surreal comedy â€™ .
â€˜ certainly if you bring a literal interpretation to the story , it 's a minefield , â€™ allan ahlberg say .
bye bye baby get away with it particular minefield thanks to the sleight of illustrator janet ahlberg 's hand .
the visual inspiration for the book wa the famous advert for start-rite shoe , showing a small boy and girl walking off down a tree-lined avenue â€” an innocent image if ever there wa one .
the ahlberg version feature a solitary figure .
the advert gave the story it vaguely thirty setting .
the success of the ahlbergs ' working relationship must have a lot to do with their long and happy marriage .
in spite of apparently incompatible personality â€” she is small and shy , he is large and doe most of the talking â€” they have an empathy which is essential to the way they work , a persistent mutual pestering .
the strength of the partnership is highlighted for allan , who also work with a number of other artist .
the time-consuming nature of janet 's part in the process , combined with a bad back which prevents her spending more than a couple of hour at the drawing board every day , mean she can not keep up with her husband 's prolific output .
â€˜ with other illustrator , i work at a distance â€” we visit and phone , but there is a point at which i ca n't pester them any more , â€™ allan say .
they see themselves not a author and illustrator , with separate role , but a a partnership of â€˜ book-makers â€™ , contributing equally to the process .
allan ahlberg say : â€˜ in the past , a lot of child 's book seemed to be the work of talented illustrator whose picture looked brilliant framed in a gallery , but when you tried to read the book , there wa nothing there , because the word started a a coat-hanger to hang picture on . â€™
given the importance they place on the interrelationship of word and picture , the couple 's working life are surprisingly separate .
they claim to see each other le than couple who go out to work , meeting for the occasional cup of coffee in the kitchen .
their 10-year-old daughter , jessica , who is likely to be seen with her head buried in the beano or a roald dahl novel , ha provided the inspiration for many of her parent ' book .
a a baby , she wa fascinated by the mothercare catalogue , and so the baby 's catalogue , which document the paraphernalia of the infant 's small world in minute detail , wa born .
allan 's experience a a primary school teacher is another rich source of idea .
the couple still maintain close link with local school , where they spend hour researching , sketching and absorbing joke .
after 10 year of teaching allan had had enough .
â€˜ i ran out of steam .
it 's a killing job .
little kid eat you alive , they want so much physically and emotionally . â€™
every year he would have half a dozen nine- and 10-year-olds in his class who could barely read .
he treated reading a a team effort , getting them to read the daily mirror rather than the dull , babyish book on offer .
these day , he writes the book intended to help child learn to read .
â€˜ a lot of book used in school are technically readable but rather drab story .
there 's no reason why they should n't be funny or gripping even if they do n't use many word . â€™
writing book for child is becoming increasingly complex , a the arbiter of the modern child 's more â€” librarian , teacher and publisher â€” lay down ever more stringent criterion .
â€˜ the problem with child 's book increasingly is that there are army of people who are properly concerned with , for example , the way girl were always discriminated against in the past , â€™ say allan .
â€˜ that is bad , and must be thought about .
but people seem to think that story are written to a prescription , that you sit down and say , you do n't want it to be sexist , you do n't want it to be racist .
in reality you just invent a tale , a if you were sitting round a fire in a cave . â€™
in fact anti-racism and anti-sexism seem to come naturally to the ahlbergs .
their book are full of female breadwinner and new men changing nappy and comforting bawling baby in the small hour .
although they are a principled pair â€” they once turned down the smarties award on moral ground â€” the ahlbergs reject the notion of child 's author a moralist .
â€˜ i would say , just sit down and tell a good story , and to hell with the morality , â€™ allan ahlberg declares .
with that characteristically strong statement , he put his finger on the secret of the ahlbergs ' phenomenal success with adult and child alike .
the revolution the countryside need : christian wolmar say britain is lagging behind in setting up â€˜ telecottages â€™
by christian wolmar
the information technology revolution ha left large swath of rural britain untouched .
the only computer in many village are those owned by the teenage boy of the affluent to play their wham-bam game .
ashley dobbs , an engaging young entrepreneur , is setting out to change that by creating a nationwide network of â€˜ telecottages â€™ .
this clever name , with it neat evocation of new and old , describes a concept which wa developed in sweden and is spreading to rural area across the world .
in mr dobbs 's word , a telecottage is â€˜ a room in a village filled with computer communication that can be used by local people to learn and work â€™ .
the equipment consists of , perhaps , four word processor , a modem , a laser printer able to produce artwork-quality output , a fax and a photocopier .
software programme for desk-top publishing , word processing , accounting and business planning are also essential .
mr dobbs , who is chairman of telecottages uk , warns possible user not to get bogged down with the technology : â€˜ the equipment is a tool to overcome what need to be done .
people must n't think that because there 's a computer , they 've got to think of a use for it . â€™
he reckons there are no shortage of possible us â€” farmer can use it to do their account , woman with child-care responsibility can work in their own time on the word processor , publicity material for local craftsman can be produced and so on .
this may sound utopian , but it ha been borne out by the experience of existing telecottages .
so far about 100 have been established , mainly in scandinavia but also in japan and several third world country .
a the network expands , so do the us .
the first wa opened in the village of vemdalen in the remote forest of sweden in september 1985 .
the impact , according to henning albrechsten , president of telecottages international who ha been involved in their development since the outset , wa immediate .
â€˜ by holding open meeting for child and having the telecottage above the local store , people of all walk of life came in - small-scale entrepreneur and their spouse , shop assistant , craftspeople and child .
now , 15 per cent of the village regularly us telecottage and at least 20 per cent ha taken course there . â€™
britain is lagging behind .
no telecottages have been established , but there are several initiative in the offing .
the most advanced is a plan for five in the north of scotland which will be funded by the highland and island development board and british telecom .
colin pavey of british telecom hope that they will be running by the end of the year .
â€˜ two would be business-orientated and three would be community-orientated and located in school .
we are most interested in the idea of networking , sharing skill such a translation and using them for distance learning . â€™
telephone cable in the region are being upgraded to ensure they are capable of handling fax .
mr dobbs 's more unlikely ally are the woman 's institute .
most of the worker in telecottages and many of the user are woman .
he say : â€˜ in village there are many woman with skill who are n't using them because of child-care problem .
but they are interested in working .
user of telecottages are given the key and people can use them at any time , which mean that woman can come in during the evening , early in the morning or whatever . â€™
he is working on a scheme with the national federation of wi to organise a competition to celebrate next year 's 75th anniversary by offering 10 telecottages a prize for it 9,000 branch and he is hoping the wi will establish a pilot telecottage near it college in denman , oxfordshire .
while telecottages should become self-financing once established , they do need initial subsidy .
the cost of providing the start-up equipment is in the order of â£10,000 and mr dobbs think space is not much of a problem : â€˜ there 's usually the village hall or an empty shop . â€™
henning albrechsten reckons that it take three year for a telecottage to be able to function without subsidy .
in scandinavia , with their mixed economy , money is always available , after a bit of persuasion , from both local and central government .
in britain , the tight financial regime imposed on local council and the reluctance of central government to take on any new expenditure make it necessary to seek fund from the private sector .
the only government support so far ha been a recommendation by the rural development commission , part of the department of the environment , to establish a pilot scheme .
mr dobbs sigh : â€˜ this is a typically british bureaucratic response .
it 's been proved elsewhere that they work .
we do n't need pilot , we need telecottages . â€™
a for the private sector , while bt may be happy to provide pump priming for five telecottages in the north of scotland , it is not about to bankroll a nationwide network .
nor , it seems , is anyone else .
there is more at stake here than just bringing box of sophisticated equipment to remote area .
a mr dobbs , who run a country house restoration business from his home in micheldever , hampshire , say : â€˜ village are good place to live and work .
the village is a fantastic institution and they make people happier . â€™
but the village are dying , becoming suburb or dormitory where few people work but many sleep .
employment in agriculture , still the main rural industry , fell from over a million in the mid-sixties to under 100,000 now .
the aim of telecottages is to create well-paid , flexible employment which will provide new job and make existing rural industry more competitive .
the emphasis on high wage is important .
village are expensive to live in and if they are to become vibrant community again , local people must be able to earn enough to be able to continue living in them .
there is a wider impact , too .
the abstract talk of commuting by rail or road being replaced by information technology find a concrete expression in the idea of telecottages .
once people overcome their fear about computer and begin to use them in telecottages , they acquire their own equipment .
many may eventually be able to work from home rather than commute to an office .
henning albrechsten say : â€˜ modern information technology ha given people in remote region the opportunity to overcome their worst handicap ; their distance from the centre of learning and development . â€™
he see them a not only strengthening small community , but also a actually beginning to reverse the population flow from the countryside to the town .
britain , with it growing traffic problem , seems an ideal place for the onset of this revolution .
despite mr dobbs 's tireless effort , this is another revolution which britain is likely to be viewing from the sideline .

health update : zinc deficiency in pregnancy
by olivia timbs
fad for vitamin supplement have been overtaken by a vogue for mineral and trace element .
zinc is the latest : lack of it supposedly cause a range of problem , although little scientific evidence support the link .
but some report have suggested that low zinc level may lead to problem in pregnancy , from difficult labour to congenital malformation in child .
there is stronger evidence that a zinc deficiency may cause the foetus to grow slowly in the womb .
doctor in bristol decided to test the theory .
in a study of nearly 500 woman , they found that zinc supplement did not improve either foetal health or reduce the number of pre-term labour .
the doctor conclude in the british medical journal that there would be little benefit if all woman in the uk were given zinc supplement during pregnancy .
they recognise , however , that the woman in their study were largely middle-class , who might well have sufficiently large zinc store because they were well nourished .
further study , the researcher believe , should concentrate on pregnant woman known to have a zinc deficiency or to be at risk from problem during labour .

by olivia timbs
renee henry underwent a lumpectomy and radiotherapy for her breast cancer .
dr maurice slevin , a consultant physician at the department of medical oncology at st bartholomew 's hospital , london , said the chance of a woman being alive and well four year after such treatment were at least 75 per cent .
mr henry refused the drug treatment given to reduce the chance of a relapse , but there are many who would remain well without it , dr slevin said .
part of the attraction of alternative cancer treatment such a the gerson therapy is that they allow a patient to feel more in control of the treatment .
dr slevin agreed that this is often beneficial , but is concerned about other aspect of such therapy .
very few have been evaluated , and the evidence is largely anecdotal , he said .
some of the treatment have dramatic side-effects .
â€˜ if they do work , that is wonderful â€” but we have no way of knowing for sure .

by john lichfield
where are the tv western of yesteryear ?
what happened to ben cartwright , ho and little joe ?
where is the wagon train that seemed permanently to be circling it wagon waiting for a drunken pioneer 's wife to have twin in the last of them ?
the tv western seemed to fade into the sunset some time in the mid-1970s , victim of a narcissistic age that preferred to look at prosperous , smart-alecky or violent image of itself ( dallas , cheer , miami vice ) .
it wa a time when child were encouraged to fantasize about machine and outer space rather than marvel at the open space where the deer and buffalo roamed .
now , however , it seems , the wheel ha turned full circle .
the western is being revived , reinterpreted for the 1990s .
one of the most successful hollywood tv producer , david jacob , the creator of dallas , belief the western also fell victim to late 1960s iconoclasm .
â€˜ the john wayne stereotype wa rejected , along with his conservative value , â€™ he said .
a series of spoof or obsessively realistic 1960s western ( cat balou , butch cassidy and the sundance kid , soldier blue , the wild bunch ) stripped the mask from the lone ranger 's face .

howard rosenberg , tv critic of the los angeles time , said : â€˜ just look at the re-runs of the old western series .
you had hero scotch-taped to their horse and lightly sprinkled with dust .
you ca n't get away with that any more . â€™
john mantley , producer of gunsmoke for cbs for 11 year , say element of the old story began to fall apart .
â€˜ we all read bury my heart at wounded knee .
we knew that the u government had signed 453 treaty with the indian and broken every one .
a part of the myth just did n't work after that . â€™
at their peak in the mid-60s , there were 39 western series on the three american tv network â€” almost two a day on each channel .
in the u weekly audience rating in 1965 , there were three western in the top five , and seven in the top 10 .
by the mid-80s they had all gone the way of the buffalo : extinct save for preservation in the national park of permanent re-runs .
gene autry , the first tv cowboy , now 82 , say western dominated the early day of tv partly because they had lively , cheaply produced visuals , but mainly because early tv wa starved of feature film by the studio .
western were a handy substitute .
there are encouraging sign , however , that the tv western is struggling back into the saddle .
paradise , launched on cbs last season and shown on bbc1 this summer , wa the first new western serial for a decade .
it wa the only new drama series in u network tv to be allowed a second series this year , despite a relatively lowly 49th ranking in the rating .
it creator and executive producer is , perhaps surprisingly , david jacob .
why did he follow j r ewing , the epitome of 1980s tv , with a western about a gunfighter turned foster father ?
â€˜ i believed the genre wa still an important and powerful one .
it 's something i 've always had an affection for .
i found it wa possible with paradise to talk about individual and personal responsibility , something which if set in a contemporary context would have had a preachy feel about it . â€™
lonesome dove , a five-part story of a texas-to-montana cattle drive , also shown on cbs last year , wa the best-watched tv mini-series of any kind in america for five year .
it won substantial critical acclaim and will show in britain next year .
the 1989-90 season , which started this month , brought another new prime-time western series , abc 's young rider , which tell the story of the pony express .
it also say a lot about modern american tv .
because the network believe western appeal mainly to middle-aged men , young rider ha a cast of five pouting young male actor , in an attempt to make a western with â€˜ good demographic â€™ .
the effect is rather like an extended advertisement for marlboro light .
western are no longer a cheap option .
the classic western tv series were filmed in back-lots , or in the semi-desert 20 mile from los angeles .
modern audience no longer accept plastic bush , and the southern californian suburban sprawl mean the production company have to take their horse and cow to location far away in arizona or montana .

â€˜ at one time there were dozen of company which specialised in renting out western paraphernalia .

maybe .

motoring : a lead-free lover 's guide to used car : driver with a tight budget and an environmental conscience can buy second-hand without losing principle , say james ruppert
by james ruppert
from this month , all new car submitted for british type approval , the test which allows them to be legally sold in the uk , must be able to run on lead-free petrol .
a a result of this rule , and many carefully orchestrated publicity campaign , the motor industry 's image ha now changed from polluter to pal of the earth .
if you can afford an environmentally friendly g-registered runabout , then the flora , fauna and child of the british isle will have a lot to thank you for .
but if you have a tight budget and a conscience , running older car on lead-free fuel becomes a much more complicated issue .
without the presence of a lead compound , an engine 's valve seat go unprotected .
this lead to premature wear and plume of blue smoke spewing from the exhaust .
the only effective remedy is to install hardened valve seat or a replacement cylinder head , both expensive option .
le costly , but not entirely satisfactory , is the practice of making every fourth tankful leaded petrol , which protects the valve seat but rather defeat the object .
alternatively there are several chemical additive on the market which aim to provide a substitute for lead , although the case for these product ha yet to be proved .
however , many car built within the last few year have hardened valve seat , or alloy cylinder head that need little ( simply a retune ) or no modification to use unleaded petrol .
finding out which car can and which can not is not difficult .
esso filling station have a free booklet listing most model produced since 1980 , a doe the charity clear ( 01-387 4970 ) , with more detail available in the unleaded petrol guide ( autodale , â£2.99 ) .
looking at those list provides interesting reading and bear out the used-car trade 's view that having a lead-only car could prove to be a liability .
it is not a if four-star leaded fuel is going to be phased out tomorrow , but more and more buyer are asking : â€˜ doe it take unleaded ? â€™
what is certain is that the pre-1989 austin/mg range , all skodas , yugos and quite a few lancias do not , which doe not help already depressed resale price .
a 1985 lancia y10 ( lead only ) sell for around â£2,000 whereas the similar fiat uno 55 from the same year , converted to run on unleaded , is worth around â£500 more .
a lead-only second-hand car is now worth about 10 per cent le than an unleaded equivalent .
in year to come the percentage difference is likely to be much larger .
even so , exotica such a lead-only porsche 911s and ferraris are unlikely to be part of this trend , so no bargain there i 'm afraid .
more impressive are the japanese manufacturer whose car dating from the early eighty can run on unleaded without adjustment .
find a 1980 toyota corolla or 1982 nissan sunny that ha not succumbed to rust , and you can buy with confidence .
on the home front , most ford and vauxhalls from at least the mid-eighties can use unleaded .
if the â€˜ greenness â€™ of going unleaded still seems a bit of a grey area , it is possible to switch to diesel without a shadow of environmental doubt .
diesel always ha been lead-free and the only adjustment you need to make are mental one , because diesel-powered car are no longer slow , smelly or noisy .
they also return more mile to the gallon than their petrol equivalent , although service interval are shorter .
but after the publicity on unleaded fuel there ha been increased interest in used diesel power .
but remember that these car accrue , and can withstand , significantly higher mileage than petrol car , 50-90,000 being the norm .
the tradition for diesel car is far better established on the continent and that is where the best buy come from .
perhaps the most successful maker is peugeot , with a complete range of car from the small 205 â€” around â£3,000 for a 90,000-mile 1985 car â€” to the legendary 504 estate , about â£2,000 for a 1982 example .
the german are also fairly accomplished manufacturer , although a five-door 1980 golf with a six-figure mileage will still cost â£1,500 .
prestige come in the shape of a mercedes with the 240d or â€˜ stuttgart taxi â€™ , commanding â£7,000 but returning unfailing reliability .
even if lead level in the atmosphere do not bother you , i am sure you will find the fact that unleaded petrol is 15p cheaper , and that diesel fuel boost mpg by at least a third , most interesting .
auction : dazzling rich of the orient
by roger bell
it is asian week at sotheby 's and christie 's .
they are mixing islamic art from the middle east with indian and south-east asian .
the sale combine item of charm at fairly accessible price with some rare treasure aimed at big-time collector .
homely persian pottery of the twelfth to thirteenth century combine animal and bird image with sinuous plant motif and mark the beginning of lustre ware ; they are mostly priced in the â£500 to â£1,000 range and rate high on charm .
the iznik pottery from turkey dating from the fifteenth and sixteenth century explodes with colour on more sophisticated white ground ; iznik is now highly sought after by turkish yuppie and price run from â£2,000 or so to â£15,000 .
both house combine elegant early metalware ( twelfth/thirteenth century ) , bronze vessel with silver inlay â€” which are expensive â€” with nineteenth- century imitation , more flashy but in the mid-hundreds .
there are plenty of indian miniature , colourful and charming evocation of princely court , priced in hundred or low thousand .
stone figure chipped off the war-torn temple of cambodia are more expensive ; a striking khmer figure will cost â£20,000 or more .
there are also outstanding bit of indian stone monument , with god and goddess sensuously entwined in spiritual embrace ; they start with the gandhara grey schist carving of the third/fourth century .

drink : desire in search of a name : do the french have the â€˜ mot juste â€™ to describe the desire for a reviving drink ?
in the first of a monthly series , michael jackson hunt in vain
by michael jackson
thank god it 's saturday .
i know that today is saturday because already i am thirsty for a dry , hoppy pint of bitter at the end of the afternoon : an aperitif to sharpen my appetite for this evening 's indian meal .
how did you feel a you came to the end of your working week yesterday afternoon ?
boy , i had a thirst .
we say we are thirsty when we mean that we fancy a glass of wine , a pint of beer , a gin and tonic or a shot of whisky â€” the pleasure of naming our poison â€” at the end of a busy day or tiresome week .
that is not thirst .
to enjoy it , we do not need to be dry : just in need of a restorative or relaxant , or maybe a focus for a social moment or brief encounter .
we call it â€˜ thirst â€™ in lieu of a more accurate description .
no , not more accurate : â€˜ thirst â€™ is not accurate at all a a description for this apparently indescribable condition .
it is a desire million of u know and share , yet for which we have no name .
this desire that can not find it name ( though it would dare speak it , if it could ) is pleasurable .
it is a enjoyable a feeling gently hungry or amorous .
no , not amorous : randy â€” we have a word for that .
any of these condition is pleasurable so long a there is a good likelihood of satisfaction in reasonably short order .
let puritan and pessimist damn , disapprove or dismiss these appetite ; that is their loss .
those of u who enjoy them need only a name for each condition , so that we may discus them in expectation and in recollection .
come to think of it , neither hunger nor randiness hit quite the spot .
peckishness and lust , perhaps ?
or doe that sound like a firm of country solicitor ?
this is to digress .
what of this thirst that can be satisfied by an unquenching drink ?
wine is not quenching , but no le in demand for that .
try to quench your thirst with a light , fresh white and you will drink enough to put you on your back .
beer is , quite rightly , britain 's favourite friday night drink .
it can momentarily blunt a thirst , but not altogether cut it .
the nearest to being quenching is the â€˜ white â€™ beer of berlin .
anything with even a modest smack of hop dry the palate ready for the next one .
so , of course , doe alcohol itself .
it may for a moment seem to quench but it dehydrates , a purportedly healthy abstainer are forever telling me .
to quench is more than to refresh although our lying , lascivious eye tell u the two are the same .
doe any drink look more quenching than one of those italian or french patent aperitif , swirling in a refractive pink or russet oiliness , clothing the melting ice : a campari , dubonnet , lillet or ( too rare in this country ) a picon ?
are they quenching ?
no , refreshing .
in strasburg , they have been known to lace beer with picon , thereby ruining both drink ( a queen victoria reputedly did with claret and malt whisky ) , but still the result is not especially quenching .
perhaps a shimmering dry martini cocktail ( minimum dilution , stirred , not shaken ; and served straight up ) look even more refreshing , though it effect is that of a silver mallet .
in the right place ( possibly new york , but san francisco is better ) , the martini is the ultimate â€˜ thank god it 's friday â€™ drink .
the requirement for such pleasure is a matter of time and place , and it doe not have to be friday , berlin or strasburg , new york or san francisco .
not feeling thirsty , but fancying a drink , ha a world of moment .
some frenchman start the day with a coffee and calvados .
the people of munich call their version of weissbier â€˜ breakfast beer â€™ and have it with a pair of coddled veal sausage .
new orleans favour a mid-morning milk punch , or it own variation on the gin fizz .
if the moment is before a lunch on the plane , it really ought to be a bloody mary .
if it is at an elegant dinner table , there is a choice between a fino sherry ( or a manzanilla ) or champagne .
none of these requires anything even approaching a thirst .
do we really â€˜ wash down â€™ a good meal with claret ?
port is immediately suggested by stilton ; cognac by coffee .
i do not fully appreciate the experience of an american late-night movie without a bourbon , or a book at bedtime without a single malt .
these are not moment of thirst â€¦ but nor are they cause of drunkenness ( unless one try to pack all such experience into one day ) .
we do not drink because we are thirsty .
nor , if we are in civilised cast of mind , because we wish to become drunk .
a friend of mine say that her body tell her , without being asked , the season of the year .
at the end of summer , her body stop wanting gin and tonic and suddenly desire whisky .
if it were only a matter of alcohol , would a body care ?
people will nevertheless say that the desire is for nothing more than alcohol .
so why not a pill or an injection ?
because there would be no colour , no ritual , no time and place , no glorious variety .
worst of all , there would be no aroma , taste , texture or finish .
these are sensuous pleasure at their most enjoyable when we ritualise them , but how do we feel when we desire them ?
we , who have so many word in our language , have none for this .
but then , do the french , spanish or italian have a word for it ?
perhaps they do ; maybe i have not being paying attention ; too busy with the eau-de-vie , ani or grappa .
do the greek have a word for it ?
could we borrow , beg or invent one ?
answer in a bottle , please .
if you are feeling â€” for lack of a better word -thirsty , let's meet again next month .
same time , same place ?
it will be my turn to get them in , i think .
in the garden : planning to beat a leafy retreat at home : in the first of a series for novice gardener , anna pavord stress the value of careful forethought
by anna pavord
plant should come a long way down the list of priority for the novice gardener .
there are many thing to think about before even the first tentative daffodil bulb is sunk into the ground .
the first and most important question to mull over a you gaze at your patch is : what is this garden for ?
if you start writing down some of the response to that question , you will find you have along the way acquired a ragbag of element that will have an important bearing on the design .
if there are small child in the equation , there will need to be a flat , soft area where they can fall off swing and climbing frame .
if you like hanging out washing rather than drying it in the bowel of an expensive machine , you will need to tuck away a line and make a dry path to get to and from it .
some novice gardener may not want to do much actual gardening at all , but conversely , do not want to look out every day over a scene of threadbare grass and rotting fence .
their ideal garden will look good and demand little attention .
if you are a dedicated sun-worshipper , you need to mark out the part of the garden where the sun lingers longest .
it may not be the part nearest the house , though this is where sitting out area always tend to be made .
you may , even a a novice , care so passionately about plant that all you want is the maximum space to grow them in .
raised bed built down an unpromising alley could provide extra gardening space .
privacy is an important consideration .
a first time garden is usually small and certainly the typical one i am imagining for this series is no more than 40ft x 20ft and surrounded on all side by other house .
privacy is a ticklish business where close neighbour are concerned .
to throw up an impenetrable berlin wall between you and them could be tactless .
trellis is a useful compromise and you can always pretend it wa forced on you by the speed of growth of your climbing rose .
aesthete among the novice will by now be thoroughly irritable and complaining that garden should have a much to do with the soul a with the necessity or not of a washing line or a barbecue pit .
so they should , and when the list of necessity is securely written down , you can ask the second question : how do i want this garden to feel ?
this question produce a varied a response a the first .
some urban gardener , a short of space inside a they are out , want their garden to be extension of their home , with a great deal of hard paving , good furniture and lighting .
the garden for them should feel like an extra sitting room , gnat-ridden perhaps , damp occasionally , but an important overflow of living space .
closet countrysiders may want to pretend their patch is actually a corner of some sussex meadow and picture it swagged with honeysuckle and waving with ox-eye daisy and scabious .
this is a difficult trick to bring off with suburban train hurtling by on one side and lorry grinding through their gear on the other .
a leafy retreat is an excellent goal for a small town garden , but it is best made acknowledging the urban setting , using un-wild plant such a bamboo , acanthus , fig , vine , alchemilla , euphorbia , wisteria .
while you are working through this process , take note of the feature in the garden that you would like to keep .
on a new estate , there may be nothing .
in an old garden , there may be a fruit tree , or a good bulwark of evergreen laurel or box that , tidied up , could add a dash of venerability to the new garden that will emerge around it .
if you do not know what a tree or shrub is , exercise clemency and give it a year 's grace to demonstrate it worth before you turn it over to the axeman .
the mood of the garden will dictate it eventual style .
it may be clipped classical , random cottage , modern high-tech , or some intensely personal vision of byzantium , that no one ha ever imagined before , but which exists in your head and want to get out .
do not let anybody , particularly gardening writer , tread on your dream .
all this thinking and doodling will have done nothing physically to change the view outside the window , but it is a vital part of the gardening process .
before you can make , you have to know what it is you are making .
property : house that are going , going , gone : fed up with trying to sell , more people are auctioning their home instead .
andrew bibby report
by andrew bibby
in the small devon town of south molton , julie widlake is waiting with anticipation for friday 20 october .
she and her boyfriend have had their future planned for some time : they want to start farming in a nearby village , where they also intend to build a bungalow .
first , they have to sell their present house , a two-bedroom cottage in the centre of the town .
a is the case almost everywhere else , the market in devon is depressed and selling ha not been easy .
so instead of waiting indefinitely for a buyer , they have decided to try selling their house by auction .
it come under the hammer at 2.15pm on the 20th , at the barnstaple motel .
â€˜ we accept that we might not get very much for it , but on the other hand we could get more , â€™ say julie widlake .
â€˜ we 've been waiting to move for a long time , and we need the money to build the new house . â€™
their south molton cottage ( guide price â£44,000-â£46,000 ) is one of more than 20 lot to be put up for sale at the barnstaple auction by bristol and west property service , and one of more than a hundred which the building society subsidiary will be auctioning in eight west country venue over a three-day period .
bristol and west claim these auction will help to bring some action to an otherwise slow market , and say it wa overwhelmed by the response from people wanting to sell in this way .
auction certainly speed up the house-buying process .
once the hammer ha fallen , the successful bidder for a house must exchange contract immediately and pay a deposit .
completion normally take place four to eight week later and there is no opportunity for second thought .
a the bristol and west explains , if a bidder doe not sign the contract , the auctioneer can sign on their behalf .
â€˜ from the vendor 's point of view , the house is actually sold once a successful bid is made , â€™ say graham harrison , director of bristol and west property service .
â€˜ many people have suffered from sale falling through and from house-buying chain , and some of our vendor feel that now 's a good a time a any to sell . â€™
from the purchaser ' point of view , auction can also be attractive â€” if slightly nerve-racking a you wait for bidding to start for the house of your dream .
the problem is that normally potential purchaser have to incur the expense of undertaking survey and arranging mortgage finance , without any idea whether they will be able to afford the final selling price .
not surprisingly , several estate agent report difficulty at the moment in attracting enough serious bidder .
â€˜ with an auction , you 've got to be certain that at least two buyer are there who can commit themselves , and the difficulty is that buyer who can proceed are few and far between , â€™ said david phillip , a director of yorkshire estate agent dacre son and hartley .
bristol and west hope that by arranging collective auction it will be able to overcome this problem .
it is also guaranteeing mortgage at 1 per cent below the normal interest rate , for endowment or pension-linked policy , on several of the house up for sale , an interesting and innovative service for would-be bidder ( mortgage survey will already have been undertaken for these house ) .
the building society will be staffing a mortgage desk at each auction , and say buyer could arrange finance there and then , subject of course to proof of income and status .
obviously , however , bristol and west hope to hear from interested people in advance â€” if only so it can gauge the likely demand for each property before the auction start .
â€˜ we 're trying to smooth the path for purchaser , and streamline the system , â€™ say graham harrison , pointing out that live telephone bidding will also be available for those unable to attend .
holiday home and commercial property have been included among the lot to be auctioned .
although collective property auction take place regularly in london ( often primarily for investor rather than house-hunters ) , in country area the more typical auction will be just for one house , and a likely a not will take place in a nearby pub or village hall .
in fact , country auction are often the nearest many village get to live theatre .
â€˜ the local come for the entertainment , and to see who 's buying into their village , â€™ observed one auction-goer .
pub landlord are usually only too happy to provide free accommodation .
â€˜ it 's very exciting and get the adrenalin going , â€™ say david phillip , whose firm usually undertakes auction in the otley area .
only certain type of property normally come to auction , however .
traditionally , country cottage and house which have unusual or unique feature or are in need of a lot of renovation may be auctioned , if only because valuation is likely to be difficult .
sometimes , too , house have to be sold by auction to satisfy the legal requirement of trustee or executor ( auction provide adequate proof that the property ha been sold at the going market value , and protect trustee from future claim of negligence ) .
le happily , but for the same reason , repossessed house are often auctioned .
anyone considering selling by auction need to be aware that the cost involved will be higher .
advertising cost and any expense incurred in hiring premise are normally passed on by the estate agent to the vendor , whether or not the auction result in a sale .
in addition , agent may charge slightly more commission than usual for a successful sale .
in bristol and west 's forthcoming auction , a set fee of â£375 plus vat will be charged to everyone entering property to sell , with it usual agent 's commission ( about 2 per cent plus vat ) charged on top when sale are made .
â€˜ we accept that we could lose the money we 've paid to go to auction , but that 's a risk we 're prepared to take , â€™ say julie widlake .
the secret of a successful outcome depends partly on setting the right reserve price , a task the auctioneer undertakes in conjunction with the seller .
normally , the reserve is set only day or hour before the auction , when the agent will already have a good indication of how much interest ha been shown .
too high a reserve price can mean no sale , and disappointment all round .
â€˜ you need to understand what your client â€™ expectation are beforehand , â€™ say graham harrison of bristol and west .
agent and their client may also have the dilemma of deciding whether to accept any offer made prior to the auction .
inevitably they do not always get it right .

the church built on wool : angela lambert enjoys the rich at the heart of east anglia
by angela lambert
until six or seven year ago , outsider associated east anglia with fen , flatness and the vanished edwardian nanny of frinton .
it old church , green field and long beach were cherished by the inhabitant of essex , suffolk , norfolk and cambridgeshire , but not yet on the tourist agenda .
today , i wa told , villager living around the great wool church of lavenham and long melford have to bar their door and ignore the knocking of tourist , who take them for antique shop and offer to buy their furniture .

east anglia on a sunny weekend is a place of matchless beauty .
it village look too good to be true .
lavenham and long melford , for example , have no telegraph pole , to preserve the timeless air that pervades them .

most of east anglia 's prosperity today is due to city commuter , the second-homes market and tourism .
the ideal hotel from which to explore is dedham hall , set among tall tree in the vale of dedham , looking directly across towards dedham church on one side and flatford mill on the other : both painted by constable .

these are so popular that you will need to book at least a couple of month in advance .
the other great joy of dedham hall is the food .
the slingos have their own vegetable garden , chicken , duck , sheep and cow .
everything is home-grown , home-made and fresh : it taste like forgotten food .
if dedham hall is fully booked â€” and it may well be â€” try the best bed-and-breakfast we have ever found , just a few mile away at the old vicarage , higham , near colchester .
it is a rambling tudor house run by john and margaret parker , whose breakfast room and drawing room look out over a wonderful walled garden , beyond which field of grazing cow stretch down to the river .
another hotel that would make a good starting point for a weekend â€” or longer â€” in east anglia is the black lion at long melford .
it changed hand recently and is now run by stephen and janet errington .
after dinner there , when the moon wa full and low and deep orange , we walked up the road from the hotel to long melford church .
it vast wall of flint and glass and gothic tracery were brilliantly floodlit and the churchyard cat , sleek and black a tar , greeted u querulously and led u right round the church and through the gravestone at the back , glimmering and pale in the moonlight .
we were quite alone and the great church , the size of a cathedral , wa unearthly in it floodlit , moonlit beauty .
long melford is one of the wool church that commemorate the wealth of east anglia in the fifteenth and sixteenth century .
another is at lavenham , 10 minute ' drive up the road .
just inside the front porch , a handwritten book is prefaced with the word â€˜ this book ha been compiled that something , more than their mere name , may be known of the lavenham men who gave their life for their country in the great war . â€™
this is followed by 50 or so name , each with a description .
the much-thumbed book is deeply moving , and set the tone for a walk round this glorious building , which ha seen the christening , marriage and burial of the folk of the village for more than 600 year .
east anglia is exceptionally rich in church and cathedral .
there are several splendid house near long melford that would make for a gentle afternoon 's potter through the room and possession of the rich of a few century ago .
the loveliest , to my mind , is melford hall , whose charming , octagonal jacobean gatehouse is reached along an avenue of clipped yew tree and , with it high window flooding the octagon with light , is my fantasy of the ideal study .
lavenham is often called the finest medieval village in england .
it is an image of the world we have lost , it gently sloping street converging upon the market place .
here stand the early sixteenth-century timbered guildhall , now a museum to 700 year of the cloth industry .
next to it is the little hall , dating from the fourteenth century , with it floorboard gleaming with the patina of age , low-beamed ceiling and a tiny , unruly , charming walled garden .
precisely next door to this is a restaurant called the great house .
here , in the cool , pretty setting of the dining room that wa once stephen spender 's , you will be served an exquisite three-course lunch for â£9.50 , â£6 for child .
