homicide
this chapter deal with the approach of the criminal law to behaviour which cause or risk causing death .
murder , manslaughter , and several other offence are discussed , and one recurrent issue here is whether english law responds proportionately to the different degree of culpability manifested in case where death is caused .
7.1 death and finality
for practical purpose , the culpable causing of another person 's death may fairly be regarded a the most serious offence in the criminal calendar .
there is an argument that treason is a more serious offence , since it strike at the very foundation of the state and it social organization , but treason is so rare that it is surely permissible to treat homicide a the most serious form of crime .
the reason for this is not difficult to find : the harm caused by many other crime is remediable to a degree , whereas the harm caused by homicide is absolutely irremediable .
even in crime of violence which leave some permanent physical disfigurement or psychological effect , the victim retains his or her life and , therefore , the possibility of further pleasure and achievement , whereas death is final .
this finality make it proper to regard death a the most serious harm that may be inflicted on another , and to regard a person who chooses to inflict that harm a the most culpable of offender , in the absence of some excuse or justification .
although many death arise from natural cause , and many others from illness and disease , each year see a large number of death caused by â€˜ accident â€™ , and also a number caused by act or omission which amount to some form of homicide in english law .
for 1987 the statistic show that there were some 20,000 accidental death , of which some 7,000 occurred in the home , 6,000 at work , and 5,000 on the road .
by comparison , the number of death recorded a criminal homicide wa 600 , which includes all the murder and manslaughter .
this figure is relatively small when compared with many american city , but that is no cause for satisfaction .
there are still awkward question to be confronted .
for example , are we satisfied that the 600 death recorded a homicide are in fact more culpable than all , or even most , of the death recorded a accident ?
in other word , doe english criminal law pick out the most heinous form of killing a murder and manslaughter , or are the boundary frozen by tradition ?
another question is whether the criminal law ought not to be wider in it application to activity which carry some risk of causing death than in other sphere .
thus , even if it would be excessive to sweep large number of the death now recorded a â€˜ accident â€™ into the law of homicide , there may be sufficient justification for creating or enforcing offence designed to ensure safe condition of work , safe good , safe building , and so on .
it wa argued in chapter 2 that the criminal law ought to spread it net wider where the potential harm is greater .
it will be seen that english law doe this up to a point , and in the process seems to accept social-defence argument a reason for departing from several of the principle set out in chapter 3 .
7.2 requirement of criminal homicide
english law distinguishes between the offence of murder and manslaughter , a we shall see , but the two crime do have certain common element .
it must be proved that the defendant 's act or omission caused the death of the victim within a year and a day .
( the requirement of causation in the criminal law are discussed elsewhere . )
the â€˜ year and a day â€™ rule is a legacy of time when medical science wa so rudimentary that , if there wa a substantial lapse of time between injury and death , it wa unsafe to pronounce on whether the defendant 's conduct or some other event caused the death .
nowadays the problem is quite the reverse : medical science is generally able to determine whether d's conduct caused the death , and innovation such a life-support machine mean that life can be prolonged for month and year in some instance .
in one recent case the victim of a stabbing died two and a half year after the incident , having been kept on a life-support machine for almost the entire time .
in principle , it seems unjust that a homicide conviction should not be possible in such a case , if all the other element can be established and only the â€˜ year and a day â€™ rule stand in the way .
but the rule wa confirmed by the criminal law revision committee in 1980 , on the ground that a defendant should not be left in peril of a homicide conviction indefinitely .
surely prosecutorial discretion should be enough to prevent unfair conviction year after the event .
the rule itself is out of accord with modern medical condition and should be abolished .
defining murder : the inclusionary question
( a ) the procedural context
if causing death is to be regarded a the most serious harm that can be inflicted , it would seem to follow that the most blameworthy form of homicide should result in the highest sentence imposed by the court .
indeed , many system of criminal law impose a mandatory sentence for murder ( or whatever the highest form of homicide is called in that system ) .
in some jurisdiction this is a mandatory sentence of death .
in others , such a those in the united kingdom , it is the mandatory sentence of life imprisonment .
why should this penalty be mandatory , and not at the discretion of the court a in other offence ?
one argument in favour of the mandatory sentence of life imprisonment is that it amount to a symbolic indication of the unique heinousness of murder .
it place the offender under the state 's control , a it were , for the remainder of his or her life .
this is often linked with a supposed denunciatory effect â€” the idea that the mandatory life sentence denounces murder a emphatically a possible â€” and with a supposed general deterrent effect , in declaring that there is no mitigation of sentence available for this crime .
it might also be argued that the mandatory life sentence make a substantial contribution to public safety .
none of these argument is notably strong , let alone conclusive .
the mandatory penalty doe indeed serve to mark out murder from other crime , but whether the definition of murder is sufficiently refined to capture the worst killing , and only the worst killing , remains to be discussed below .
whether the life sentence is regarded a a sufficient denunciation in society depends on the public 's perception of what life imprisonment mean : if it is widely believed that it result in an average of nine year ' imprisonment , the effect will be somewhat blunted .
the same applies to the general deterrent argument : it effectiveness depends on whether the penalty for murder affect the calculation of potential killer at all , and , if it doe , whether life imprisonment is seen a significantly more or le severe than the alternative of a long , fixed-term sentence .
a for public protection , this depends on executive decision with regard to release ; it fails to take into account whether it is necessary for public protection to keep â€˜ lifer â€™ in for so long .
this brings the discussion to a crucial point : what doe the sentence of life imprisonment mean in practice , and what would be the alternative ?
what it mean is that the time of release from prison is determined by the home secretary , on the advice of the parole board and the lord chief justice , rather than by the judge at the trial .
in procedural term , therefore , the life sentence involves a transfer of function : normally it is the judge who determines the sentence ( or at least it upper limit , since earlier release on parole may be possible ) , whereas a life sentence entrusts that function to the executive , who must first ascertain the opinion of the lord chief justice and the trial judge .
the home office is not bound by those opinion , and appears to depart from them frequently .
in 1983 the home secretary imposed restriction on the release of person serving life for murder of police- and prison-officers , terrorist killing , murder during robbery , and the sadistic or sexual murder of young child , fixing a minimum of twenty year ' imprisonment for these offender .
whatever the justification for introducing this policy , it serf to underline the fact that sentencing in murder case is enacted without each case being considered individually , after hearing representation from the offender 's counsel , and without a sentence announced in court and subject to appeal .
at present , the executive take decision to release some murderer after only a few year and to keep others in prison for extremely long period .
should those decision not be taken by the court , in the same way a other sentencing decision ?
the answer sometimes given is that murderer should be treated differently because they are particularly dangerous : anyone who chooses to kill once can choose to kill again .
but this argument will seem le persuasive when we have discussed case of manslaughter by reason of diminished responsibility : where a murder is reduced to manslaughter , the judge ha a wide sentencing discretion and may , according to the fact of the case , select a determinate prison sentence , a hospital order , or life imprisonment .
those who kill and are convicted of manslaughter by reason of diminished responsibility are no le dangerous than those convicted of murder , and yet the judge ha sentencing discretion in one case and not in the other .
consideration of this kind have led the house of lord select committee on murder and life imprisonment to recommend the abolition of the mandatory sentence for murder .
in it place would be a sentencing discretion , which judge would use , in many case , to mark the relative heinousness of the murder by a determinate prison sentence .
in case where life imprisonment wa thought to be the appropriate sentence , the trial judge would still specify â€” in open court â€” the number of year to be spent in prison , taking the gravity of the offence into account , and release would be determined by a judicial tribunal .
such a reform would bring improvement in natural justice without loss of public protection .
( b ) degree of homicide
english criminal law ha two degree of homicide , murder and manslaughter .
many american jurisdiction have three degree of murder â€” first-degree murder , requiring premeditation , and second- and third-degree murder , covering lesser shade of culpability .
in england there ha been a proposal that we should do away with all these distinction , leaving a single offence of criminal homicide .
that proposal , if implemented , would shift almost all the decision-making in homicide case from the trial to the sentencing stage : the only issue of any consequence in most case would be the sentence to be imposed , and all the distinction now drawn at the stage of criminal liability would be reflected in the sentence alone .
the jury would have no role in most homicide case , since a plea of guilty would be the norm .
contrast this with the position in jurisdiction where there are three or more degree of homicide : plea of guilty to first- and second-degree murder would be le frequent , since there would be the possibility of argument in court ( if no bargain wa struck beforehand between prosecution and defence ) , and the defence might succeed in persuading the jury to reduce the degree of the killing .
moreover , the borderline between the various degree would raise question of law , which would provide much opportunity for legal argument and for appeal .
the result would be that killing were classified and labelled in a more refined way , but at the cost of lengthy trial and mounting legal-aid bill .
that cost is not difficult to justify where capital punishment is the penalty for first-degree murder .
where there is no capital punishment , however , the question of how many degree of homicide it is desirable to have call for careful weighing of the principle of fair labelling ( see chapter 3.3 ( l ) ) against the cost and time argument mobilised by the policy of efficient administration ( see chapter 3.3 ( m ) ) .
the structure of the english law of homicide is rather strange .
although there are two offence , murder and manslaughter , the latter includes two distinct variety : â€˜ voluntary â€™ manslaughter ( killing which would be murder but for the existence of defined extenuating circumstance ) ; and â€˜ involuntary â€™ manslaughter ( killing for which there is no need to prove any awareness of the risk of death being caused , but for which there is thought to be sufficient fault to justify criminal liability ) .
the argument therefore tend to focus on three borderline question : what is the minimum fault required for conviction of murder ?
what condition are needed to reduce murder to manslaughter ?
and what is the minimum fault required for a conviction of manslaughter .
( c ) requirement for murder
in english criminal law there are now two alternative fault requirement for murder : an intent to kill , or an intent to cause grievous bodily harm .
what do these requirement mean ?
do they extend the definition of murder too far , or are they too narrow .
intent to kill may be regarded a the most obvious and indisputable form of fault element for murder , but to some extent that depends on the meaning of â€˜ intent â€™ .
this ha been the subject of a number of house of lord decision , and yet the definition is still not clear and settled .
probably the most accurate statement would be that a person intends to kill if it is his or her purpose to kill by the act or omission charged , or if he or she foresees that death is practically certain to follow from that act or omission .
in this way , both purpose and foresight of practical certainty are regarded a part of the definition of intent , although there are other statement suggesting that foresight of practical or â€˜ virtual â€™ certainty is merely evidence from which intent may be inferred .
how would this test be applied ?
in many case the word â€˜ intent â€™ is used without elaboration , but there are some for which a full explanation of the meaning of intention is necessary .
a fairly typical set of fact is provided by nedrick ( 1986 ) , where d had a grudge against a woman and had threatened to â€˜ burn her out â€™ .
one night he went to her house , poured paraffin through the letter-box and on to the front door , and set it alight .
one of the woman 's child died in the ensuing fire .
when asked why he did it , d replied : â€˜ just to wake her up and frighten her . â€™
a defence of this kind , a claim that the purpose wa only to frighten and not to cause harm , requires the full definition to be put to the jury .
the question is : granted that d's purpose wa to frighten , did he nonetheless realize that it wa practically certain that his act would cause death or grievous bodily harm to someone ?
the jury should answer this by drawing inference from the evidence in the case and from the surrounding circumstance .
what about the alternative element in the definition , an intent to cause grievous bodily harm ?
this ha considerable practical importance , since this is all that the prosecution ha to prove in order to obtain a verdict of guilty of murder .
it must be shown that the defendant intended ( which , again , includes both purpose and awareness of practical certainty ) to cause really serious injury to someone .
the house of lord confirmed this rule in cunningham ( 1981 ) : d struck his victim on the head a number of time with a chair , causing injury from which the victim died a week later .
d maintained throughout that he had not intended to kill , but there wa evidence from which the jury could infer â€” and did infer â€” that he intended to cause grievous bodily harm .
the house of lord upheld d 's conviction for murder : an intent to cause really serious injury is sufficient for murder , without any proof that the defendant intended , or even contemplated , the possibility that death would result .
doe the â€˜ grievous bodily harm â€™ rule extend the definition of murder too far ?
if the point of distinguishing murder from manslaughter is to mark out the most heinous group of killing for the extra stigma of a murder conviction , it can be argued that the â€˜ grievous bodily harm â€™ rule draw the line too low .
in term of principle , the rule requires justification because it departs from the principle of correspondence ( see chapter 5.2(a) ) , namely that the fault element in a crime should relate to the consequence prohibited by that crime .
by allowing an intent to cause grievous bodily harm to suffice for a murder conviction , the law is violating a general principle , turning the most serious of it offence into a constructive crime .
why should it be necessary to â€˜ construct â€™ a murder conviction out of this lesser intent ?
there are argument in favour of this : death is final , murder is the gravest crime , and there is no significant moral difference between someone who chooses to cause really serious injury and someone who set out to kill .
no one can predict whether a serious injury will result in death â€” that may depend on the victim 's physique , on the speed of an ambulance , on the distance from the hospital , and on a range of other medical and individual matter .
if one person chooses to cause serious injury to another , it should be presumed that he or she realizes that there is always a risk of death , and such case show a sufficiently wanton disregard for life a to warrant the label â€˜ murder â€™ if death result .
the counter-arguments , which would uphold the principle of correspondence , are that breach of that principle is unnecessary when the amplitude of the crime of manslaughter lie beneath murder , and also that the definition of grievous bodily harm includes a number of injury which are most unlikely to put the victim 's life at risk .
in the leading case of cunningham lord edmund-davies ( dissenting ) gave the example of breaking someone 's arm : that is a really serious injury , but one which is unlikely to endanger the victim 's life .
so in practice the â€˜ grievous bodily harm â€™ rule go further than the argument of it protagonist would support .
would it be right , then , to confine the fault element in murder to an intent to kill ?
that would have the merit of simplicity , but would it strike the right note socially ?
there are powerful argument in favour of saying that there are other killing where an intent to kill can not be established , and yet where the moral or social culpability is equal to that in most intentional killing .
of course , one can not be adamant about whether they merit the label â€˜ murder â€™ , because that is a question of drawing the line between murder and manslaughter , which is not susceptible of any precise resolution .
it is hard to argue conclusively that the category of murder should be smaller or larger ( unless the death penalty or a mandatory life sentence follows ) ; it is a question of social judgment .
let u briefly consider some of the possibility .
the fault element for many serious offence is intent or recklessness : why should this not suffice for murder ?
the question is whether all killing in which the defendant is aware of the risk of death are sufficiently serious to warrant the term â€˜ murder â€™ .
one answer sometimes given is that they are not , because a driver who overtakes on a bend , knowingly taking the risk that there is a car travelling in the opposite direction , should not be labelled a murderer if a collision and death happen to ensue .
this example assumes that a sympathy for motorist with overwhelm any tendency to logical analysis .
one might ask whether motorist are ever justified in knowingly taking risk with other people 's life .
yet if the example is modified a little , so that the overtaking is on a country road at night and the risk is known to be slight , it becomes questionable whether the causing of death in these circumstance should be labelled in the same way a intentional killing .
this is not to suggest that motorist should be treated differently .
the point is rather that , even though knowingly taking risk with other people 's life is usually unjustifiable , taking a slight risk is le serious than intentionally causing death .
in discussing the boundary of murder , we are concerned with classification , not exculpation .
to classify all reckless killing a murder might be too broad , but the point remains that some reckless killing may be thought no le heinous than intentional killing .
can a satisfactory line be drawn here ?
one approach would be to draw the line by reference to the degree of probability .
murder is committed in those situation where d caused death by an act or omission which he knew had death a the probable or highly probable result .
a version of this test of foresight of high probability is used in several other european country ; it wa introduced into english law by the decision in hyam v.
dpp ( 1975 ) , but abandoned in moloney ( 1985 ) on ground of uncertainty .
a second approach is to frame the law in such a way a to make it clear that the court should make a moral judgment on the gravity of the defendant 's conduct .
section 210.2 of the model penal code includes within murder those reckless killing which manifest â€˜ extreme indifference to the value of human life â€™ .
scot law treat a murder killing with â€˜ wicked recklessness â€™ , a phrase which directs the court 's attention to the circumstance of the killing .
both the model penal code test and the scot test may be reduced to circularity , however , for when one asks how extreme or how wicked the recklessness should be , the only possible answer is : â€˜ wicked or extreme enough to justify the stigma of a murder conviction â€™ .
admittedly , the model penal code doe contain a list of circumstance which may amount to extreme indifference , which assist the court and increase the predictability of verdict in a way that scot law doe not , but the essence of both approach is that there is no precise way of describing those non-intentional killing which are a heinous a intentional killing .
their protagonist argue that the law of murder is so important socially that derogation from the principle of maximum certainty should be allowed in favour of more accurate labelling by the court ; opponent argue that the principle of maximum certainty is needed here specifically to reduce the risk of verdict based on discriminatory or irrelevant factor , such a distaste for the defendant 's background , allegiance , or other activity .
a third , more precise formulation derives from the recommendation of the criminal law revision committee in 1980 , namely , that a killing should be classified a murder in those situation where there is an intention to cause serious injury coupled with awareness of the risk of death .
neither an intention to cause serious injury nor recklessness a to death should be sufficient on it own , but together they restrict one another , producing a test which both satisfies the criterion of certainty and mark out some heinous but non-intended killing .
a fourth approach , adopted by english law until 1957 and still in force in many american jurisdiction , is some form of felony-murder rule : anyone who kill during the course of a felony ( or , more restrictively , a serious crime of violence ) or whilst resisting arrest should be convicted of murder .
thus stated , there is no reference to the defendant 's intention or awareness of the risk : the fact that d ha chosen to commit rape , robbery , or another serious offence , and ha caused death thereby , is held to constitute sufficient moral ground for placing the killing in the highest category .
plainly , this is a form of constructive criminal liability : the murder conviction is constructed out of the ingredient of a lesser offence .
presumably the justification is that d ha already crossed a high moral/social threshold in choosing to commit such a serious offence , and should therefore be held liable for whatever consequence ensue , however unforeseeable they may be .
the objection would be reduced if awareness of the risk of death wa also required : in other word , if the test were the commission of a serious offence of violence plus recklessness a to death .
the effect of that test would be to pick out those reckless killing which occurred when d had already manifested substantial moral and legal culpability , and to classify them a murder .
four alternative approach have been described , and many others could be listed .
the point is that the traditional concept of intention and recklessness do not , of themselves , appear to be sufficiently well focused to mark out those killing which are the most heinous .
the law must resort to some kind of moral and social evaluation of conduct if it is to identify and separate out the most heinous killing .
many people might think that a person who cause death whilst using an unlawfully held firearm or explosive ought to be convicted of murder because there is , generally speaking , no excuse for using such dangerous equipment .
some of the people thus covered would be armed robber , others would be terrorist .
the armed robber might say that he had no intention of using the firearm , that he carried it with him simply to frighten the victim , and that it went off accidentally : if the jury belief that , should he be convicted of murder ?

travel : copse and robber christopher somerville see how woodland ha usurped the ancient cattle thief of the cheviot hill
by christopher somerville
when a charlton chief sat down to dinner in time gone by and found a spur on the table where his meal should have been , he knew the cupboard wa bare once more .
his wife wa telling him to saddle up and steal a fresh batch of cattle from his neighbour 's herd .
the family of north tynedale in westernmost northumberland â€” charltons and robsons , milburns and dodds â€” were never slow to lay hand on each other 's property in the lawless old day .
here , where the scottish border ran across the cheviot hill , far from civilisation , â€˜ reiving â€™ , or cattle-thieving , wa a way of life .
remoteness is still the keynote of this region , but a charlton reiving party would be hard put to it these day to work up a good gallop along the upper reach of the north tyne river .
since 1926 the western fringe of the cheviot have been planted with 250 square mile of close-packed conifer , while in their depth sprawl the seven mile of the kielder water reservoir , europe 's largest man-made lake .
i found it hard to picture what kind of landscape those statistic might have shaped , until i opened the ordnance survey map to find half of it coloured green with a great blue stain in the centre .
kielder water is beautiful , and the kielder tree formidably impressive in their domination of the hill .
but by the time i had turned off the road from bellingham at kielder village and driven up the bumpy forest drive to east kielder farm , i wa longing for the sight of something other than water and tree .
relief wa at hand .
the farm stand on a spur of moorland on the northern edge of the forest , and within 10 minute i had turned my back on the corduroy battalion of tree and wa striding under a still , cloudy sky over tussock of rush and coarse grass , with my face to the long , bare shoulder of open hillside that flank the winding shallow of the east kielder burn .
the footpath that run with the burn is not one of those carefully waymarked and leafleted by the forestry commission .
there are few attraction for the kielder tourist here .
the burn thread a wild and inhospitable crevice of the hill , where the wind blow cold and the sense of isolation grows with each lonely mile .
walking here , you leave the 20th century behind on the outskirt of the forest and enter the reconstructed emptiness in which the reivers and cross-border raider of the past could operate unhindered .
i squelched across tiny burn running in black channel of peat , and stood looking down from the hillside on to the grey roof of scaup farm .
the hollow barking of a dog came from one of the stone shed , but there wa no sign of life around the building .
scaup must be just about the loneliest farm in england , tucked down in the shelter of the hill with kielder forest 's dark mass blocking it southward view and the narrowing valley of the burn filling half the sky to the north .
the east kielder burn divide here into scaup burn and white kielder burn .
i dropped down the hill to ford white kielder upstream of the ruined farmstead of kielder head , where a line of stunted alder , shaggy with lichen , leaned over their leafless reflection in the peat-brown water .
spring come late to this windm_bitten and rain-soaked valley .
but there were new-born lamb in the sheep pen of scaup , and a cheery â€˜ hello now â€™ from the farmer and his wife tending them .
those were the last human voice i heard all day .
no-one life up here in the cleft of the white kielder burn .
the rough land is altogether too harsh and unyielding , giving sustenance only to sheep , and to the conifer high on the flank of east kielder moor and grey mare knowe , immense sweep of hillside to right and left of the valley .
the footpath on the left bank of the burn wa little better than a sheep track , but there were clue to it former importance a a droving road over the hill to byrness in redesdale .
large boulder stood out beside the path a grey blob in the featureless sheet of pale grass , and flat stone had been placed a bridge across the burn , their back hollowed by the tread of foot through the century .
following these now disregarded sign of past activity along the old path , i traversed the hillside and came down to a crumbling stone sheepfold with the roofless remains of a shepherd 's hut built into one wall .
the tiny single room wa jammed with the mossy timber of disused sheep pen , but the little iron fire grate that once warmed the hardy men who worked here wa still set in the foot of the hut 's tottering gable end , and the massive stone lintel had not yet fallen from it place above the doorway .
a dipper flew up from the burn a i emerged from the hut , his white breast flashing a he darted downstream .
somewhere ahead in the crag of kielderhead moor a hawk wa whistling , a piercing sound in the lonely valley , overlain for a few heart-stopping moment by the snarl of a jet .
that jarring manifestation of furious technology seemed a remote and ridiculous a a dream , a silence settled down on kielderhead and the hawk 's whistle came down from the moor .
the valley of the white kielder burn steepened again a i trudged north , it curve tightening .
now the path ran through heather high above the burn , past circular sheepfold long disused and over the stony bed of side stream where the grass hung smooth and inviting , concealing ankle-breaking drop .
a mile above the shepherd 's hut the hillside ceased their upward rolling and curved together to form a dead-end valley of broad-breasted slope five mile from the nearest tarmac road , hidden deep in the fold of the moor .
the old drover 's road turned aside here and climbed under the rock face of white crag to continue it lonely course over girdle fell and down into redesdale .
i could have gone with it , giving myself four blissful mile of upland walking followed by a many miserable hour of fruitless searching for return transport to east kielder farm .
instead i lay back in the heather and savoured the trickle of the burn and the crisp smell of peat-laden ground .

in the last of his series on the leader , frank johnson report

a non-punitive paradigm of probation practice : some sobering thought l.r. singer
lawrence singer is an assistant chief probation officer with merseyside probation service .
beforehand he worked a a probation officer for berkshire probation service and later a a research officer for somerset probation service where he undertook the research reported here .
the paradigm which is described originates from doctoral research into generic probation practice and ha been presented in a book adult probation and juvenile supervision : beyond the care-control dilemma ( 1989 , gower ) .
summary
the government white paper crime , justice and protecting the public proposes a radical change for probation practice .
henceforth probation work will no longer be an alternative to punishment but an alternative form of punishment .
this article challenge the conceptual and practical appropriateness of applying the notion of punishment to the probation context .
probation officer , it is argued , are concerned with establishing rule for probationer and not inflicting pain on them .
an alternative non-punitive paradigm of probation practice is described .
the paradigm is based on the joint supervisory aim of support and surveillance which are implemented through the method of counselling , assisting , directing , and monitoring .
to illustrate the paradigm , reference is made to an alcohol education course developed for offender in trouble through drink .
the efficacy of the alcohol project and the non-punitive paradigm is demonstrated by the result reported from a three year evaluation study covering 152 offender .
the article concludes with an affirmation of the disciplined but non-punitive character of current probation work and argues that , to successfully protect the public and reintegrate offender into the community , the probation officer 's employment of established social work knowledge , skill , and value is a strength not a weakness .
the aim of this article is to challenge the assertion contained in the white paper crime , justice and protecting the public ( home office , 1990a ) that probation practice can be conceived of a a form of punishment in the community .
issue will be taken with the appropriateness of applying the concept of punishment to the probation context through reference to a demanding and successful probation project concerned with offender in trouble through drink .
in describing the project , a non-punitive model for understanding probation practice will be developed .
this non-punitive paradigm will provide a framework for contrasting the efficacy of current practice with future plan inappropriately to transform probation work into â€˜ a new approach to punishment in the community â€™ ( p. 18 , para. 4.3 ) .
the punitive approach anticipated for the probation service in the white paper is underpinned by a definition of punishment that resides â€˜ in the restriction on liberty and in the enforcement of the order â€™ .
the white paper go on to emphasize : â€˜ it is the loss of liberty involved in carrying out the term of the order rather than the activity carried out during the order which is the punishment â€™ ( p. 18 , para. 4.4 ) .
notwithstanding the popular appeal and political capital to be derived from such a humane-sounding statement , this definition is nevertheless at variance with the traditional understanding of the meaning of punishment .
discourse on punishment and dictionary definition of the term clearly specify the central feature of punishment a being the infliction of pain rather than the loss of liberty per se ( hudson , 1987 ) .
the conceptual and practical implication of this discrepancy will be examined in the third and final section of this article .
beforehand , however , consideration will be given first to the probation project and second to it evaluation .
in these section punishment will be understood to mean the infliction of pain and the review of the project will seek to demonstrate that the probation practice described is not only more demanding but also more effective than this traditional understanding of punishment .
at a time when the home secretary is inviting the probation service to â€˜ move centre stage in the criminal justice system â€™ ( nacro , 1990 , p. 17 ) it may seem misguided and unnecessarily pedantic to take issue with the white paper 's notion of punishment .
punishment is just a word after all : nothing more than convenient political rhetoric meriting only lip-service from probation manager and practitioner poised at the â€˜ window of opportunity â€™ .
however , this fails to acknowledge a fundamental axiom of social work practice ; namely that language not only reflects but also shape social reality .
a davy ( 1981 ) ha pointed out , speech is the basic tool of the social worker .
what we say influence what we think and in turn , what we do .
if punishment is assimilated into the probation glossary it will inevitably influence the sort of practice the service undertakes .
in the section to follow the stark question is therefore put â€˜ is the probation service in the business of inflicting pain ? â€™ and the answer which unfolds may be summarized â€˜ no , because it neither aspires nor wish to ! â€™
the project : more demanding than punishment
government concern about the problem of drink-related offending is a old a the probation service .
indeed , a bochel ( 1976 ) ha shown , the establishment of a probation service in england and wale wa inextricably linked with work undertaken with offender in trouble through drink .
almost a century later the new approach to punishment in the community outlined by the white paper once again present alcohol misuse a a key problem requiring community-based intervention .
â€˜ alcohol â€™ , the white paper state , â€˜ is implicated in a wide variety of crime , drunken driving , offence of public disorder and domestic violence â€™ ( p. 21 , para. 4. 14 ) .
in describing it vision of effective supervision the white paper go on to note :
offender take part in alcohol education programme so that they can learn to drink sensibly .
they learn about the relative strength of different drink , the amount which is safe to consume , and the pitfall to avoid ( e.g. competitive drinking and buying â€˜ round â€™ ) ( p. 37 , para. 7.11 ) .
a a summary of alcohol education programme , this is something of a simplification .
however it doe provide a useful introduction to the origin of the alcohol education course reported here .
furthermore , it highlight the suitability of referring to this type of project for testing out the appropriateness of applying the notion of punishment to probation practice .
in 1985 the management of somerset probation service wa faced with the fact that nearly one in three of the offender supervised in their area were either known to be or suspected of misusing alcohol ( singer , 1985 ) .
aware of practitioner ' frustration in not being able to work effectively with these problematic client , probation management , in partnership with the voluntary sector , determined to develop an alcohol education course ( aec ) .
by 1986 , when social inquiry report monitoring information covering 1,257 offender indicated that the problem of drink-related offending wa virtually a great for pre- a for post-sentence client , that is , alcohol wa cited a a pre-offence problem in twenty-nine per cent of case , a specialist project worker had been appointed .
towards the end of 1986 an aec wa launched and ha been running ever since .
the aim of the course is to educate offender about alcohol to facilitate a lasting change in their subsequent use of this drug .
this aim is underpinned by three interrelated objective and it is not without significance how they complement and serve to implement the white paper 's own trinity of â€˜ aim of supervision â€™ , that is , protection of the public , prevention of reoffending and successful reintegration of the offender in the community ( p. 35 , para. 7.3 ) .
these were :
1 .
to increase knowledge about alcohol so that future alcohol use can be more informed and therefore more prudent .
2 .
to instil or reinforce responsible drinking attitude and behaviour .
3 .
to encourage the maintenance of , or , where appropriate , a reduction to a personally and socially safe level of alcohol unit intake .
to meet these objective the aec ha a structured programme involving group and individual contact , that is , six group session sandwiched between one-to-one interview held immediately before and after the group .
the initial one-to-one meeting , held with one of the two group leader , serf to clarify what participant can expect from the course and what is expected of them .
a standard contract which cover condition of attendance and an agreement to complete various assignment between group session is signed by each participant .
the final interview entail a personalized review of the course , a well a the formulation of a follow-up plan to be undertaken with the offender 's supervising officer .
the course employ a variety of material and technique including the use of checklist , magazine and newspaper clipping , quiz , video , brainstorm exercise , discussion , drink diary , and role play ( menary , 1986 ) .
the aec is primarily intended for young adult subject to a probation order with a special condition of attendance ( schedule 11 4a(ii) criminal justice act 1982 ) and reflects the fact that most somerset offender in trouble through drink commit serious offence and/or posse a substantial number of previous conviction .
in short , they are seriously at risk of committing further offence and becoming trapped in the revolving door of custody .
the development of the somerset aec both accord with and constitutes a practical example of the home office 's recent guidance to sentencers :
in recent year the probation service ha devoted increasing resource to supervising the more serious and persistent offender .
probation order which are combined with constructive , disciplined and effective programme of supervision are increasingly being used for offender who stand to receive custodial sentence ( 1990b , p. 35 ) .
from the description of the aec already given the demanding and disciplined nature of the course can be appreciated .
however , the course is not punitive ; it is not concerned with the infliction of pain .
instead , it is concerned with the establishment of rule .
this is plainly evident from the section of the contract signed by each participant where what is expected of them is spelt out :
you will be expected to keep a drink diary throughout the course , and make attempt at reducing the amount you drink if it is greater than accepted healthy drinking level .
you must be willing to be helped and help others â€” your experience will be useful to other group member .
you must attend 8 session ( these will consist of : an introductory session , six group session and a final assessment interview ) .
you must not drink before the session .
you must be on time â€” lateness will count a an absence .
unlike the prison inmate , aec participant can not sit back and passively do their time .
probation practice is not , however , solely concerned with the establishment of rule .
a ha been demonstrated elsewhere with regard to the generic probation setting ( singer , 1989 ) , the supervision of offender involves two overarching aim : namely support and surveillance .
support refers to anything which is said or done to help the offender solve or mitigate their personal or social problem : surveillance to anything which is said or done to induce the offender to conform to socially acceptable standard of behaviour .
these aim are implemented through the practice of four distinguishable but related method .
in term of support , there is counselling and assisting : in term of surveillance , there is directing and monitoring .
each of these four method are employed during the eight session .
although summarized a an alcohol education course , the session are not just concerned with giving information but also with enabling participant to make practical use of it .
in the first group session the counselling method is registered in the course tutor 's note ( menary , 1986 ) by the explanation : â€˜ this session aim to get participant to consider the subject of alcohol in way they had not thought of in the past â€™ ( p. 3 ) .
during this session the importance of monitoring is also emphasized :
drink exercise introduce the notion of measurement , concentration of alcohol content ; information which will be necessary to carry out the recording of alcohol consumption throughout the duration of the course ( p. 3 ) .
in the fifth group session entitled â€˜ focus on the individual â€™ , the counselling and the monitoring combine with the directing and assisting method .
at the start of the session the tutor 's note make clear that directing extends beyond enforcing the condition of attendance :
this session allows for an in depth examination of the drinking diary over the last five week â€” identifying the risky circumstance or situation â€¦ .
the aim at the end of the session is to encourage participant to make rule for their future drinking which will lead to a lessening of identified problem ( p. 4 ) .
similarly , the assisting extends beyond simply providing handout of useful information :
it is important that participant are provided with the skill to reduce their drinking level or handle more effectively â€˜ pub centred â€™ problem .
the use of role play , incorporating previously discussed difficulty ( i.e. homework assignment session 4 ) in â€˜ real â€™ or set up situation provides an opportunity for testing these skill .
probably the best illustration of the assisting method in action during the course come in the fifth session when somerset practitioner take the logic of working with the problem in the community to it ultimate conclusion .
in the second half of the session , course leader sponsor participant to an evening at the pub for the purpose of practising drinking skill and purchasing non-alcoholic drink .
the counselling , assisting , directing , and monitoring on the aec give substance to the aim of support and surveillance and provide the basis for establishing a model of probation practice .
punishment ha no place in this model .
instead the aec can best be understood a an example of a non-punitive paradigm of probation practice .
twenty year ago the advisory council on the penal system observed that notion of punitiveness do not accord with â€˜ the british concept of probation a an alternative to , rather than a suspension of , punishment â€™ ( quoted in bochel , 1976 , p. 238 ) .
twenty year later the aec demonstrates that this remains no le true now than it wa then .
but doe the aec work ?
bringing fossil back to life
in this chapter some of the way in which palaeontologist determine the way fossil animal lived are described , reanimating the dead fragment to build up a living creature .
since popular idea of life in the past are often founded on vividly coloured reconstruction of â€˜ the world in the jurassic period â€™ and the like , it is important to remember that these imaginative scene are all inference from bone , and similar fragment .
in fact there is nothing fixed about such interpretation , because the way the fossil are understood may change over the year , but it is usually some time before new discovery percolate into the popular presentation .
even the most solid-looking dinosaur may have changed their habit in the last few year !
many different line of evidence may be used to flesh out the bare bone of the fossil .
the first of these is the evidence of the rock from which the remains were recovered .
the sediment themselves reveal much about the environment of deposition , a wa shown in chapter 3 .
it is important to establish whether the fossil animal actually lived in the environment which furnished it sedimentary cover , or whether it remains were swept in from some other place .
fortunately it is usually easy to spot such intruder .
the type of sediment and the associated fossil will show whether the environment wa marine , freshwater or terrestrial , providing the basic information into which the ecology of the animal ha to be accommodated .
the sediment themselves may have preserved some of the track left by the animal ( p. 20 ) to give direct evidence of it past activity .
from the track alone it is possible to be certain of the bipedal stance of certain dinosaur , and to measure their stride .
the character of the rock , and their setting in the past geography at the time when they accumulated , diagnosis the climatic setting in which the extinct fauna lived , and climate imposes certain restraint on possible mode of life .
savannah animal differ from those of tropical rain forest , and these again from inhabitant of the tundra at high latitude .
looking at the fossil animal itself , the first necessity is to reconstruct it a accurately a possible from it fragmentary remains .
sometimes this is a very complex business , particularly for vertebrate with number of small bone .
to proceed from the reconstruction to an assessment of probable life habit two different but complementary approach are used .
one method attempt to compare the structure of teeth or limb or some other feature of the extinct animal with living analogue .
these do not have to be biologically related organism .
the basic argument is that structure that are closely similar were probably adapted to a similar function .
sometimes these similarity are quite obvious : the ferocious teeth of a predatory dinosaur are a sure indication of hunting habit , with hardly a glance at the fang of living mammalian carnivore .
but the structure and functioning of grinding or chewing teeth in other mammal , in which opposing teeth co-operate in action , and which can be matched in extinct , unrelated mammal , is a much more subtle matter , involving detailed study on the operation of living dental system to help elucidate the functioning of fossil one .
the technique of â€˜ hunt the analogue â€™ is a favourite one practised by palaeontologist , but it is certainly not a foolproof one , because there are many fossil animal that defy comparison with living organism , and some analogue do not stand up to detailed scrutiny .
the second method try to analyse the structure of the fossil almost a if it were a piece of engineering .
if the fossil is constructed in a certain way , then there are only a limited number of â€˜ job â€™ that the structure could perform .
the idea here is to decide which of the possibility is the most likely one .
this assumes that nature only manufacture efficient design .
for the most part this is a reasonable assumption to make .
most animal today do seem to have body that accord well with the function they have to carry out to survive : flyer are aerodynamically efficient , active swimmer have suitable streamlining , herbivorous mammal have teeth appropriate for grinding plant food , and so on .
ideally one could construct a model of the fossil to test out these various function in experiment , but the number of example where the analysis ha been pursued this far are limited .
of course it can never be known whether the right answer ha been reached ( unless somebody dredge up a â€˜ living fossil â€™ from the depth of the ocean ) â€” there are only varying degree of probability .
but the best answer are probably obtained where the functional design of the fossil point to the same life habit a a living analogue , and where both are consistent with the geological circumstance in which the fossil remains occur .
swimming trilobite
there are thousand of different kind of trilobite .
all of them were marine and all of them are extinct .
since many kind of trilobite co-existed at any one time they were probably occupying different ecological niche , behaving in different way , corresponding with the wide variation in shape that they show .
we can use the method described above to elucidate some of these occupation , and get a glimpse , albeit an imperfect one , of the trilobite a it lived .
i will take an example where all the method can be used , and where all point in the same direction , so that the answer is probably correct .
most trilobite were broadly oval-shaped being rather longer than wide , not greatly convex , and with eye that occupy perhaps a quarter of the length of the head .
there are a few trilobite specie , however , with enormous , globular eye ( see below ) .
by examining the way these animal were put together it is possible to suggest a likely mode of life .
giant-eyed ordovician trilobite , seen from the top and side .
twice it natural size .
enlargement show a section through the lens to show the direction of the field of view .
we can take the eye first .
the trilobite eye wa of a compound type , and each lens wa made up of a calcite crystal .
from the optical property of this mineral we know that the lens wa able to interpret light coming from a direction more or le at right-angles to the lens surface .
so we can deduce what the field of vision of our globular-eyed trilobite wa , by looking at the direction in which all the lens face .
it turn out that our animal wa able to see in almost every direction â€” upwards , downwards , sideways and forward , and even backwards , because the eye bulged out beyond the line of the rest of the body .
most other trilobite have a predominantly lateral field of view .
if the animal lived on the sea bottom , it seems unlikely that it would have eye lens specialized for looking downwards , and we begin to suspect that the animal habitually dwelt above the sea floor .
other feature of it shape are consistent with this .
at theâ€“ edge of the eye are a pair of long spine , and these point downwards at a steep angle , at very awkward attitude for resting comfortably on the sea floor .
most â€˜ normal â€™ trilobite have a more or le horizontal rim around the forward margin , which may have rested on the sediment surface .
the thorax of the large-eyed trilobite is remarkably long compared with the average trilobite ( compare fig. 33 ) .
the convex , middle part of the thorax contained the musculature that operated the appendage ( which , a usual , are not preserved ) , and the relatively large volume of this region show that the musculature wa powerful .
the construction of this giant-eyed trilobite suggests that it lived an actively swimming life well above the bottom , and possibly in the surface water of the sea .
of course because there are no living trilobite we can not find direct confirmation of this hypothesis , but we can look for other arthropod that have the same modification of the eye , for example , in the present day ocean .
the analogy is found in such ocean-going amphipod ( a kind of â€˜ shrimp â€™ ) a cystosoma , which also ha enormously expanded eye , looking like headlamp , compared with it bottom-dwelling relative ( see above ) .
in fact many of the arthropod that inhabit the water column have large , globular eye of this kind .
49 fossil bony fish , lates gracilis , eocene .
this fish is preserved in a fine-grained limestone which preserved most of the skeletal detail of the fish ( but not it colour ) .
this is a stout-bodied fish , something like a perch in general proportion , with long , low fin on upper and lower rear of the body ( fin ray finely divide near their end ) .
head to tail the fish measure 17 cm .
50 vertebra of the jurassic pliosaur , liopleurodon specie , kimmeridge , southern england .
this is only one vertebra of an animal much too bulky to appear in a book of this size !
the original porous bone ha been consolidated to become much heavier than it would have been in life .
diameter of the vertebra is about 10 cm .

this specie is preserved in a soft shale , but one which allows for the skeletal anatomy to be preserved in it entirety .
the outline of the soft part are also clearly displayed , so that the animal ha been preserved almost in frozen motion .
frog are one of the more numerous of amphibian fossil .
specimen 12 cm long .
52 fossil calcareous alga , belonging to the genus coelosphaeridium , ordovician , ringsaker , norway .

there is a central sphere visible on one of the specimen .
related form are spread over much of the world in ordovician and silurian time .
individual globe have a diameter of 1â½â€“2 cm .

it classification in relation to other plant is still unsolved .

these flower have five oval petal , finely veined .

giant-eyed living crustacean ( cystosoma )
if it is correct that our trilobite lived above the sea bottom , actively swimming in the water , there are certain prediction we can make about it geological occurrence which can be tested by looking in the rock .
most bottom-dwelling trilobite preferred to live at a particular water depth , or on a particular type of sea bottom ( mud , sand or lime ) .
no such restriction should apply to our large-eyed specie ; it should be found with all other different kind of trilobite assemblage without preference .
this ha been proved in several place : in some arctic locality it is found with trilobite that lived at great depth in the mud of the ordovician ocean , while in canada the same specie occurs mixed with the inhabitant of the shallow-water sea , where limestone were accumulating .
by the same token the free-swimming trilobite may be expected to have a very wide geographical distribution , for ocean would be no barrier to it .
and again this prof to be the case : our example is found in arctic canada , in the high arctic island of spitsbergen , thousand of mile away in what is now the desert country of nevada and utah in the united state , in western ireland , in russia , and in northwestern australia .
so in this case the different source of evidence all point in the same direction , whether derived from a detailed consideration of the way the trilobite is constructed , or by analogy with living animal with similar adaptation , or from the evidence of the rock or the distribution of the fossil .
these giant-eyed trilobite of the ordovician were active swimmer in the surface water , a near certainly a we can ever know .
there are all kind of different question we can ask about the life habit of these trilobite , which are not subject to the same kind of careful scrutiny .
what did they eat , for example ?
because we do not have the leg preserved , it is not possible to see how the mouth appendage functioned in feeding , and neither are the stomach content preserved .
so here there are only more or le likely speculation .
active swimmer in the surface water of the present ocean are likely to feed directly on the plankton , and the trilobite may have had a method of harvesting large quantity of minute food .
or they may have been hunter after larger prey , in which case when appendage are eventually discovered they may prove to have adaptation for grasping and manipulating larger food .
puzzle remain , even though we can be certain of the rudiment of the story .
controversy among the giant dinosaur
the giant sauropod dinosaur like brachiosaurus were the largest land animal the world ha seen they weighed more than 80 tonne and present quite different problem in the interpretation of their life habit from the diminutive trilobite .
such spectacular animal have obviously attracted much attention , and one might suppose that the problem of how they lived would have been satisfactorily solved long ago .
most of the popular book showing the mesozoic giant in their natural setting portray them wallowing about in swamp flanked by deep vegetation , their body largely under water .
surely an animal of this bulk , it wa argued , must be partly supported by water , and their relatively inconsequential teeth must have been adapted for chewing on the kind of soft , luxuriant vegetation that flourish in and around swamp .
because their nasal opening were on the top of their head they could even continue to breathe if it became necessary ( see p. 115 ) to totally submerge .
recently the life habit of the giant have been looked at in a way that disproves most of these traditional notion .
consider the structure of their leg .
the sauropod have relatively long , pillar-like leg , resembling those of the elephant , the largest living land animal , and may have been well adapted for supporting the huge bulk of the animal .
the foot of the sauropod are small ( relatively speaking ! ) , with short , stubby toe , yet animal that walk on soft mud tend to have spreading foot to distribute their weight more evenly .
it is difficult to see how the compact foot of the sauropod could avoid becoming stuck fast in the soft , muddy bottom of a lake .
if the dinosaur did , after all , live on dry land , then the long neck could have usefully functioned to allow the animal to browse the high foliage of tree ( see p. 116 ) .
it ha been suggested that an animal of the size of brachiosaurus could not have breathed under water because of the pressure on the lung .
their fossil remains seem to occur with other animal and plant , which are generally accepted a terrestrial .
there is even some evidence from the track they have left behind that diplodocus and it ally moved about in herd .
they may have been the gigantic reptilian analogue of the elephant , and it may be no coincidence that the elephant also ha it nasal opening on top of the skull , with the nostril in this case sited at the end of the trunk it ha been suggested that some sauropod may have had a proboscis of some sort .
the balance of evidence seems to be swinging away from the original idea of swamp-dwelling giant dinosaur to fully terrestrial habit .
but other aspect of the dinosaur living habit are still more strongly debated .
in the last few year a powerful controversy ha arisen over whether the dinosaur a a whole were cold-blooded , like all living reptile ( and there is no doubt that dinosaur were reptile ) , or warm-blooded , resembling mammal in this respect .
cold-blooded animal have to â€˜ warm up â€™ before they can be fully active ; that is why lizard and snake bask in the sun in temperate climate .
for this reason they can not cope with climate having greatly extended winter .
warm-blooded animal have the same body temperature at all time , and can be more continuously active , but they use far more energy â€” and hence need more food â€” than cold-blooded animal of the same size .
the posture of many dinosaur , and particularly the carnivorous theropod dinosaur , wa fully erect with the leg beneath the body , and unlike the sprawling leg of living reptile ( see p. 116 ) .
the long back leg of such hunter look highly suitable for running , and a they did so the long tail may have been held erect a a kind of counter-balance ( see p. 116 ) .
for any kind of prolonged activity , warm-bloodedness would have been a distinct advantage .
under the microscope even the bone structure of these dinosaur look more like that of living mammal than cold-blooded reptile .
on the other hand it can be argued that the sauropod like brachiosaurus were so large , and with a relatively small surface area through which to cool compared with their enormous volume , that their cooling rate could have been slow enough to allow them to sustain more continuous activity than smaller , living reptile .
it ha also been argued that the small mouth of the giant simply could not have downed enough food to support a warm-blooded metabolism , particularly plant food that need a lot of processing before it becomes available a energy .
the armoured or plated dinosaur include some specie that seem too heavily burdened with protective armour to have been very active , but this doe not necessarily mean that they had to be cold-blooded .
protagonist of the warm-blooded theory will show some of the horned dinosaur charging over the cretaceous plain like furious reptilian rhinoceros !
one idea about the life habit of the dinosaur giant , published in joc and colette at the natural history museum in 1935
the warm-blooded supporter tend to ally themselves with the supporter of a theory about the relationship of the dinosaur .
this is that the dinosaur , or a close dinosaur relative , included the ancestor of the living bird .
some of the smaller , and most certainly highly active dinosaur were about the size of a chicken , and there is more than a passing similarity between a running ostrich and the kind of reconstruction that show fleet-footed , running dinosaur .
the point is that bird themselves are warm-blooded , like mammal , and if bird and dinosaur are a closely related a now seems likely , then it obviously increase the likelihood that the dinosaur themselves may have been warm-blooded .
the pro and con of this theory are the subject of much contentious debate by expert , but it would probably be correct to say that the greater number of specialist believe that at least the more bipedal of the dinosaur , including small and large carnivore , were warm-blooded , active animal .
a lot of the argument among the authority on these animal is about the equivalence or otherwise of certain bone in dinosaur and bird skeleton ( and particularly the jurassic bird archaeopteryx ) ; this make for rather dry reading for the layman .
but the end-product is the accurate reconstruction of the jurassic and cretaceous terrestrial world , over which the dinosaur were indisputably dominant .
action and inaction in the city
susanne macgregor and ben pimlott
the essay in this book do not amount to a programme : but they are intended to provide a springboard for one .
the aim is to analyse a problem which economic growth alone ha failed to cure â€” and to consider possible new form of public action .
the biggest single difficulty in drawing attention to urban poverty is that it is not new , but simply â€” in some of it most worrying manifestation â€” getting worse .
like the poor themselves , the inner city ha long been with u .
ever since the industrial revolution created a mass urban society , the condition of the poorest city dweller have given rise to anxiety among the better off .
the inner city ha been variously regarded a a cause for moral outrage , a threat to public order , or a a stain on the nation 's conscience .
meanwhile bad housing , bad sanitation , lack of education , and other perennial ill associated with overcrowding and poverty have been routinely denounced by political leader and social reformer .
a central objective of the post-war welfare state wa , indeed , to alleviate the problem of the urban poor , over whom beveridge 's five giant ( want , disease , ignorance , squalor , and idleness ) had for so long held sway .
in the early 1940s and 1950s , the belief wa widely held that a combination of government intervention â€” in the form of town planning , housing and health programme , and the provision of social security â€” and permanent male full employment , together with an increase in real wage , would reduce suffering due to poverty to manageable proportion .
the 1964 labour government wa elected on a tide of impatience at the slackening pace of social reform , but also of optimism that little more than economic growth , fuelled by technological change , wa needed to remove the main cause of urban deprivation ( macgregor , 1981 ) .
disillusion swiftly followed .
the work of peter townsend and others showed that , far from presiding over the elimination of poverty , the wilson government actually failed to prevent some of it feature from worsening ( townsend and bosanquet , 1972 ) .
evidence began to mount that much of the social welfare paternalism of the post-war period had created almost a many problem a it solved .
neither the butler education act of 1944 , nor the introduction of comprehensive school , prevented a high proportion of child from under-achieving .
post-war reconstruction and housing programme seemed to do nothing to prevent , and seemed even to encourage , a rise in crime and mental illness .
meanwhile , in many part of the country , a problem a old a city themselves â€” tension between cultural group , between earlier immigrant and newer one , between black , brown , and white , and ( increasingly ) between large section of urban youth and the police â€” came to be identified a the key inner-city issue .
rising unemployment turned a lingering and growing malaise into a crisis .
a in the united state , so in britain it wa an outbreak of violence that dramatically brought the plight of the inner city to public notice .
in the early 1980s , at a time when manufacturing industry wa collapsing and the outlook for the unskilled looked particularly bleak , frustration boiled over in a series of riot in london , bristol , birmingham , liverpool , and elsewhere .
all were spontaneous outburst : none , contrary to some claim , were politically inspired or orchestrated ( except in northern ireland where urban problem existed on a grander , more devastating scale , and where there wa a unique heritage ) .
many had an â€˜ ethnic â€™ dimension , in the sense that many of the rioter were black .
unlike , however , the 1958 notting hill riot , few of those involved were immigrant â€” the vast majority were british born .
and the disturbance could not strictly be called racial : the most prominent feature wa that of violent clash between young people and the police .
the problem , however , were not confined to violent upheaval of this kind .
paul harrison 's inside the inner city : life under the cutting edge focused on hackney , a london borough ranked among the worst off on any scale of urban deprivation ( harrison , 1983 ) .
harrison describes ( p. 369 ) the way in which the inner urban powder-keg lead to a vicious circle , with many victim , but few individual villain :
the police force face the virtually impossible task of keeping the lid on the explosive mixture of ingredient that the dynamic of british society have assembled in the inner city .
this mixture , heated by recession and high unemployment , inevitably generates a high level of crime .
this necessitates , in turn , a far more numerous and ubiquitous police presence than in other kind of area , far more frequent unpleasant contact with the public a potential suspect , and far greater opportunity for police misjudgment or abuse .
the inevitable sufferer , of course , are the poor , of whom those who actually commit the offence that concern the police are a tiny minority and whom , indeed , it should be the purpose of the police to protect .
not only are the most disadvantaged on the receiving end of most crime , argues harrison , they also have to put up with the heaviest police presence .
they are more likely to be stopped and searched , and to be the victim of mistaken arrest or conviction .
at the same time , the resident of the poorest area have to suffer , in a way that is not true of those who live elsewhere , a growing fear of attack on themselves or their property .
improved police method might help , writes harrison , to contain the problem .
but , without other change , the result is likely to be a â€˜ society of barricaded self-defence , and a steady erosion of civil liberty â€™ .
and he concludes , â€˜ of all the warning that the inner city ha to offer for the direction british society is taking , this is perhaps the most sinister â€™ .
harrison 's vision of a nightmarish future for inner city , locked in a cold or hot war between have-not and the force of public order , wa echoed by robert chesshyre ( 1987 , p. 95 ) , who concluded that by the late 1980s :
the basic social contract , whereby citizen enjoy certain right â€” including health care , decent education and housing and a job â€” in return for which they observe the rule of law , wa breaking down .
a a consequence an underclass wa evolving â€” football hooligan , mugger , inner city rioter â€” somewhat more frightening than their dickensian forebear because they were mobile and all too visible .
recently , of course , there ha been an added dimension â€” part media-and-politician inflated , part real : drug .
the spiral in drug abuse and trafficking , with direct consequence for the aid epidemic , ha created a new kind of alarm .
the early 1980s witnessed a radical change in pattern of heroin taking , alongside the global increase .
smoking heroin ( â€˜ chasing the dragon â€™ ) wa one feature of the upsurge .
another wa the increased availability of , and demand for , cocaine .
sensational medium and police report in 1989 seemed to suggest an increased use of â€˜ crack â€™ among young people in part of south london , liverpool , and birmingham â€” with attendant fear that this particularly dangerous substance might become widespread , a it wa reported to be in part of the united state .
for the moment , however , the pattern of drug abuse in britain remains a varied a ever â€” with home-produced drug such a amphetamine a serious a problem a those that hit the headline .
it is a symptom of public anxiety about urban squalor â€” and , le creditably , of class and racial prejudice â€” that â€˜ drug â€™ and â€˜ inner city â€™ are automatically linked in the popular consciousness ( macgregor , 1989a ) .
nevertheless , drug-taking and more particularly the disruptive social consequence of addiction , are especially serious in area of city poverty ( burr , 1989 ) .
in short , if life wa always harder in the inner city than elsewhere , and if condition may temporarily have been alleviated by the impact of the welfare state and rising income , the last decade ha been widely represented a a period of deterioration .
moreover , few would dispute that this apparent decline is a reality : the first word of the prime minister herself , after the result of the 1987 election wa known , were an acknowledgement of the challenge posed to government by the â€˜ inner city â€™ .
what ha been lacking however â€” a well a the political will to do something â€” ha been any shared agreement on the cause .
can the problem of urban poverty be blamed on individual pathology ?
on feckless parent and grandparent ?
or on the social or physical environment â€” the replacement of companionably unhygienic slum by soulless tower block with broken lift ( coleman et al . ,
1985 ) ?
or would a simple injection of resource transform kafkaesque honeycomb into happy living-spaces , with a consequent decline in inner-city ill ?
â€˜ a duke can live comfortably in a castle , after all â€™ , the architect cedric price is quoted a saying .
â€˜ you and i would die of frostbite .
same building â€” just a matter of money and management â€™ ( parkin , 1989 ) .
the view that only â€˜ outstanding management â€™ could meet the problem wa held by the audit commission ( 1987 ) .
the picture painted by the commission 's report on inner london is a gruesome a any ever presented ( and is itself revealing of common prejudice in it choice of indicator of deprivation ) :
in some london borough , unemployment among young men exceeds 45% ; in some place , among young black , it exceeds 60% .
more than one child in three is born into a single parent family .
half the school leaver have no o level or their cse equivalent .
homelessness , housing condition , crime are worsening year by year , a the cycle of urban deprivation becomes more established ( p. 1 )
drawing on north american experience , the commission concluded that â€˜ poor management wa an important contributory factor to new york 's problem â€™ , and sought to apply the same lesson here ( p. 2 ) .
the government 's post-1987 initiative have , to some extent , reflected a diagnosis based on the need for better management â€” though on the principle that central authority can provide a firmer grip than local one â€” but combined with an anti-collectivist belief in the restorative power of capitalism .
leech and amin ( 1988 , p. 14 ) summarize this approach , not unfairly , in the following term :
the encouragement of private investment and the liberation of the market ; the containment of the force of social discontent ; the control of the power of the local authority ; and the encouragement of the movement of the wealthy into the inner city thus creating buffer area of tory support , accentuating inequality and injustice and ( hopefully though not necessarily ) reducing the potential for conflict among the urban poor .
the main thrust of the government 's policy is contained in the january 1988 white paper on regional policy and the enterprise economy , in the new employment training programme , and especially in the march 1988 action for city programme â€” all resting on wider reform to local government structure and finance , and to education and housing .
the most striking feature of this package , apart from it aim actively to involve employer and entrepreneur , is a sharp reduction in the role of local government .
despite the contemporary tone of much of action for city , the programme 's heritage can be traced to the early 1960s study which provided the basis for the 1965 milnerâ€“holland report on housing and the 1967 plowden report on education , a a result of which general improvement area and education priority area were established .
the 1970s â€˜ urban programme â€™ had a social , rather than economic , emphasis and wa administered by the home office .
it wa initiated partly in response to the furore caused by enoch powell 's 1969 â€˜ river of blood â€™ speech , much a disturbance at the 1976 notting hill carnival prompted further step on the part of the department of the environment â€” â€˜ a riot make a much bigger impact on government thinking â€™ , a donnison ( 1987 ) ha pointed out , â€˜ than any amount of earnest and accurate research â€™ .
the urban programme consisted of grant and initiative under the 1969 local government grant ( social need ) act , which wa intended a a flexible instrument to provide supplementary help to local authority in the field of housing , education , and health .
originally , the programme supplemented existing central and local government scheme designed to meet special need in urban area .
the scope wa extended in an â€˜ enhanced â€™ urban programme , which included economic and environmental a well a social project , and wa specifically directed at the inner urban area where the problem were most severe .
earlier , the change of emphasis from a â€˜ social â€™ to an â€˜ economic and environmental â€™ focus had been reflected in a switch of responsibility for the programme from the home office to the department of the environment .
the â€˜ enhanced programme resulted from the 1978 inner urban area act , based on a white paper , published the previous year , on policy for the inner city ( doe , 1977 ) , the first comprehensive policy statement on the subject to acknowledge it a a definable and cohesive problem .
the white paper drew on study conducted earlier in the decade in liverpool , birmingham , and london which pointed to a debilitating flight of capital and skilled labour from the critical area of deprivation .
