a key aspect of the 1977 white paper wa the stress it placed on the wider effect of poverty : that it wa not just those with acute need who suffered .
it argued that in talking about inner city it wa vital to think in term not just of individual , but of society ; that the multiple deprivation of the worst hit area affected all the resident ; that the consequence included a pervasive sense of neglect and decay , a decline in community spirit , a low standard of neighbourhood facility , and an increase in crime and vandalism ; and that , without government intervention , the future wa one of reduced job opportunity , deteriorating housing , and a decline in public service .
the document , and the legislation that derived from it , pointed to a positively discriminatory approach : with both central and local government supplementing existing programme in such a way a to strengthen local economy , a well a the physical and social environment .
such measure constituted the present government 's heritage , when it took office in 1979 .
none of them , of course , anticipated the scale of the problem which were caused by the slump and collapse of much of what remained of inner urban manufacturing industry in the early 1980s .
neither , at first , did the incoming administration foresee the impact of rising unemployment , which wa to be especially serious among the urban young .
true , the new secretary of state for the environment laid stress , from the outset , on the need for economic regeneration , and indicated his sympathy for the voluntary sector in the tackling of urban problem .
the previous government , however , had already pointed to the need to revitalize urban economy , and at first the shift wa more rhetorical than real .
michael heseltine ( 1987 , p. 138 ) ha since claimed that â€˜ we set a new objective : to make the inner city place where people would want to live and work and where the private investor would be willing to put his money â€™ .
partnership became his slogan â€” the partnership of public and private brain and money .
a new urban development grant wa devised , to pump-prime with public fund scheme which would then attract private investment .
heseltine 's vision wa of â€˜ partnership which recognise that there are some thing that only government can pay for , but also that the ingenuity and flexibility of the private sector is indispensable â€™ ( p. 156 ) .
he claim that serious work on inner-city policy began in 1979 : but it wa , undoubtedly , the riot of 1981 , coinciding with the biggest drop in employment and job vacancy since the war , which gave impetus to the new approach .
urban development corporation were introduced in 1981 in london docklands and merseyside , and the urban development grant came in late the following year .
conservative instinct were , of course , to minimize â€” within the â€˜ partnership â€” state involvement and expenditure , relative to that of private enterprise .
yet , the irony of the early 1980s â€” a a deteriorating , but perennial , urban problem rapidly became the most acute aspect of the crisis of mass unemployment â€” wa that a tory government , willy-nilly , found itself presiding over an increase in state intervention through a variety of agency .
the increased scale of operation also ushered in another fashion of the 1980s â€” new management structure .
thus in 1985 city action> team were introduced to each â€˜ inner city partnership â€™ area to co-ordinate the work of the three government department involved .
task force , meanwhile , were directed to tackle youth unemployment .
by 1986/7 the urban programme wa supporting 1,380 starter unit and 400 business start in enterprise workshop .
it wa providing 27,500 training place ( often in conjunction with the manpower service commission ) : some 2,500 building were being improved ; estate action aimed to tackle run down housing ; and a large number of new â€˜ partnership â€™ ( which some might see a subsidy , or discredited regional grant , in a new guise ) were in operation , in an effort to bring private investment to run-down urban area .
by 1988 it wa claimed that â£200 million of urban development grant and urban regeneration grant had successfully â€˜ levered â€™ more than â£800 million of private investment into the inner city ( although how much of the latter sum might have been invested in any case is difficult to say ) .
meanwhile , there were other incentive scheme â€” enterprise zone , for example , and simplified planning zone , which aimed to lift tax and planning â€˜ barrier â€™ in order to facilitate investment .
indeed , while with one hand trying to hold down expenditure by local authority , the government found itself by the late 1980s involved with the other hand in massive ear-marked spending on inner urban area .
the department of the environment alone by 1988 wa spending half a billion pound out of an action for city total , involving most home department , estimated at â£3 billion .
perhaps a labour government would have spent even more : it would certainly have spent it in different way .
it is a curious comment on thatcherism , however , that the administration most committed to a reduction in the role of the state , and in the need for an independent private sector , ha spent more on specific urban regeneration and employment scheme and incentive to private investment in urban area than any other in recent history .
one of the most bizarre aspect of government policy ha been the effective murder of the voluntary sector , and it appropriation , by subterfuge , into the tory version of the corporate state .
conservative have always lauded the â€˜ voluntarist â€™ principle in welfare , and encouraged it from platform .
faced , however , with the catastrophic increase in unemployment and the need to occupy large number of workless people without directly employing them ( which would have run counter to the economic doctrine which had led to the redundancy of many of them in the first place ) , the government poured money into any â€˜ voluntary â€™ agency willing to put in a bid for government-funded cheap labour .
one result wa the triumphant expansion of some charitable organization , which rejoiced both at their new-found importance and at the government 's munificence .
another wa the transformation of many such body , already heavily subsidized from public fund , into de facto agency of the state , which financed them and indirectly determined their policy .
nowhere wa the impact of this clumsy sleight of hand more evident than in the inner city , where voluntary organization had been most active , and where the government wa most anxious for them to mop up unemployment .
however , the later withdrawal of government support , with the scrapping of the community programme a unemployment receded , left many of these voluntary organization high and dry .
the national council for voluntary organization ( ncvo ) ha suggested from a recent survey that around 3,000 project had been affected and had lost â£285 million in funding ( macgregor , 1990 ) .
there wa , of course , a crucial element in the government 's anxiety : the high concentration of ethnic minority in the area of concern .
in 1981 nearly 40 per cent of ethnic minority in the uk lived in â€˜ partnership â€™ or â€˜ programme â€™ area .
there is a danger in reading back from this figure another stereotype , similar to the pavlovian â€˜ drug addiction/inner city â€™ assumed link .
some urban area have serious problem without a major ethnic dimension ( newcastle , teesside ) ; not all member of ethnic minority are poor or deprived .
nevertheless , the high proportion of inner urban population composed of people from ethnic minority , and the high incidence among these group of all the index of poverty and social and environmental hardship â€” combined with the specific problem of inter-racial rivalry , harassment , and tension â€” make race and ethnicity an aspect that is central to any analysis of the inner city and of any ameliorative approach .
making sense of the 1981 riot
reaction to the riot have concentrated on a number of key variable that are said to characterize the locality in which the riot have occurred .
lord scarman 's report on the 1981 disorder highlighted the importance of unemployment , urban deprivation , racial disadvantage , relation between young black and the police , the decline of civic consent , and political exclusion a the key issue .
other reaction have concentrated on the issue of the interplay between inner-city decay and racial disadvantage in contemporary britain .
yet others have argued that the unrest can be seen a a symptom of the breakdown of law and order in british society ( gaffney , 1987 ; hall , 1987 ; keith , 1987 ) .
the main issue that were prominent in debate during 1980â€“1 were â€˜ race â€™ and â€˜ law and order â€™ .
this wa by no mean an accident , since throughout the 1970s a powerful body of medium , political , and academic opinion had been constructed around the theme of how britain wa drifting into a â€˜ violent society â€™ , and how the basis of consent wa being shifted by the pressure of immigration and the growth of multi-racial inner city area .
a glimpse of the impact of the 1980â€“1 riot at this level can be achieved through two important debate in parliament .
the first took place in the midst of the july 1981 riot , and had a it theme : â€˜ civil disturbance â€™ .
the tone of the debate wa set by home secretary william whitelaw 's introductory statement in which he spoke of ( a ) the need to â€˜ remove the scourge of criminal violence from our street â€™ , and ( b ) the urgency of developing â€˜ policy designed to promote the mutual tolerance and understanding upon which the whole future of a free democratic society depends â€™ ( hansard , vol. 8 , 16 july 1981 : col. 1405 ) .
the â€˜ scourge of criminal violence â€™ wa , whitelaw argued , a danger to the whole framework of consent and legality on which the political institution of british society were based .
in reply roy hattersley supported the call for the immediate suppression of street violence , but warned that the root of such riot could not be dealt with until all people felt they had a stake in british society ( ibid. : cols. 1407â€“9 )
the second debate took place on 26 november 1981 , on the publication of the scarman report , and had a it theme : â€˜ law and order â€™ .
the importance of the riot in pushing the law and order issue , and specifically policing , on to the main political agenda wa emphasized by the liberal leader , david steel , who argued that â€˜ urgent action â€™ to prevent a drift into lawlessness wa necessary from both a moral and a political perspective ( hansard , vol. 13 , 26 november 1981 : cols. 1009â€“11 ) .
a subsequent debate on the same issue in march 1982 wa also full of reference to the experience of 1981 , the impact of street violence , crime , decaying urban condition , the breakdown of consent between the police and many local community , and the spectre of â€˜ more violence to come â€™ if change in both policing tactic and social policy were not swiftly introduced ( hansard , vol. 20 , 25 march 1982 : cols. 1107â€“81 ) .
the need to support the police wa accepted by both the labour and the conservative speaker in the parliamentary debate on the riot , and wa established a a benchmark for the official response to the riot long before the scarman report wa published in november 1981 .
any substantive disagreement centred around the issue of what role social deprivation and unemployment had in bringing young people to protest violently on the street .
intermingled with the discourse about race and law and order were constant reference to unemployment , particularly among the young , and various form of social disadvantage and poverty ( solomos , 1988 , pp. 186â€“911 ) .
throughout 1980 and 1981 debate about the riot in the medium , in parliament , and in various official report hinged around the interrelationship between racial , law-and-order , and social factor .
the importance of this debate can be explained , partly , by the political capital which the opposition could make from linking the social and economic malaise of the country at large with violent street disturbance .
conversely , throughout this period government minister strenuously denied that unemployment and social deprivation were significant cause of urban unrest .
although the scarman report is often taken to be the central text which argues for a link between â€˜ social condition â€™ and â€˜ disorder â€™ , the term of the debate were by no mean set by scarman .
during both april and july 1981 vigorous exchange took place in the press and in parliament about the role that deteriorating social condition and unemployment may have played in bringing about the riot .
during the 16 july parliamentary debate on â€˜ civil disturbance â€™ , roy hattersley 's formulation of this linkage provided a useful summary of the â€˜ social condition â€™ argument .
after some preliminary remark about the labour party 's support for the police , he went on to outline his opposition to the view of the riot a essentially anti-police outburst :
i repeat that l do not believe that the principal cause of last week 's riot wa the conduct of the police .
it wa the condition of deprivation and despair in the decaying area of our old city â€” area in which the brixton and toxteth riot took place , and area from which the skinhead invader of southall come .
( hansard , vol. 8 , 16 july 1981 : col. 1408 )
much of the subsequent controversy about this analysis centred on the question of youth unemployment .
hattersley had suggested that the riot were a â€˜ direct product â€™ of high level of youth unemployment , and a furious debate ensued in both parliament and the medium about this assertion .
the final symbolic cue used to make sense of the 1980â€“1 protest is more difficult to categorize , but it basic meaning can be captured by the term â€˜ political marginality â€™ .
while a number of discussion of the root of urban unrest in the united state have noted the salience of political marginality in determining participation in violent protest ( skolnick , 1969 ; fogelson , 1971 ; edelman , 1971 ; knopf , 1975 ) , this issue ha received relatively little attention in britain .
nevertheless , during the 1980â€“1 event and their aftermath the political context wa discussed from a number of perspective .
the scarman report , for example , located part of the explanation for the riot in the feeling of alienation and powerlessness which were experienced by young black living in depressed inner-city area .
a successful policy for tackling the root of urban disorder wa seen a one which sought to involve all the community in dealing with the problem of each area so that they could come to feel that they have a stake in it future ( scarman , 1981 , para. 6.42 ) .
where such argument did not fit in with the overarching theme of race , violence and disorder , and social deprivation they were either sidelined or pushed into the sub-clauses of official report .
the scarman report , for example , contained the following policy proposal : â€˜ i recommend that local community must be fully and effectively involved in planning , in the provision of local service , and in the management and financing of specific project , ( scarman , 1981 , para. 8.44 ) .
such a move towards greater political integration wa seen by lord scarman a essential if the gap between inner-city resident and the force of law and order wa to be bridged .
but the concern with overcoming political marginality remained on the sideline of the main public debate because it questioned the perception of the rioter a driven by irrational , uncivilized , and criminal instinct .
this did not , however , stop the question of political marginality and the need to reform existing policy from being raised at all , a can be seen subsequently by the attempt after 1981 to introduce both locally and nationally measure which were meant to address some of the grievance of the rioter and to ensure that further disturbance did not occur .
aspect of these measure are considered further on in the chapter .
perspective on the 1985 riot
despite widespread prediction of further unrest in the aftermath of 1981 , the scale and the location of the 1985 riot seem to have surprised even some of the most astute commentator .
handsworth , for example , wa presented even shortly before september 1985 a a â€˜ success story â€™ in term of police â€” community relation .
the outbreak of violence in this area wa therefore widely presented a an aberration .
similarly the spread of violence in london to area such a the broadwater farm estate in tottenham wa seen a a break from previous experience , which had centred on area such a brixton .
there are many continuity between 1980â€“1 and 1985 in relation to the common-sense image used in the press and television to cover the event .
but response in 1985 were different , at least in term of degree , and probably in relation to the extent to which the riot were seen a a â€˜ race â€™ phenomenon by a wider body of opinion .
the ambiguity and sub-clauses to be found in much of the press coverage during 1980â€“1 had at least acted a a countervailing tendency against the more extreme form of discourse which blamed the riot completely on black people .
during the 1985 riot and their immediate aftermath , the imagery of â€˜ race â€™ wa used by section of the press without the sense of ambiguity which could still be found in 1980â€“1 .
the â€˜ silence over race â€™ wa breached in 1980â€“1 , but in 1985 debate about racial issue wa taken a step further .
peregrine worsthorne , for example , used the ferocity of the confrontation in handsworth , brixton , and tottenham to argue that there wa a major question mark over the possibility of assimilating the â€˜ coloured population â€™ into mainstream â€˜ british value â€™ ( sunday telegraph , 29 september 1985 ) .
to be sure there wa still a strong response opposing enoch powell 's call for repatriation , from all shade of political opinion , but the racialization of public debate about the 1985 riot went much further than 1980â€“1 .
in this context it wa the externality of british afro-caribbeans and asian which wa highlighted rather the racist institution and process which worked against black at all level of society .
the usage of â€˜ race â€™ during the september-october 1985 period took on new meaning , which had little if anything to do with the impact of racism a such , since the emphasis wa on the cultural characteristic of the minority community themselves .
after handsworth part of the press response wa to blame the riot on rivalry between west indian and asian , and even after the argument were criticized by local resident ; and community leader , they were used to â€˜ explain â€™ ' what happened .
in addition , the question whether the culture and value of the black community , their family structure , and their political attitude â€˜ bred violence â€™ wa constantly raised ( van dijk , 1988 ) .
the actual fact ' of who wa arrested during the riot , whether black or white , were hardly debated since it wa assumed that they were mostly black and mostly unemployed and involved with crime ( keith , 1987 ) .
the imagery of the â€˜ black bomber , used in handsworth wa extended to the notion that there were group of alienated and criminalized young black who saw the riot a a chance to engage in an â€˜ orgy of looting â€™ .
the dear report on handsworth capture this image and link it to the social condition of young black :
the majority of rioter who took part in these unhappy event were young , black and afro-caribbean origin .
let there be no doubt , these young criminal are not in any way representative of the vast majority of the afro-caribbean community whose life ha contributed to the life and culture of the west midland over many year and whose hope and aspiration are at one with those of every other law-abiding citizen .
we share a common sorrow .
it is the duty of u all to ensure that an entire cultural group is not tainted by the action of a criminal minority .
( dear , 1985 , p. 69 )
this â€˜ black â€™ criminal minority wa constructed not only into the leading force behind the riot , but sometimes a the only force .
indeed , throughout september and october 1985 , and during the following month , the imagery of race continued to dominate debate about both the cause and the policy outcome of the riot .
a pointed out earlier , the â€˜ social cause â€™ argument wa another major plank of public debate about the 1980â€“1 riot , particularly in relation to the highly politicized issue of unemployment .
during 1985 this issue wa raised once again , though by then the extent of mass unemployment and urban de-industrialization and decay wa more stark than it had been in 198l .
image of â€˜ urban decay â€™ , â€˜ tinderbox city â€™ , and â€˜ ghetto street â€™ linked up with the image of â€˜ race inequality â€™ and â€˜ black ghetto â€™ to produce an analysis more complex but also more contradictory .
an interesting mixture of the various image wa provided by a story in the daily telegraph under the headline : â€˜ broadwater farm : like the divis flat with reggae â€™ ( 8 october 1985 ) .
the mirror described the estate a â€˜ living hell â€™ , and quoted one resident a saying that â€˜ you 'm_ve no idea how awful daily life is â€™ ( 8 october 1985 ) .
such image were reworkings of argument already used about toxteth in 1981 and brixton in 1981 , but they were used more widely than in 1980â€“1 .
even the daily mail , which deployed the clearest use of â€˜ race â€™ and â€˜ outside agitator â€™ argument , ran a major story on broadwater farm under the headline : â€˜ burnt-out hulk litter this concrete jungle â€¦ despair hang heavy â€™ ( 8 october 1985 ) .
a number of story using such imagery were run by both the quality and the popular press during this period , but similar argument are to be found in parliamentary debate ( hansard , vol. 84 , 21 october 1985 , cols. 30-.46 , 388 ) , and even in official report produced by the police on the riot in birmingham and london .
the â€˜ city of inner despair â€™ were conceived a the breeding ground for disorderly protest , and however hard the government tried to break the causal link between the two , it wa forced to take on board the need to restore order not only through the police but through promise of help for the inner city .
much a in 1980â€“1 , the â€˜ social cause â€™ argument can not be seen separately from the broader debate about the future of the british economy and society .
the government 's record on unemployment wa a heavily politicized issue , and just a in 1981 it vehemently denied any responsibility for the riot through it pursuit of free-market policy .
but the government did find a way of accepting a link between the riot and social problem without bringing it main policy into the debate : namely by linking the growth of violent disorder to crime and drug .
the emphasis on â€˜ crime â€™ and the â€˜ criminal act â€™ of the rioter in the official response to the 1985 riot took a general and a specific form .
the general form relied on the argument that the riot were not a form of protest against the insufferable social condition of inner-city area or the action of the police , but a â€˜ criminal act â€™ or a â€˜ cry for loot â€™ .
this wa an argument put most succinctly by geoffrey dear , chief constable of the west midland ( dear , 1985 ) and by douglas hurd , the home secretary , in relation to handsworth .
but it recurred a a theme in official and press response to the other riot .
the specific form wa built upon the notion that the outbreak of violence in handsworth and brixton , in particular , wa brought about by â€˜ drug baron â€™ who saw the police attempting to curb their activity and control â€˜ their territory â€™ .
numerous example of this line of argument can be found in dear 's report on handsworth , and in press coverage during the riot .
taking the specific argument about the role of drug and â€˜ drug baron â€™ in stimulating the riot , this seems to have served two purpose .
first , it distanced the riot from the social , economic , political , and other grievance which had been linked to them by locating the cause outside the â€˜ social problem â€™ of inner-city dweller and in the â€˜ simple greed â€™ of the drug baron to accumulate â€˜ loot â€™ .
second , just a dear 's image of a few hundred â€˜ young black criminal â€™ wa used to explain what happened in handsworth , the problem of drug wa used to explain what happened at a national level .
the issue of drug provided an everyday image , already a national issue through saturation medium coverage and public debate , around which the police , the home office , and other institution could depoliticize the riot .
responding to the handsworth event douglas hurd wa moved to argue forcibly that such event were senseless and reflected more on those who participated in them than on the society in which they took place : â€˜ the sound which law abiding people in handsworth heard on monday night , the echo of which i picked up on tuesday , wa not a cry for help but a cry for loot â€™ ( financial time , 13 september 1985 ) .
the chief constable for the west midland , geoffrey dear , took this argument further by pointing out that the day before the riot a successful carnival had taken place , with the support of local community leader .
he drew the conclusion from this that the riot â€˜ came like a bolt out of the blue â€™ ( guardian , 21 november 1985 ) .
such language focused attention on the individual or group who were â€˜ breaking the law â€™ , â€˜ committing criminal act â€™ , and threatening the interest of the law-abiding â€˜ majority â€™ .
potential application
overview
ian masser
given the extent to which geographic information management is application driven , it is important to consider in some depth the characteristic of some potential application field .
in the process it will be necessary to examine not only the extent to which geographic issue feature on the policy issue agenda in these field but also the institutional context which governs decision-making and the extent to which data are available for research and policy analysis purpose .
question on data availability are likely to be particularly important where user are heavily dependent on secondary source .
in situation of this kind provision governing access and format may exert powerful constraint on the use that can be made of these data .
the first four chapter in this part examine issue relating to geographic information handling in term of four potential application field .
the author were asked to review the current state of the art in their field with particular reference to the likely requirement of the future policy issue agenda in term of the demand that they make on geographic information management .
author were also asked to evaluate critically the state of geographic information provision in term of the need of user and to consider the impact of user requirement on the development of geographic information handling methodology .
in this way the chapter in this part present a number of perspective which essentially complement and amplify the discussion in the previous part .
in this case , however , methodological issue are tackled from a largely user standpoint , whereas in the previous part they were viewed a substantive problem in their own right .
in effect the product of the discussion in both part is a matrix where the methodological issue are listed along one axis and particular application field on the other .
figure 8.1 summarizes the main feature of this matrix .
there are important difference between the chapter in this part both in the nature of the demand that they make on geographic information and also in the relative importance that is attached to geographic criterion a against other issue by planner and decision-makers in these field .
the first three chapter dealing with environmental monitoring , natural and technological hazard and settlement and infrastructure application make use of a very wide range of geographic information .
a major feature of these application is the extent to which they involve overlay of a variety of information drawn from different source on a topographic map base .
for this reason they make particularly heavy demand on current gi technology both because of the size of the data set and the diversity of the data structure that are involved .
brown 's chapter on geodemographics discus an application field where primary data collected for operational purpose are typically evaluated in relation to secondary data derived from source such a the census of population .
the range of data set involved and the demand that are made on gi technology in application of this kind are often more limited than those of previous application field .
however , this is offset by the demand that are made on spatial analysis and modelling methodology .
rhind 's chapter on environmental monitoring and prediction discus the role of geographic information management in promoting sustainable development on a global scale in the context of the issue identified in the brundtland report .
it point to the impact that the finding of global environmental research are likely to have on most sector of the uk economy .
this contribution also indicates the wide variety of group that are involved in monitoring environmental change and draw attention to the role that the european community is increasingly playing in co-ordinating these effort .
one of the example of work in progress discus research that ha been carried out for the european commission a part of the corine project .
this highlight the challenge for research in a field categorized by a lack of data for some country , varying definition and standard in use , and unreasonably high user expectation .
the chapter a a whole draw attention to a number of key methodological issue that are involved in environmental monitoring and prediction .
these include the highly specialized nature of much of the technical analysis , the limited capacity for checking the accuracy of many of the finding of this kind of research and the consequent need for the development of knowledge-based inference machine for extracting useful information from secondary and often proxy data .
in chapter 10 gatrell and vincent consider a wide range of natural and technological hazard including industrial hazard , risk assessment , hazardous waste disposal , emergency planning , natural hazard and environmental epidemiology .
the substantive content of much of this research straddle the social , environmental and mathematical science .
to model the dispersal of a plume of toxic gas generated by a chemical explosion and it impact on the surrounding area , for example , an understanding of atmospheric dispersion model , epidemiology and population distribution would be required .
in application of this kind the author argue that geographic information management in general and gi in particular have an important role to play and they give a number of example from research in progress to illustrate the current state of the art in this field .
the discussion of operational problem and prospect further amplifies many of the issue raised in preceding chapter , particularly with respect to the problem caused by the dependency on secondary data source and the need for much more detailed geographic information to overcome some of the problem associated with linking data set .
like most of the other contributor to this section , gatrell and vincent see decision support system a a major area for future development in the geographic information management field .
given that â€˜ most gi are rather dumb system , requiring intelligent , very computer literate user â€™ , managing natural and technological hazard mean that this knowledge base must be built into the system so that it can be utilized quickly by untrained user after a disaster ha taken place or in an emergency .
the settlement and infrastructure field is also characterized by a wide range of user with a great diversity of interest .
these include not only central and local government but also major utility company and transport supplier .
according to shepherd these user share a common need for up-to-date information on the amount , capacity and condition of urban land , transport network , utility , hospital , school and other major communal facility .
in addition to these data requirement , such user are also interested in developing their capability for modelling the changing relationship between the supply and demand for infrastructure in the context of both public and private sector decision-making .
shepherd , more than any other contributor to this section , draw attention to the extent to which current effort in the geographic information management field are constrained by the highly fragmented nature of both data supplier and user both in sectoral and spatial term , the great diversity of data set in varying format and the difficulty presented by institutional factor such a copyright provision , data ownership and the control of access to data .
digital data occupy a central position in this application field and a number of example are given to illustrate the extent to which their development ha been hampered or facilitated in various country by the policy of the organization with responsibility for digital data provision .
in chapter 12 brown review the development of geodemographics from it origin in the computer-based social area analysis of u census tract data from 1950 onwards .
he describes the feature of the four main system that are currently in use in the uk and illustrates their application with reference to example drawn from retail analysis and store location , market research , credit rating and target marketing .
brown demonstrates the extent to which the growth of research activity in this field ha led to the emergence of improved cluster analytic method for the multidimensional classification of people according to the type of area in which they live .
in the last part of this contribution , brown identifies a number of priority for future research .
like gatrell and vincent and shepherd before him , he is concerned with the lack of detailed geographical information particularly in this case with reference to the year between census .
spatial linkage also feature prominently in brown 's discussion which amplifies many of the point made in the earlier contribution from openshaw in chapter 3 .
the analysis point to the need for the development of a fuzzy geodemographics methodology which take account of the effect of different type of spatial error .
other major research priority for the future in this application field are the development of response-modelling technique to explore the factor affecting consumer choice .
this is particularly important in a field where many of the demand made by user are highly specialized in nature .
in summary , then , a fig. 8.1 show , many of the issue raised by this review of geographic information handling in the four potential application field are essentially complementary to those discussed in the part on methodological development .
spatial analysis feature particularly prominent on the research agenda relating to natural and technological hazard and geodemographics .
question relating to data integration emerge a being particularly critical in term of environmental monitoring and settlement and infrastructure application .
spatial linkage and question relating to spatial representation feature prominently in the settlement and infrastructure and geodemographics field .
the evaluation of error propagation is a matter of considerable significance in the environmental monitoring and settlement and infrastructure field .
finally , in all application field , the finding of the review above suggest that there is a need for improvement in user interface of all kind .
this is particularly evident in the demand for the development of knowledge-based decision support system and modelling capability .
such demand highlight the extent to which geographic information management must be regarded a a mean to an end rather than an end in itself when viewed from the standpoint of the need of planner and decision-makers .
the final chapter in this section round off the discussion of applications-related research with an examination of organizational issue that need to be taken account of in handling geographic information .
campbell present a conceptual framework for the analysis of the organizational issue involved in the development and implementation of gi .
this contribution cut across many of the question relating to particular application that were raised in preceding chapter and draw attention to the danger of neglecting institutional and personal consideration in practice .
all too often , a the experience of many mature application indicates , technical and data-related matter dominate the system development phase and technically elegant solution are devised which fail to take enough account of user need and capability .
campbell discus the role of geographic information in organization and the process of gi diffusion drawing upon research from a variety of social science discipline .
the finding of the analysis are summarized in two set of research question for all application field .
in this way she set out a research agenda for applications-related research which serf a similar function to the spatial analysis research agenda set out by openshaw at the beginning of part two .
environmental monitoring and prediction
david rhind
introduction
monitoring of the state of and the change in the environment is now universally recognized a being of profound importance to mankind ( tickell 1986 , 1989 ) .
this awareness is most obviously manifested by the frequent appearance of press report on ozone depletion , on the greenhouse effect and possible global warming , on nitrate pollution , on deforestation in the tropic , on decreasing biological diversity and on many other environmentally related topic .
usually these report concentrate on prophecy of a forthcoming armageddon but many also describe a dire contemporary situation .
thus the algal bloom off the italian adriatic shore in 1989 is reckoned to have caused the holiday trade and local fisherman loss running into ten if not hundred of million of pound .
more globally , the brundtland report ( wced 1987 ) noted that , in the 900 day during which the world commission wa at work ,
1 .
the african drought put the life of 35 million people at risk and may have killed 1 million of them ;
2 .
the bhopal accident killed 2000 and injured 200 000 more ;
3 .
the chernobyl disaster caused environmental damage across europe ;
4 .
a chemical fire in switzerland caused toxic material to be transported by the rhine at least a far a the netherlands ;
5 .
at least 60 million people died of diarrhoeal disease caused by malnutrition and dirty water .
a a consequence of these and other disaster , of rapid and world-wide broadcasting of them and of the concerned action of many individual , the environment is now â€˜ moving fast up the political agenda â€™ ( patten 1989 ) .
the then prime minister also emphasized the political awakening to the environment in statement such a : â€˜ it is possible that with all these enormous change ( population , agricultural , use of fossil fuel ) concentrated into such a short period of time , we have unwittingly begun a massive experiment with the system of this planet itself â€™ ( thatcher 1988 ) .
the political concern is not altruistic .
mr thatcher , a recent convert to environmental conservation , clearly held the opinion that environmental problem are not only soluble but that solving them can be shown to be cost-effective :
in the past when we identified form of pollution , we have shown our capacity to act effectively .
the great london smog are now only a nightmare of the past .
we have cut airborne lead by 50 per cent .
we are spending â£4 billion on cleansing the mersey basin alone , and the thames now ha the cleanest metropolitan estuary in the world .
even though this kind of action may cost a lot , i believe it to be money well and necessarily spent because the health of our economy and the health of our environment are totally dependent upon each other â€¦ .
the government espouses the concept of sustainable economic development .
stable prosperity can be achieved throughout the world provided the environment is nurtured and safeguarded ( thatcher 1988 ) .
the primary official manifestation of this uk commitment to the environment ha been the government 's white paper ( hmso 1990 ) .
while criticized by many on the ground that it had few â€˜ teeth â€™ and represented a compromise between the doe and all other department ( notably those of agriculture and of transport ) , it wa none the le a major achievement .
it main section dealt with the government 's approach to the environment ( nationally , at the european level and globally ) , the nature and effect of the greenhouse effect , environmental problem in both rural and urban area ( especially in relation to land-use matter ) , pollution control and enhancing awareness of environmental matter .
central to this approach is said to be an emphasis upon guidance from the best available scientific evidence .
there is also a clear recognition of the need for monitoring and modelling of the state of the natural and man-influenced environment .
though there ha been a change of prime minister and of the secretary of state for the environment since the publication of the white paper , the environmental cause seems a strong a ever .
thus , the time of 17 december 1990 reported that a group of 40 of the most senior civil servant had met the previous weekend to discus â€˜ way in which the government machine could respond better to national and international problem such a the threat of global climate change â€™ .
pearce et al. ( 1989 ) have argued that the effect of government , commercial and other action on the environment can and should be assessed on a cost/benefit basis and this seems likely to be the basis on which at least the uk government will proceed .
given such a lead , it is scarcely surprising that british scientist have sought to demonstrate the practical and financial consequence of environmental event and process ; table 9.1 , for example illustrates their perception of the importance of global environmental research to the uk domestic economy .
if it now seems self-evident that monitoring of the global environment is necessary , indeed is even vital , the prediction of what is likely to happen is almost a important : the 170 000 people in the maldives are understandably worried about the prospect of global sea-level change since no part of the island is more than 2 m above present sea-level !
yet , a we shall see below , the prediction of the activity of natural and man-influenced environmental system requires an understanding of the interaction of process that , in many case , we simply do not yet have .
even monitoring of the present is very imperfect , a witness the order of magnitude variation in estimate now current for deforestation rate .
this chapter , then , attempt to review some of the problem in environmental monitoring and prediction , describes some of the relevant scientific and organizational activity and look ahead to what is likely to happen next .
the nature of environmental process
notwithstanding mr thatcher 's confidence that we can â€˜ fix â€™ environmental problem , the difficulty associated with a task even a apparently simple a monitoring a state variable are considerable .
some of the reason for this â€” the difficulty of deciding on appropriate proxy variable and on economical yet non-biasing temporal and spatial sampling framework , taking account of the relative importance of aperiodic and rare event a compared to near-continuous process , processing the vast volume of data usually involved and organizing the multidisciplinary and ( often ) multinational researcher involved â€” are discussed briefly later .
even at this stage , however , it is important to stress that we have a very incomplete understanding of many process in the natural environment .
the next section therefore attempt to summarize what we do know ; it is derived chiefly from the earth system science committee ( essc ) ( 1988 ) .
temporal and spatial scale of operation
even ignoring the effect of man , earth system process operate at a variety of spatial and temporal scale ( see fig. 9.1 ; see also rosswall et al. 1988 ) .
thus microclimate effect can have major short-term implication for local population ( such a those brought about by the photochemical smog of los angeles ) , are often applicable only over area of a few hectare or le and may operate on a diurnal cycle .
in contrast , soil creation ( cf. soil degradation ) may require hundred or thousand of year .
in between are variation in phenomenon such a el nino .
the sampling theorem indicates that we must sample these phenomenon at a frequency of no le than half their wavelength if we are to avoid bias in measurement .
in this chapter , the primary concern is with change occurring over time span from minute to century and from spatial wavelength from about 1 m to about 100 km .
it is , however , impossible to separate out process acting at different scale and over different period : a single volcanic eruption lasting hour or day sometimes lead to dust and gas being ejected into the atmosphere over a period of month and this in turn may lead to global climatic fluctuation over year or decade .
conversely , a large-scale and long-period phenomenon such a the movement of tectonic plate can lead to local stress accumulation and to it release by an earthquake in a few second and with highly local effect .
this therefore ha the following consequence :
1 .
we should know something about the periodicity of the variable we are monitoring if we are not to risk either unnecessarily detailed data collection or aliasing of trend ;
2 .
since we know that the periodicity of some variable is longer than the monitoring already carried out by human being , we must make use of surrogate or proxy variable to extend our range of knowledge ( such a via tree ring , palynology , oxygen isotope ratio from ice core and geomorphological evidence of environmental change ) ;
3 .
since we are not yet able to predict all the interaction which may be important in a study of environmental problem , collecting potentially useful data a well a those believed central to the problem under study is often wise ;
4 .
we must accept that most data collection exercise will inevitably be a compromise on the ground of cost and hence le than perfect data set will be produced ;
5 .
irrespective of the use of surrogate , monitoring environmental process only make sense over time-scales which are extended by human standard ( hence the igbp ( see below ) is designed to run for two or three decade ) ;
6 .
change in political priority may simply result in the need for informed guesswork if suitable data are not available .
much attention will be given throughout this chapter to monitoring the environment on a global basis .
this is not merely because of it topical nature .
the main reason is that â€” though some problem are local and are best studied that way â€” many others ( even involving a limited geographical extent ) are best understood within a global framework .
thus no meteorologist would attempt to forecast the weather 2 or 3 day ahead without using a mathematical model based on observation from all around the world .
moreover , recent work ha shown that much of the heat energy in the sea of the north atlantic ( every square kilometre of which give off a much energy a a nuclear power station and hence influence our weather ) is absorbed from sunlight in the tropical pacific and is carried by ocean current through the drake passage and up into the atlantic .
thus , to understand some local and many regional , national and continental and all global environmental event , we need to have a global perspective and hold global database ( rhind and mounsey 1989 ; mounsey and tomlinson 1988 ) .
in no way , however , doe this invalidate the need for microscale study â€” for instance , while global data set may indicate the extent of algal bloom and may suggest cause of them , only detailed study of the algae themselves can provide understanding of why the bloom is occurring .
the process themselves
a major complication , however , is that the environment can rarely be treated a in a laboratory experiment .
given this , determining the nature of the interaction between the variable becomes a matter of major difficulty .
figure 9.2 is taken from essc ( 1988 ) and is an attempt to show the interaction between environmental process , a well a the indicator and implication of change in the state of the environment .
in essence , the figure can be summarized a showing process within the physical climate system and within the biogeochemical one , these system being interwoven by the global role of water and increasingly affected by human activity .
the main component of the first system are well known : the atmosphere is it engine and atmospheric circulation is driven by difference in solar heating between the equatorial and polar region .
we are , however , becoming ever more aware of the massive influence of ocean on our climate ; research into ocean/atmosphere interaction is now a priority .
in ocean dynamic study , important topic are heat storage , circulation and the role of sea ice .
atmospheric circulation ( and , in turn , precipitation and temperature ) is also greatly affected by the balance of thermal energy at the land surface , itself affected by the vegetation and terrain characteristic .
the biogeochemical system embrace the totality of the movement of the key element essential to life â€” carbon , nitrogen , oxygen , phosphorus and others â€” through what ha been described a the total earth system .
the ocean and their biota play a central role in the carbon and nutrient cycle ; global ocean circulation is also critical to the global carbon budget .
trace gas in the atmosphere are key component of the cycle of such element a carbon , nitrogen , oxygen , sulphur and the halogen ; a is now well known , their concentration are much influenced by biogenic and anthropogenic activity and the concentration of such gas a carbon dioxide , ozone , methane , nitrous oxide and the chlorofluoromethanes ( cfms ) strongly affect radiative transfer and provide a link with the physical climate system .
a particular cause for concern is that the concentration of the two main cfms is increasing by about 5 per cent per annum while the equivalent figure for methane , nitrous oxide and carbon dioxide are 1 , 0.4 and 0.3 per cent .
finally , the chemistry of the stratosphere is dominated by the photochemical production and catalytic destruction of ozone .
there are several important way in which biogeochemical process can influence climate and hence habitability ( except where the latter is maintained at enormous cost ) .
if , for instance , the concentration of certain trace gas ( see above ) continue to increase , it ha been predicted that the earth 's surface temperature will increase by an amount comparable to that since the last major phase of the most recent glaciation ( about 18 000 year bp ) â€” though there is some dispute about the magnitude of the likely change .
the converse case â€” the effect of physical climate change on biogeochemical process â€” is also of obvious importance : change in annual average and cycle of temperature and precipitation , together with the probability of extreme event such a prolonged drought or frost , are major determinant of terrestrial ecosystem type , at least in area largely unaffected by human activity .
human-induced change to the environment have increased rapidly a a consequence of growing population number and of numerous development in technology .
fossil fuel combustion , for instance , ha led to increased concentration of sulphate in precipitation ( â€™ acid rain' ) and many river , lake and estuary have been greatly affected by phosphate from agricultural , urban or industrial source .
it should be stressed , however , that â€” while the outline of human activity are clear â€” there are still many uncertainty : for instance , we know that the atmospheric concentration of carbon dioxide ha risen about 9 per cent since 1959 .
but calculation of the total carbon released by fossil fuel combustion imply that , if all such carbon remained in the atmosphere , the concentration would have increased by twice a much .
the absolute difference between the two figure seems to be due to two different factor : absorption by the ocean and other reduction in carbon stock in living biota , the latter being strongly affected by human activity since the industrial revolution .
doe god play dice ?
much environmental prediction is predicated upon a logical positivist or newtonian deterministic basis .
the environmental system are effectively regarded a machine whose working can all be discovered , described in differential equation form and whose future activity can thus be predicted in detail if their initial state are known .
an argument by many atmospheric physicist , for example , is that shortcoming in the accuracy of weather prediction over period of more than a few day largely result from the unsophisticated nature of existing model , the lack of suitable data and inadequate computer power ( see fig. 9.3 , derived from tyler 1989 ) .
in contrast , the advocate of chaos theory ( e.g. stewart 1989 ) argue that some natural system are so sensitive to tiny stimulus that even â€˜ the faint beat of a butterfly 's wing could be the ultimate trigger for a hurricane â€™ .
in essence , they argue that tiny difference in the initial condition of many system can lead to widely different outcome since the system exhibit stochastic behaviour within a deterministic framework .
if so , there is little chance of making accurate long-term forecast of these system .
this ha been recognized by , among others , essc ( 1988 ) .
the author of that report accepted that there is a theoretical limit of 2â€“3 week in explicit prediction of day-to-day fluctuation in weather because of the influence of small-scale turbulence within the large-scale dynamic ; such limit imply that medium and long-term prediction must inevitably be stochastic .
to complicate matter still further , however , it seems at least possible that the component of the earth system may best be predicted deterministically at some spatial and temporal scale but stochastically at others and that the basis of prediction will differ for different component .
a an example , atmospheric temperature show marked day-to-day variability , a strong seasonal cycle with some year-to-year variation , a weak minimum variability for 10â€“20 year average and then increased variation again at longer time-scales ( essc 1988 ) .
it need hardly be pointed out that coping with such multiple database on which modelling is to be carried out is not a trivial exercise .
finally , it is perhaps worth concluding this section by emphasizing that there is still much controversy among the scientific community about the magnitude of many of the effect being discussed .
in general , the research work of the international group of scientist , including those involved in the inter-governmental committee and in the uk 's inter-agency committee on global environmental change , ha progressively reduced the magnitude of the predicted effect of , for example , global warming .
in giving the 1990 robens coal science lecture at the royal institution , sir john mason ( a former director of the meteorological office ) urged caution in interpreting the result of the massive atmospheric and oceanic modelling exercise ; a the equilibrium model are refined to take account of the thermal lag in ocean , etc. , lower value of predicted change are likely .
thus , while almost all scientist are â€” at the time of writing â€” convinced from the evidence that change in climate is under way and that regional effect may be much greater than the global average , the magnitude of the latter is now thought likely to be about half of what wa predicted only 5 year ago .
who is involved and what is going on ?
if we consider the environment in the broadest sense , much monitoring of it ha been in progress for many year and by an enormous diversity of organization in the uk ; these include the doe , the forestry commission , the nature conservancy , the health and safety executive , local authority , the component member of the natural environment research council ( such a the institute of terrestrial ecology and the british antarctic survey ) , individual or research group in academia , the cegb , the friend of the earth , and uk centre for economic and environmental development ( ceed ) ( 1989 ) .
the diversity of monitoring organization seems just a great in other country ; numerous multinational organization , both quasi-governmental ( e.g. the un environment programme ( unep ) ) and non-governmental ( e.g. the international union for the conservation of nature ( iucn ) and the international institute for environment and development ( iied ) ) are also active in the field .
moreover , commercial monitoring of the environment is now also routine , notably to predict crop yield in the main grain-growing area and hence facilitate the buying of future .
the international scientific involvement
scientist have both promoted and responded to the increasing interest in the environment .
thus , at the global scale , the international council of scientific union ( icsu ) ha endorsed the setting up of the international geosphere biosphere project ( igbp ) to â€˜ describe and understand the interactive physical , chemical and biological process that regulate the total earth system , the unique environment it provides for life , the change that are occurring in that system and the manner by which these change are influenced by human action â€™ ( report of ad hoc planning group for igbp 1986 , quoted in igbp 1988 ) .
priority in the igbp is intended to be given to those area which deal with key interaction and significant change on the time-scales of decade to century , that most affect the biosphere , that are most susceptible to human perturbation and those that will most likely lead to a practical , predictive capability for global change .
this is to be put into operation by concentrating on four theme :
1 .
documenting and predicting global change ;
2 .
observing and improving our understanding of dominant forcing function ;
3 .
improving our understanding of interactive phenomenon in the total earth system ;
4 .
assessing those effect of global change which will be large scale and cause major modification to both renewable and non-renewable resource .
by 1990 , igbp had progressed a far a defining a set of seven core project ( igbp 1990 ) addressing these four theme .
in addition , plan for regional research centre and for the data and information system for igbp ( known a igbp-dis ) had been announced .
the latter are particularly relevant to this chapter .
based in paris and working under professor i. rasool , it is intended to concentrate on managerial and policy aspect of the task in the first 2 year ( 1990â€“92 ) but then to expand considerably thereafter .
task agreed for the igbp-dis include making available a â€˜ directory of data directory â€™ modelled on nasa 's master directory , providing data set for education and training and undertaking a land cover pilot study .
until 1990 , remarkably little interaction had taken place between the â€˜ hard science â€™ and the social science community on global monitoring and prediction .
it is noteworthy , however , that the royal society made a plea for just this type of multidisciplinary work in it submission on greenhouse gas to the house of lord ' select committee on science and technology ( royal society 1989 : 40 ) .
fortunately , in addition to the action of the world â€˜ hard science â€™ community , a parallel effort is now being initiated by social scientist ( fuchs 1989 ; jacobson and price 1990 ) .
currently this is organized and sponsored by the international social science council ( issc ) , the un university and the international federation of institute of advanced study .
it objective are to :
1 .
improve scientific understanding and increase awareness of the complex dynamic governing human interaction with the total earth system ;
2 .
study , explore and anticipate social change affecting the global environment ;
3 .
identify broad social strategy to prevent or mitigate undesirable impact of global change or to adapt to change which are unavoidable ;
4 .
analyse policy option for dealing with global environmental change and promoting the goal of sustainable development .
jacobson and price ( 1990 ) provide a useful summary of the way in which human factor contribute to , and are affected by , likely global change ; in particular , they stress the importance of the data-handling issue and the role of gi in coping with some of the problem of data integration and manipulation .
the issc proposed it own work programme in the human dimension of global change in late 1990 .
while these organization intrinsically have a world-wide membership and are concerned with research into global environmental change , others share the same interest but from a more restricted geographical base .
a leading example of this is the european science foundation which ha sponsored research programme in both the physical and social science aspect of environmental change ( esf 1990 ) .
governmental and political involvement
the spread of environmental awareness is readily demonstrated : in 1972 , 26 country had environmental and natural resource management agency whereas , a decade later the number wa 144 ( wec 1985 ) .
in most country , the governmental and political concern with the environment ha come somewhat later than that of scientist or concerned lay people : the early development of the green party in the federal republic of germany is something of an exception .
such a commitment to the environment is , none the le , both welcome and necessary if resource are to be made available to tackle existing , let alone forthcoming , environmental problem .
the â€˜ conversion â€™ of the uk government ha been briefly described earlier ; it is manifested in their july 1989 commitment to spend â£10m. on climate change research in 1989/90 and the confident request to them from the advisory board for the research council ( abrc ) for an extra â£11m. in 1990 and â£13m. in the two succeeding year for additional environmental research .
perhaps the most significant development of all , however , is the increasing role of the european commission .
their role in bringing environmental impact assessment ( eia ) on to the statute book is well known , but the commission ha now made a proposal ( cec 1989 ) for the â€˜ establishment of the european environment agency and the european environment monitoring and information network â€™ .
the commission argues that there is at present no monitoring of environmental quality and trend on a european scale , nor any guarantee that the result of environmental monitoring will be comparable on a community-wide basis ( a realization brought about through the corine programme described later ) .
the objective of the new agency would be to assist the community and the member state to achieve the goal set out in the treaty of rome and in successive environmental action programme ; it must also , however , be seen in the context of the european council 's adoption on 2 december 1988 of the rhodes declaration on the environment and the environment chapter in the single european act which come into effect in 1992 .
the detailed specific role of â€˜ the system â€™ ( comprising both agency and network ) are envisaged a :
1 .
to provide the community , the member state and participating third country with the objective ( sic ) information requested for the formulation and implementation of sound and effective environmental policy ;
2 .
in particular , to provide technical , scientific and economic information requested by the commission in it task of identification , preparation and assessment of the implementation and result of environmental action and legislation ;
3 .
to stimulate the development and the application within the agency of technique of environmental modelling and forecasting in order that adequate preventative action can be taken at the appropriate time ;
4 .
to help ensure the harmonization and comparability of environmental data in the community a well a the integration of european environmental data into international environmental monitoring programme , such a those established within the framework of the united nation and it system of agency .
considerable stress is laid by the commission on collaboration with national data-gathering agency and with state adjacent to the community 's border .
it is envisaged that the totality of activity will extend the existing state of the european environment report ( cec 1988 ) through addressing the issue of the quality and sensitivity of the environment and also the pressure upon it .
priority for the new agency include study of atmospheric emission and quality , water resource and quality ( including the marine environment ) , soil erosion and pollution and important land resource , and biotopes and nature conservation .
at the time of writing , the proposal ha been approved and discussion are in progress on the location of the agency 's headquarters .
it seems certain to have major and growing effect upon environmental monitoring and prediction in europe and on the policy of component government .
yet this is by no mean the only plan which the community ha in relation to the environment : directorate-general xii ( that for science , research and development ) ha published detail of an extensive r&d programme ( cec 1990a ) and the commission a a whole ( cec 1990b ) ha made public it plan to develop regular official statistic of the environment .
the private sector
certain aspect of environmental monitoring have long been carried out by the private sector , notably that of crop state on a world-wide basis .
derived from satellite imagery at comparatively low resolution , predicted yield for different crop in different nation state become of commercial value .
however , significant resource are also being made available by the private sector where there is no immediate commercial gain : thus ibm uk ltd ha donated â£3.5m. of computing equipment to unep and ibm in general is actively supporting other r&d which can be expected to facilitate â€˜ sustainable development â€™ : ibm europe , for instance , ha invested $16m. in it bergen scientific centre to make it the focal point for the company 's environmental modelling and a centre for information on the environment and sustainable development .
on a more modest basis , ibm uk have agreed to fund a pilot global population database being built in birkbeck college to permit assessment of the impact of environmental change on the world s population .
many other company , of course , are supporting environmental work but ibm seems almost to be unique in it corporate commitment to use of high technology for facilitating â€˜ sustainable development â€™ .
some example of environmental monitoring project
the local scale
at the local scale , it is usually impossible to neglect the effect of multiple process operating .
an example of environmental modelling and prediction where the interaction between different process and dynamic feedback is taken into account is given by haber and schaller ( 1988 ) .
their work formed part of one unesco man and biosphere ( mab ) project , carried out in the berchtesgaden national park in bavaria .
following holling ( 1978 ) and grossman ( 1983 ) , they conceptualized the man/environment relationship at a series of hierarchical level :
1 .
the lowest , or process , level , is directly and ( usually ) obviously connected to perceived reality .
thus process and interrelationship are mostly obvious , simple and linear ; data are usually readily available and may be voluminous .
this level is readily handled in commercial gi ; ,
2 .
the intermediate , or dynamic , level is concerned with le frequent and often irregular event , e.g. a temporary lack of water because of fire , frost or excessive demand from an increasing populace .
characteristically , the data used may be more difficult to obtain and are usually more spatially aggregated ; modelling software which permit feedback loop is essential ;
3 .
the top , or strategic , level is the most difficult to explore ; the external influence are often unpredictable by formal mean .
relevant data are therefore difficult to identify and scenario building is one of the few approach available .
the haber and schaller approach is to â€˜ soft-couple â€™ these different level and their accompanying approach using an ecological balance model .
result of running this with different scenario include â€˜ time slice â€™ map of possible outcome and of the exchange of material and energy within the study area .
while ultimately qualitative , it seems to offer a method for coping with different type of process and incorporating stochastic and truly random event .
the national and continental scale : the corine project
the corine programme represents the product of much activity by the european commission 's directorate-general ( xi ) for the environment .
it originates from an italian request to the council of minister in 1973 to identify environmentally â€˜ balanced â€™ and â€˜ unbalanced â€™ area in the community ; the first attempt to do this were unsuccessful and , though by 1981 it wa clear that a new approach based on an environmental information system wa the most promising one , funding for this wa not secured until 1985 .
a 4-year experimental programme to â€˜ collect , co-ordinate and ensure the consistency of information on the state of the environment and natural resource in the european community â€™ wa set up and labelled corine .
the objective of corine were to be achieved through bringing together existing data holding in the member state , developing method for holding , analysing and presenting the data , and encouraging the exchange of data .
a number of priority area were identified , including biotopes of importance for nature conservation , acid deposition and it effect on soil and biotopes and the protection of the environment of the mediterranean region ( briggs and martin 1988 ) .
many achievement can be claimed by the corine project team .
a a result of some 40 sub-projects involving all the member state ( and often several group within each one ) , database of topography , soil , water resource and quality , biotopes , atmospheric emission , climate , soil erosion and administrative boundary have been built up for the whole community and can be interrelated ; other data set ( such a land cover , derived from satellite imagery ) have already been compiled for some of the member state .
various aspect of the corine project have been described in paper by rhind et al. ( 1986 ) , wiggins et al. ( 1987 ) , briggs and martin ( 1988 ) , briggs and mounsey ( 1989 ) and cec ( 1990c ) .
for the present purpose , however , the key finding are :
1 .
many necessary data set were unavailable for reason of administrative inadequacy , confidentiality constraint , cost or non-collection in certain country ;
2 .
the unavoidable reliance upon existing data set led to many problem due to non-harmonization even of commonly used variable , e.g. eight different definition of potential evapo-transpiration were in use in the member state and â€˜ maximum temperature â€™ at each weather station wa defined in at least four different way ;
3 .
the compilation of a spatially coherent database from mapped information compiled by different organization at different scale and on different topographic base ( see rhind and clark 1988 ) is often difficult ;
4 .
user expectation are often unreasonably high ; use of administrative computing system encourages bureaucrat to believe that environmental data can just a readily be analysed and used to support decision .
the â€˜ fuzzy â€™ nature of much geographically distributed data , the inherent error in it , the effect of the processing algorithm used and the assumption of known interaction between variable ensures that completely routine use by unskilled user is unlikely to be possible in the foreseeable future ;
5 .
more detailed data are required for particular task a the role of corine grows .
the european commission 's proposal for an environment agency ( see above ) will subsume the corine team and database .
the global scale : nasa and gems/grid
without question , the largest â€˜ player â€™ in the global environment arena is nasa .
the organization ha initiated many of the scheme which now are under discussion â€” the igbp , for instance , ha been strongly influenced by nasa proposal .
a summary of current activity and future plan is given in nasa ( 1988b ) .
by way of example , current programme are collecting global data on stratospheric ozone , on sea-surface and sea-ice variable and on the earth 's radiation budget .
approved future programme include the upper atmosphere research satellite , the nasa scatterometer and the joint nasa/cnes ocean topography experiment .
in addition , however , funding ha recently been approved for a much more ambitious scheme â€” the earth observing system ( eos ) .
this is conceived not a a set of hardware but a a comprehensive information system focused on the need identified by the essc ( see above ) and anticipating somewhat those of igbp .
it will collate data from the two proposed space station polar platform , one european space agency platform , a japanese one and also from the manned space station .
on this basis , a data flow of no le than 1 terabyte ( i.e. 10&sup12 ; byte ) day is anticipated by the mid-1990s .
a major user of nasa data and skill ha been the un environment programme .
formed in the wake of the 1972 stockholm conference , unep soon spawned the global environment monitoring system ( gem ) a well a an international register of potentially toxic chemical and a global network of source to locate and provide technical , scientific and management information on the environment .
the need for a mechanism to handle the global environmental data wa defined in the early 1980s and the global resource information database ( grid ) resulted .
mooneyhan ( 1988 ) ha described the progress of the pilot stage of grid which culminated in approval for a full operational phase ; grid is now involved in integrating , storing and exploiting a variety of global environmental database , mostly acquired from nasa .
in addition to the work being carried out in the unep hq at nairobi and in the gems/grid site in geneva , a series of grid regional node are now being set up world-wide , each equipped with the same hardware and software and local subset of the data .
the first of these is in bangkok and another is to be opened in latin america .
moreover , national node are also being set up ; the recent ibm gift to grid ( see above ) ha ensured that powerful microcomputer have been installed in many african country , together with national and continental data set .
training of unskilled staff to operate this equipment and to exploit the scientific data is now a major role for grid staff .
technical aspect and problem
it follows from much of the above that there are major challenge in use of environmental data .
these are best considered by itemizing characteristic of contemporary environmental monitoring and prediction :
1 .
the use of secondary data , rather than primary data collected by the data analyst ;
2 .
the need , at least with data on the terrestrial environment , for an inference process to extract useful information from the secondary ( and often proxy ) data ;
3 .
the data volume collected are often voluminous even by the standard of contemporary computing facility ;
4 .
the analysis carried out are often arcane in detail to all but a small group of â€˜ high priest â€™ ;
5 .
we have only a limited capacity for checking the accuracy of many environmental monitoring result , let alone of prediction .
some of these characteristic are now addressed in slightly more detail ; it will be obvious that such problem merit a paper in themselves .
remote sensing a a data collection process
a the need for more extensive , high-resolution yet consistent and up-to-date data becomes more pressing , increased use of remote sensing seems inevitable .
no other data collection methodology obviates the short distance variation in data set induced by variation in data collection and aggregation methodology typically encountered between adjacent nation state .
the problem is now recognized , especially in europe , and strenuous effort are being made to harmonise the data set ( e.g. through the use of standard classification , such a that for land use produced by the conference of european statistician in 1989 ) .
at present , however , ground-collected environmental data are far le than ideal .
recognizing this , nasa ( 1988a ) concluded that forthcoming global science project would need land surface altitude data at three resolution : 1 km resolution in xy and 10â€“100 m resolution in z on a global basis , 100 m xy resolution and 1â€“10 m resolution in z for regional database and 10 m xy resolution and 0.1â€“1 m in z for local study .
they argued that existing map and digitized file from them are unable to meet these need at global or regional scale and only remote sensing could help in the short term : the availability of stereometric data from the french spot satellite ha already led to proposal for automated creation of global digital elevation model with a spatial ( xy ) resolution of about 30 m ( muller 1989 ) .
for many terrestrial purpose , however , a substantial inference process must take place to convert the radiometric measurement into useful information ( in contrast , more direct measurement of sea state , etc. are possible ) .
the value of this information is inherently dependent upon the quality of the inference process and that in turn depends upon the complexity of the scene , the spatial , temporal and spectral resolution of the sensor and the particular algorithm used .
