thus far , for instance , accuracy of land cover ( let alone what is often needed , i.e. land use ) determined from landsat and spot imagery for the uk have rarely been higher than 70 per cent unless trivial classification ( e.g. built/unbuilt land ) have been used .
one solution to this is to â€˜ densify â€™ the ground control and another is to use contextually based classifier rather than the traditional , spectrally based one .
but the ground condition in some part of europe make good result extremely difficult to obtain : some area of portugal , for instance , have field size of only a few metre and exhibit multi-level and multi-seasonal cropping .
a further difficulty is the cost of the data in relation to it anticipated benefit .
relatively few cost/benefit study have yet been carried out except in such area a crop prediction and ocean navigation ( where they are generally shown to be highly advantageous in saving someone else 's money ) .
it may be that a study commissioned from contractor by the british national space centre will provide useful evidence on the cash value of remote sensing ; it is particularly timely in view of the appointment of professor pearce ( see above ) a the adviser to the uk secretary of state for the environment .
other work on assessment of cost and benefit of such data have been carried out for the french government by professor didier ( 1990 ) .
finally , since data are often costly to acquire , it follows that summary of them e.g. the state of the european environment report ( cec 1988 ) and the environmental data report of the un ep ( gem marc 1989 ) are of considerable value .
equally , the â€˜ signposting â€™ role now being adopted by unep 's grid project â€” which is expected to offer on-line access to a catalogue of environmental data set and detail of the responsible agency in 1991 â€” is to be welcomed .
the data problem and partial solution
the lack of harmonization of definition and method of collection of environmental data have already been outlined above .
in the european community of 12 nation state , for instance , no le than eight procedure for calculating potential evapo-transpiration have been in use !
the need for harmonization is obvious and , in this respect , must follow from the successful pioneering achievement of eurostat in harmonizing the definition in trade , demographic and other â€˜ social science â€™ statistic ( see cec 1990b ) .
an additional and severe problem is that the volume of data which are already collected are huge by the standard of only a decade ago .
by 1995/96 , they will be very much greater , notably from the eos programme .
nasa ha announced plan to collect up to 1 terabyte ( 10&sup12 ; byte ) day .
since remote sensing data set ( especially those pertaining to terrestrial area of the globe ) typically need much pre-processing to calibrate , transform and then perform an inference process and hence convert measurement of the radiation reflected or emitted by small area of ground into useful ( e.g. land cover ) data , the processing power required is going to be formidable .
other , non-remote-sensing data set are smaller in volume but more complex in structure and in their characteristic .
to hold all of the 1/50 000 scale topographic map coverage alone for the european community would probably require about 3 terabyte of storage .
parenthetically , the form in which the data may be held can affect dramatically the scale of the problem faced .
it would be technically possible , for instance , to hold in le than 1 terabyte detailed information for every individual in the world , akin to that collected about everyone in the uk through the census of population .
in practice , legislation generally forbids such data being held thus and distributed in anything other than area aggregate form ; the cross-tabulations commonly employed ( e.g. to give table of population number broken down by age and sex ) often result in a great multiplication of the data volume .
this all present major problem of data storage , handling ( especially for global data set ) , display and dissemination .
fortunately , technical development have ensured that the increase in computing power per unit cost ha been growing at about an order of magnitude every 6 year over the last three decade .
indeed , this may grossly under-represent the present situation : the rapid spread of unix-based system seems to facilitate competition and , at the time of writing , performance of workstation per unit cost seems to be increasing at about 50 per cent per annum .
moreover , the advent of low-cost , high-density-storage device like cd-rom may well make substantial data set available even to those with modest computing power : a cd-rom , for instance , can hold about 600 megabyte ( i.e. 10&sup6 ; byte ) , can be reproduced currently for about â£1 and read on a device costing about â£400 .
yet , despite such revolutionary change in technology , novel solution will still be required to make effective use of the new data set .
one such solution is goodchild and yang 's ( 1989 ) scheme for a hierarchical spatial data structure to handle data for the spherical earth .
a particular problem is that little or no quantitative regard ha been paid until recently to the effect of quality variation in environmental data .
the literature of the last few year is thronged with paper proposing that these must be taken into account â€” but with few realistic proposal on how this is to be achieved .
goodchild ( 1988 ) ha made perhaps the best summary of the problem and possible solution to date .
finally , relatively little emphasis ha been given to the management of data a a corporate resource , available to a wide community : many environmental data are collected on a project-by-project basis and â€˜ lost â€™ once the urgency of the initial research is over .
however , the 1990 report of the data and facility working group of the uk inter-agency committee on global environmental change argued strongly that planning for the dissemination and maintenance of such data wa a vital role .
it also noted that ownership of and access to environmental data wa a policy matter of the greatest importance ; this may be more difficult in â€˜ within country â€™ data than with global data since the latter are often already the subject of international exchange agreement at zero cost .
global environmental research and the social science
thus far , the emphasis ha been very much on the natural science aspect of environmental monitoring and prediction .
that is unremarkable since the anticipation of such problem and assessment of some of their cause and magnitude can only be made by environmental scientist .
yet the consequence of any significant change are a matter of the utmost concern to planner and to politician at all level , the quantification of the cost and benefit of alternative policy strategy is very much a matter for economist , and the adaptation of society to massive change is a particular interest to sociologist and others .
for geographer â€” who span the conventional ( and now archaic ) dichotomy between the natural and the environmental science â€” all of these aspect need to be woven together to anticipate the likely spatial pattern of the effect of massive change , the redistribution in trade , health and wealth which they will bring about and the â€˜ knock on â€™ effect these consequence themselves will have on society and the environment .
for social scientist , then , environmental monitoring and prediction are a matter of considerable significance , especially a â€˜ globalization â€™ of market and economy proceeds .
the economic and social research council ( esrc ) commissioned two report on global environmental change to guide or at least inform the allocation of research funding .
these report ( pearce 1990 ; turner 1990 ) may be taken a informed view from at least the economist in the uk social science community on the social science aspect of global environmental change .
pearce ( 1990 ) , for instance , presented an economist 's view of the high-priority topic for research a follows :
1 .
the theory and practice of international agreement , viewed in a game theory context ;
2 .
the theory of optimal behaviour under scientific uncertainty ; ,
3 .
cost-benefit framework , especially for dealing with man-induced climatic effect ;
4 .
definition of the impact of climate change on eastern europe and the developing country ;
5 .
appropriate policy on non-carbon dioxide greenhouse gas ;
6 .
assessment of the ecological value of tropical forest ;
7 .
economic instrument for reducing greenhouse gas ;
8 .
the international politics of climate change ;
9 .
national and global energy option given the greenhouse effect ;
10 .
trade and global change .
turner ( 1990 ) argued that the priority are a follows :
1 .
the socio-economic consequence of sea-level rise ;
2 .
technique for the valuation of environmental resource and effect ;
3 .
an assessment of management tool , institutional adaptation and policy integration in regard to global environmental change ;
4 .
study of agriculture in relation to climate change ;
5 .
understanding of how environmental issue and technical change interact ;
6 .
the interrelationship between environmental ethic and economics .
there is , then , no shortage of social science research which is claimed to be necessary if we are to cope with environmental change .
some of it is being carried out : in march 1991 esrc announced a â£6m programme of research on global environmental change .
however , perhaps most critical of all in the early stage is social science practice rather than research .
in the usa at least , there need to be an improvement in scientist ' understanding of how to influence the allocation of funding for remedial measure or for further research ( see kitsos and ashe 1989 ) .
all too often , it seems that the coupling of an a yet imperfect scientific understanding to policy-making is tenuous ( see table 9.2 ) .
fortunately , considerable high-quality work ha already begun on the legal , political and institutional aspect of global environmental change ( see , for instance , nitze 1990 ) .
what next ?
the content of this chapter have scarcely touched upon such important environmental research a the world ocean climate experiment or the world climate research programme .
nor ha there been any discussion of the growing requirement in the uk ( a elsewhere ) to carry out an environmental impact assessment for all major development .
the consequence for some nation or subgroup in a population of being without access to environmental data or the tool for analysing them while others have such access are potentially profound â€” a the european commission ha recognized in issuing a directive on public access to environmental data ( cec 1990d ) â€” yet space limitation have also precluded their discussion .
despite these shortcoming dictated by the space available , what ha been said should be sufficient to illustrate the enormous and diverse scope of environmental monitoring and prediction .
such is it diversity that measuring the total expenditure on it seems quite impossible and the number of â€˜ actor â€™ already involved is vast .
what is clear , however , is that monitoring is rarely a simple exercise , that the time-scales of the sponsor of monitoring and environmental research are rarely long term though most science is inevitably of this nature and that the increasingly multinational nature of such work necessitates a degree of management not always present in local or nationally based scheme .
in these circumstance , it is scarcely surprising that there is evidence of international and interdisciplinary dispute , a terney ( 1989 ) ha chronicled in regard to igbp .
moreover , a newby ( 1990 ) ha pointed out , the complexity of environmental research have created a new kind of relationship between research and policy : â€˜ in the past , â€¦ the relationship wa predicated on the belief that science provided decision-makers with objective â€˜ hard â€™ fact on which to base their soft , value-ridden policy â€¦ .
but now we find scientist delivering only â€˜ soft â€™ , uncertain â€˜ fact â€™ to decision-makers facing â€˜ hard â€™ decision . â€™
yet , even if all of this cause great uncertainty among scientist , it is still a reasonable prediction that a decade ahead will see both environmental monitoring and prediction treated a an everyday need and activity in major organization .
we shall also see the existence of binding requirement upon national government â€” certainly in the european community â€” to collect at least basic environmental indicator in a harmonized form ( cec 1990b ) .
more than that , it seems highly likely that the social science aspect of environmental problem will underpin the action of government and international agency .
perhaps the single most important conclusion to be drawn from this review is that the monitoring , modelling and management of the global environment are task which uniquely require the integration of skill and technique from many discipline .
building such research team is essential .
underlying it all , however , is one obvious fact : that the gathering and analysis of geographically distributed environmental data form the necessary starting-point for ensuring the success of our future on earth .

managing natural and technological hazard
anthony c. gatrell and peter vincent
introduction
few area of the application of gi technology can be a socially significant , or environmentally relevant , a the management of emergency and disaster due to natural and technological hazard .
whether attempting to construct a database of resource for use in planning response to nuclear emergency , developing optimal route for scheduling the safe transport of hazardous substance , or monitoring the health implication of a disaster , gi can assist in identifying possibility and formulating solution .
the 1990s have been declared the international decade for natural disaster reduction .
let u hope that g1s can , in some small way , help mitigate the suffering and hardship felt by all those afflicted by the effect of hazard which , a we have learnt with bitter recent experience in the uk , can occur in an untimely fashion in the most improbable of place .
research on hazard is multidisciplinary and straddle the social , environmental and mathematical science .
if , for instance , we wish to model the dispersal of a plume of toxic gas resulting from a chemical explosion , ass it possible impact on human health and evaluate likely evacuation scenario of the population at risk we would require , for example , a knowledge of atmospheric dispersion model , epidemiology and population distribution .
some researcher suggest that hazard ( and emergency that might result ) can be regarded a either natural or technological .
we shall elaborate on this simple division below but suggest here that the wealth of literature on the former ( see , for instance , burton et al. 1978 and perry 1981 ) ha yet to be matched by a similar volume of work on the latter .
there are sign that this imbalance is being rectified ( zeigler et al. 1983 ) .
hazard research , like few other gi application area , is not only stretching the present technology to it limit but is a quite remarkable focus of international effort into several important research-related area such a expert system and simulation study .
in this sense the scene is clearly one of hazard problem looking for improved gi rather than for gi looking for good problem , a is all too often the case .
hazard research
what are hazard ?
formally , a hazard can be defined a : â€˜ a physical situation with a potential for human injury , damage to property , damage to the environment , or some combination of these â€™ ( health and safety executive 1989 : 30 ) .
a hazard is a threat which , given a set of circumstance , may become translated into a realized event .
what we choose to call this event depends upon factor such a it magnitude : we speak for instance of accident , emergency , disaster and catastrophe , each of which carry connotation concerning the scale of the event and each of which will have a set of human and/or environmental consequence .
on 30 march 1956 one of the most powerful volcanic disturbance this century , the bezymianny eruption , occurred .
few people noticed the event a it took place in an uninhabited part of kamchatcka and caused no known casualty .
in contrast , the extrusion of a small volume of lava from a secondary cone on the slope of tristan da cunha became the focus of global interest when , during october 1961 , the island 's lobster processing plant wa smothered and the 300 people of the island 's only settlement were evacuated to the uk .
these two contrasting event emphasize the central fact that even natural hazard are not defined solely by the characteristic of the event but by the interaction of those event with the human occupation of the threatened area .
a noted above , the simple division of hazard is between those which are natural and those which are technological or human-induced .
each of these may be further subdivided and the typology shown in fig. 10.1 draw upon the classic work of burton and kates ( 1964 ) in separating geophysical from biological hazard and zeigler et al. ( 1983 ) in distinguishing public ( or what we might call societal ) hazard from private ( individual ) one .
the further subdivision of natural hazard is self-explanatory a is that of individual technological hazard .
zeigler divide the public hazard into those which result from the production of raw material and manufactured good , those which originate in transport and transmission and those which threaten the public in their role a consumer .
the united nation disaster relief organization ( undro ) ha produced a classification of disaster , derived on the basis of case data ( table 10.1 ) .
undro recognizes three basic type of disaster : accident , natural event , and other disaster .
the differentiation of accident from other type is useful in the context of emergency planning and is not so clearly conveyed by other hazard typology .
however , the temptation in all classification exercise is to produce partitional scheme and this should really be resisted here .
it is more useful ( johnson 1983 ) to adopt a non-partitional scheme ( fig. 10.1 ) that recognizes , for instance , food poisoning a both a personal and consumer hazard , or lead pollution ( e.g. from car exhaust ) a both a meteorological event and technological hazard ( both public and private ) .
an air crash ( a at lockerbie ) affect an entire community directly , a well a being an occupational hazard and risk to the individual traveller .
hillslope failure ( a at aberfan in south wale in 1966 ) is a geomorphic hazard but may arise because of raw material extraction .
dutch elm disease is a floral hazard but is exacerbated by the transport of infected log .
hohensemer et al. ( 1983 ) make the important point that hazard are multidimensional in nature and suggest 12 dimension along which any hazard may be scored ( see table 10.2 ) .
these factor will , of course , be important in deciding on the role that gi can play in hazard monitoring and emergency planning .
for instance , some event will persist briefly , have immediate consequence , minor transgenerational effect and have little or no potential for non-human mortality ( an air crash is an example ) .
others , such a a chemical explosion involving the release of a toxic gas , may persist for a week or more , have delayed consequence , may affect a future generation if the toxic chemical is mutagenic and may have a significant impact on plant and animal population .
there may be more scope for a gi approach in the latter than in the former situation .
the hohensemer scheme is not , however , beyond criticism .
for example , can hazard really be thought of a having any intentionality ?
industrial hazard and risk assessment
once hazard have been identified there may follow effort to ass the hazard risk .
by â€˜ risk â€™ we understand â€˜ the likelihood of a specified undesired event occurring within a specified period or in specified circumstance â€™ ( health and safety executive 1989 : 30 ) .
it may , of course , be easier to quantify risk for natural hazard because of historical record and statistical estimation of recurrence interval .
technological hazard are more unpredictable and may be extremely rare , so that it becomes difficult to use probability concept to quantify risk , especially those which rely on notion of relative frequency .
the identification of chemical hazard requires that the location of site be known , together with the dangerous substance stored or processed there .
in the uk ( see petts 1988 for a splendid overview ) the health and safety executive must be notified of site where hazardous substance in excess of threshold limit are handled .
such substance and threshold include , for instance , 25 tonne of flammable liquid petroleum gas and the explosive sodium chlorate , and 10 tonne of toxic chlorine gas .
some 1600 installation are notifiable , along with thousand of kilometre of pipeline ( mostly high-pressure natural gas transmission line ) .
following a number of major accident involving industrial activity ( including those at flixborough , humberside in 1974 ; beek , the netherlands in 1975 ; and seveso and manfredonia in italy in 1976 ) , the ec issued a directive on major accident hazard .
the directive , which is commonly referred to a the â€˜ seveso â€™ directive , wa issued in june 1982 and in the uk it requirement were embodied in the control of major accident hazard ( cimah ) regulation ( 1984 ) .
among other thing , these regulation require operator of hazardous installation to draw up on-site and off-site emergency plan and to give information to member of the public who live or work near such installation ( houston 1987 ) .
there are presently about 300 cimah site .
however , many potentially hazardous chemical installation are not covered by such regulation .
clearly , a plant which store 1 tonne le than is required for notification slip through the net , while large number of handling site ( warehouse , for instance ) are not covered .
the regulation have obvious implication for g1s work , a illustrated below .
in halton borough , cheshire , for instance , there are 13 notifiable installation , with over 20 000 people living within the defined â€˜ consultation zone â€™ ( petts 1988 ) .
it is also relevant to point out here the work of the health and safety executive in developing technique for quantitative risk assessment ( health and safety executive 1989 ) .
such technique include the use of a â€˜ risk assessment tool â€™ ( riskat ) , input to which include data on wind direction and speed ; the output of which is a map of calculated risk contour .
in societal risk assessment â€˜ it is also necessary to input data on the distribution of population around the installation .
this can be a difficult and time-consuming process , and inevitably some simplifying assumption are needed â€™ ( health and safety executive 1989 : 17 ) .
again , the g1s implication are clear and are discussed below .
hazardous waste disposal
the disposal of both nuclear and non-nuclear waste ha proved to be of major public concern in recent year .
the rather clumsy handling by nirex of the search for site to store low- and intermediate-level waste , together with very recent worry about the import of toxic waste such a polychlorinated biphenyls ( pcbs ) , are but two example of such concern in the uk .
however , while the former ha been given considerable attention by geographer ( summarized by openshaw et al. 1989 ) there ha been relatively little such work in the uk on the disposal of non-nuclear waste .
this contrast with the situation in the usa ( greenberg and anderson 1984 ) , where the clean-up of abandoned site is a priority .
certainly , in term of the volume of waste generated in the uk non-nuclear hazardous industrial waste should be high on the environmental agenda ; while le than 1 million tonne of all nuclear waste were generated in 1985â€“86 , 10 time that amount were generated of hazardous waste ( openshaw et al. 1989 : 13 ) .
attention should be drawn to the three annual report of the hazardous waste inspectorate ( now merged with other branch of the doe to form her majesty 's inspectorate of pollution ) .
these contain some valuable material on disposal practice , and volume of waste generated and disposed of by each waste regulation authority ( wra ) â€” county in england , district in wale , scotland and northern ireland .
each wra is required to produce a waste disposal plan and these are starting to appear .
they too contain useful material on , for instance , the location of site , though individual site licence must be consulted for detail of what each is permitted to take in the way of hazardous waste .
what is not known is information on the location of former site , at which quite toxic material may have been dumped , though recent work by egger ( 1989 ) in austria ha mapped a vast number of such site .
it is these , rather than existing , licensed site which should be a focus of research activity , particularly when examining possible link to ill health .
we simply do not have enough evidence yet to back up the claim that â€˜ people are almost certainly ill , dead , or dying because of these sloppy waste disposal activity â€™ ( openshaw et al. 1989 : 12 ) .
research is in progress , however , to ass possible health effect of poor incineration ( diggle et al. 1990 ) .
particular attention should be focused on hospital incinerator , since these have been subject to crown immunity for many year and most are technically ill-equipped to burn at sufficiently high temperature for adequate disposal of waste ( gatrell and lovett 1991 ) .
we need to produce an inventory of all such site , together with a publicly available , comprehensive database on all site accepting hazardous industrial waste .
emergency planning
it is convenient to retain the nuclear/non-nuclear distinction when discussing the background to emergency planning .
nuclear emergency planning in the uk ha been reviewed by matthew and pepper ( 1981 ) .
nuclear power station must prepare a plan and submit this to the nuclear installation inspectorate and health and safety executive for approval .
evacuation plan are required to cover an area within a distance of 2.4 km ; these detail the role to be played by the emergency service .
the very narrow emergency planning zone contrast with those in the usa ( collins 1981 ) , where planning is required up to 16 km from the site to cover exposure to radiation plume .
local authority in the uk that have declared themselves â€˜ nuclear-free â€™ have been highly critical of british planning , calling for an extension of emergency zone to at least 24 km .
zeigler et al. ( 1983 ) should be consulted for material relating to evacuation behaviour in the wake of the incident at three mile island .
in the uk little work of any description seems to have been done on human behaviour in the aftermath of release of hazardous substance , nor is much available on public attitude to emergency planning .
indeed , no major study of evacuation due to hazard have been undertaken in recent time and this is a widely recognized deficiency which no research funding body seems willing to rectify .
in the usa on the other hand , a good deal of useful research in these area ha been undertaken .
this research is brought together in very useful report produced by the oak ridge national laboratory , tennessee ( sorenson et al. 1987 ; vogt and sorenson 1987 ) and the disaster research centre , university of delaware .
perry ( 1985 ) ha written an interesting book on emergency planning and evacuation , covering both natural and technological hazard .
for him , comprehensive emergency planning and management involve : prevention , protection , response and restoration .
by prevention we understand the elimination or reduction in risk , perhaps by adequate zoning or safe routeing of shipment , for instance .
included in protection are method for detecting likely hazard event and in warning those likely to be affected .
response includes search and rescue ( including the need to cope with secondary threat such a contamination of water supply after an explosion , or fire following an earthquake ) .
a perry and others have noted , relatively few resource are devoted to the first two aspect , the bulk going to response and restoration ( rebuilding ) .
it should be clear from this brief description that these issue are inherently geographical .
in one of the few study of evacuation behaviour after a non-nuclear hazard event liverman and wilson ( 1981 ) give weight to this argument .
in 1979 a train carrying chlorine , liquid petroleum , toluene and propane wa derailed in mississauga , ontario , causing a series of explosion .
this led to the evacuation of 250 000 people which , although staggered over several day , led to great pressure on transport artery .
an important finding wa that many relied on their own transport and went to stay with friend and relative some distance away , rather than relying on the 19 official evacuation reception centre .
despite this , the evacuation wa successful , partly because the accident occurred at a weekend and the immediately affected area wa sparsely populated .
in addition , the police were well prepared , stimulated largely by implementing a plan designed more to cope with an emergency at the nearby toronto international airport than for the event in question .
it is not without interest to note that in the usa there is a federal emergency management agency ( fema ) , established in 1978 , charged with dealing with all type of emergency planning and management .
in the uk there is no equivalent , though the spate of disaster in the past 4 year ( involving over 1000 life lost ) ha led to several call for such a national body to be established .
natural hazard
in this short space it is not possible to go into any depth on the nature of particular natural hazard but only to highlight those aspect relevant to gi .
needle to say , the study of natural hazard is important , a statement brought into sharp focus by the fact that natural hazard account for up to 4 per cent of total death in the world each year ( mitchell , 1974 ) .
in 1970 , for example , more than 200 000 people died in the cyclone and flooding of bangladesh and in 1979 the hurricane david and frederick caused more than $3bn damage in the usa .
shah ( 1983 ) made a comprehensive investigation of natural disaster report for the period 1974â€“80 and concluded that both disaster frequency and magnitude ( measured in term of life lost ) are increasing .
high death toll are still a characteristic of le developed , low-income country .
surprisingly , fog is the third most important hazard in europe after flood and volcano .
research on natural hazard ha a long tradition in geography going back more than half a century .
much of the impetus for such research arose in the usa from observation on river basin management and the need to reduce flood damage .
the vast majority of natural hazard study have ignored the physical nature of the event per se and have concentrated on behavioural issue such a the perception and estimation of hazard .
this emphasis is partly due to the background of the researcher involved ( many are human geographer and sociologist ) and partly because it is only really since the second world war that major hazard have been monitored systematically , culminating , of course , in the last decade or so in the use of satellite technology .
brief reference to two example of natural hazard may illustrate the potential relevance of a gi approach , application of which are considered below .
jones et al. ( 1989 ) have examined the distribution of some 8500 landslide in the uk .
their map show clearly an increased propensity for landsliding in the north and west of the uk .
more to the point , however , is the fact that this distribution can be modelled in term of slope , rainfall , rock type and so on .
in other word the actual hazard distribution can be understood and area at risk identified .
the potential benefit to insurance company , engineer and planner are clear .
second , a well-known winter hazard in the uk , with marked spatial localization , is fog .
predictive model of it distribution have clear implication for road and air safety .
perry ( 1981 ) describes a fog potential index that is a function of local topography , environmental feature affecting fog formation and distance from standing water body .
reliance is currently placed on a network of monitoring station , often sparsely located and frequently outside fog-prone area ; gi could implement a predictive model based on digitized map feature and indeed the model could also be used in site allocation study for airport and the like .
the majority of natural hazard research ha focused on single hazard but of course any area may be prone to more than one hazard type .
this idea led hewitt and burton ( 1971 ) to develop the concept of â€˜ all-hazards-at-a-place â€™ .
to achieve this they concentrated on the whole spectrum of damaging event in an area and explored their aggregate impact .
the implication for gi in the development of â€˜ hazardousness â€™ index are obvious and if time-series data are available there is the potential to produce a variety of probability map .
the collection of historical data on natural hazard is important since it is clear that their spatial pattern varies through time .
many area that appear to be hazard-free on current map may merely by passing through a temporary period of quiescence .
this is perhaps best illustrated with reference to earthquake .
for example , in 1692 , the whole of southern england wa affected by an earthquake whose epicentre wa located in what is now belgium ; a good deal of minor structural damage wa inflicted ( morse 1983 ) .
presumably the builder of the channel tunnel are aware of this ?
environmental epidemiology
this is , of course , a vast subject area , embracing the impact of the natural and technological environment on human health .
it cover , for instance , the association between water and air pollution and ill health , link between geological and pedological environment and ill health , a well a association between specific technological hazard ( e.g. high-voltage power line ) and possible health effect .
we can only consider a small fraction of this work here ; little consideration is given to occupational environment , for instance .
in the uk such research is widespread though there are specialist environmental epidemiology research institute at southampton and cardiff ( funded by the medical research council ) , and others at the university of surrey ( robens institute ) and at the london school for hygiene and tropical medicine .
the latter house the small area health statistic research unit ( sahsru ) , which ha a specific remit to conduct research into morbidity and mortality at local scale ( elliot 1988 ) .
the sahsru liaises closely with opcs .
the kind of database used by these group are discussed below .
greenberg ( 1983 ) and a collection of paper in greenberg ( 1987 ) review some of the literature on environmental epidemiology .
it seems worth stating at the outset that there are two way geographical research in this general area can proceed .
one is to collect morbidity and mortality data ( usually the latter is more widely available , though the former more useful ) and to examine spatial distribution for clustering .
this ha itself generated a substantial literature ( see the paper in elliot 1988 for an overview ) and is nowhere better exemplified than in openshaw 's geographical analysis machine ( see ch. 2 ) .
we then search retrospectively for evidence of possible environmental association .
an alternative approach is to begin by hypothesizing an environmental determinant of ill health and to collect data to test an explicit hypothesis .
if an association is suggested then we would want to collect data in other area to ass the hypothesis further .
of course , these two approach are not clear-cut in practice ; in many case the hypothesis of interest will have themselves been generated by inductive mapping .
one example of an hypothesis which ha been given serious attention in recent year is the possible link between electromagnetic field generated by high-voltage power line or supply cable and ill health .
some work ( perry et al. 1981 ) suggested association with mental illness and suicide , while others ( e.g. wertheimer and leeper 1982 ) have examined link to various cancer , including leukaemia .
a good bibliography is provided in perry and pearl ( 1988 ) .
the central electricity generating board ( now privatized and split into two company ) is funding a large-scale , 2-year project to ass link to childhood cancer , while another recently announced study aim to look at link to sudden infant death .
one obvious problem , with implication for any gi input , is that the effect are highly localized , with little measurable field effect more than a few dozen metre away from the route centre-lines .
accuracy of digital data collection ( either of the power line or residential location ) is paramount .
there seems to be a growing interest in examining association between ill health and the geological environment .
gi work in this field ( e.g. matthew 1989 ) is reviewed later , but we note here the work of the applied geochemistry group at imperial college ( responsible for a series of atlas on regional geochemistry ) and the existence of a society for environmental geochemistry and health .
one area of recent debate concern radon gas and it link to lung cancer .
this is taken very seriously in the usa , where the environmental protection agency estimate that 10 000â€“20 000 death from lung cancer each year are due to radon gas exposure .
estimate from scandinavia suggest that 10â€“30 per cent of such death are caused by exposure to radon ( richardson 1988 : 270â€“81 ) .
the gas is trapped in well-insulated house and is produced by the decay of uranium in rock and soil ; thus it ha been measured at high concentration in part of cornwall and devon .
there are great difficulty in assessing health effect , however , since exposure varies greatly from house to house within small neighbourhood .
carefully designed case-control study are therefore required to measure the separate effect of radon exposure after controlling for smoking behaviour and other risk factor .
other health concern which might merit attention by those involved in the regional research laboratory ( rrl ) initiative include air and water pollution .
the debate about lead pollution from vehicle exhaust emission is well known ; le publicity ha been given to pahs ( polychlorinated aromatic hydrocarbon ) , a product of combustion process and known to be carcinogenic ( lioy and daisey 1987 ) .
research at the north west regional research laboratory ( nwrrl ) aim to create buffer around busy road and junction and to use data from the cancer registry to examine possible link between proximity to such source of pollution and prevalence of lung cancer .
those with an interest in health and air pollution should note the availability of a very detailed database from the warren spring laboratory , containing detail of smoke and sulphur dioxide emission at a large number of site in the uk .
the possible link between nitrate in water and stomach cancer is the subject of much debate ( beresford 1985 ) , a is the association between aluminium content and alzheimer 's disease .
the problem of assessing such link are , of course , huge , but there is scope for a contribution .
the role of gi in hazard management scenario â€” example
a we have already implied , gi ha a role to play in all aspect of hazard research , from hazard monitoring , risk assessment and emergency planning to coping with an event and evaluating it consequence .
the example described below illustrate all these , though a yet there do not appear to be many instance of disaster in the management of which gi ha played a significant part .
technological hazard , emergency planning and gi
a considerable amount of research ha been undertaken to apply gi method to evacuation scenario around hazardous site .
much of this research ha concentrated on network analysis of the road system .
reference wa made above to so-called cimah site , major hazardous installation for which on-site and off-site emergency plan must be prepared .
early research at the nwrrl focused on developing a gi for use by the police and county emergency planning officer in cumbria that would aid in the management of a possible explosion at a chemical factory in whitehaven , west cumbria ( vincent et al. 1988 ; dunn 1989 ) .
the police required a portable system that could be implemented on an ibm-compatible microcomputer .
this wa written at the nwrrl using fortran 77 and the graphical kernel system .
the gi is menu-based and is exceptionally user-friendly ( pl. 10.1 ) .
it open with an outline map and the superimposition of the rather rigid zone used by the emergency planner .
full zoom facility are available at any stage .
in any emergency it is of course vital to have high-quality data on the distribution of population and resource .
the system incorporates digitized data on the distribution of all domestic and non-domestic property in the town together with digitized road centre-line data .
both set of data were provided by pinpoint analysis ltd , who are undertaking this digitizing task for the entire country .
other spatially referenced data incorporated into the system include those on the distribution of school , medical and transport service which can be displayed and supplementary information about them obtained .
the gi permit population estimate to be made for any arbitrary rectangular region on the map .
most significantly , it incorporates a shortest path algorithm which us the road network to find optimal route between user-defined start and end node .
although predating the rrl initiative by several year â€” and , indeed the emergence of gi a a major research area â€” we should remind ourselves of openshaw 's work on appraising nuclear reactor site .
using what would now be called gi skill , openshaw ( 1980 ) examined over 13 000 1 km grid square in the uk which intersect the coastline and related these to data from the 1971 census ( which were made available for such grid square ) .
he demonstrated that there is an abundance of â€˜ remote â€™ site , in contrast to the official view that such site were increasingly hard to find .
he suggested that in early spatial search for site â€˜ only a small number were ever identified in the first place because rigorous search could not be performed by manual mean with poor quality data â€™ ( openshaw 1980 : 289 ) .
heywood and cornelius ( 1989 ) have shown how gi can be used for monitoring possible radiation release and thus the relevance of gi in nuclear emergency planning and monitoring .
they propose a national radiological spatial information system and , in a pilot project in cumbria , have integrated several layer of data within arc/info to show what this might involve ( fig. 10.2 ) .
such data include those from the population and agricultural census a well a point data from rain gauge site and radiation monitoring station .
a an example , the system is used to determine restriction zone for the movement of sheep after the explosion at chernobyl in 1986 .
this is done by finding area of high rainfall and parish with high sheep population .
the map overlay of point and area information show generally good agreement with the restriction zone imposed by the ministry of agriculture , fishery and food , with the exception of a small area to the south-east of cumbria , bordering on lancashire .
the british government carried out a review of it arrangement for handling nuclear accident overseas following the chernobyl accident .
the doe , through her majesty 's inspectorate of pollution ( hmip ) , wa given the task of co-ordinating a national response plan , installing the radiation monitoring network and acting a lead government department in an emergency ( hmso 1988 ) .
the monitoring network ha become known a the radioactive incident monitoring network ( rimnet ) and ha , a it prime responsibility , the detection of abnormal increase in radiation level within the uk of the kind that might arise from an overseas nuclear accident .
the full implementation of the rimnet system will take some time .
by the end of 1988 gamma radiation dose monitor had been installed at 46 meteorological station a part of phase 1 of the development programme .
radiation reading are taken every hour at each station and are transmitted to a central computer which analysis the data .
the current criterion for alert is two reading of more than three time normal background , either successively at the same site or simultaneously at two adjacent site .
the dissemination of information in the event of an incident is dealt with at three level : the first is the normal system of press notice ; the second is the use of teletext and viewdata ( ceefax and prestel ) to get direct to member of the public at their home or place of work ; the third is the use of telecom gold electronic mail to get more technical detail to various official body , such a water authority ( jackson 1989 ) .
phase 2 of the rimnet scheme will be completed by the end of 1991 when between 80 and 90 monitoring site will have been established .
given very wide regional and local variation in deposition , question must be raised about the effectiveness of such a small network ; local authority will need to supplement it with their own network .
there are important analytical problem to be addressed concerning the siting of such monitoring equipment and these are elaborated on later .
abroad , kaspar ( 1981 ) ha discussed a computer-based exercise , with gi overtone , involving the simulated evacuation of the population living in the vicinity of the neckarwestheim nuclear power plant in baden wã¼rttemberg .
in the scenario he outline , an area affected by radioactive release is determined , the population within that area is allocated to reception area ( with capacity constraint ) , evacuation route are identified and traffic restriction implemented .
little detail is provided , however , and it is thus difficult to evaluate his work fully .
dangermond ( 1985 ) describes the use of the arc/info network module for use in emergency planning situation .
he describes how a road network is digitized and subsequently analysed using allocation , districting and routeing algorithm .
allocation determines which arc in the network will be allocated to a particular node or centre ; districting make it possible to outline rapidly set of polygon in order to define specific area of interest ( district ) and to summarize their characteristic ; routeing provides a minimum path algorithm through the network , the arc of which can be assigned weight according to road condition , road capacity and so on .
dangermond give several example in his paper including the use of network for the allocation of emergency vehicle , optimum routeing of fire engine from garage to the accident scene and the movement of spill through sewer and river network .
when combined with the other facility in arc/info , quite complex disaster management scenario can be handled .
for example , earthquake fault zone can be combined with data on housing density and structural detail to predict earthquake damage level and these data then related to the network model .
vincent ( 1989 ) illustrates the use of arc/info and it network module for finding safe route for chlorine transhipment on the road and rail system of north-west england ( pl. 10.2 ) .
in this case , population density data for a 1 km buffer on either side of network arc were overlaid on the network itself so a to act a weight for the minimum path algorithm .
chosen route were those which minimized population rather than distance .
hazardous waste disposal
there can be little doubt that a gi approach ha wide applicability in all sort of location problem where the goal is to minimize ( rather than , a conventionally , maximize ) accessibility to a population .
a particularly good application is to the search for potential site for the disposal of nuclear and non-nuclear ( but none the le hazardous ) waste .
we discus here some relevant work on the siting of facility for the disposal of nuclear waste before examining those for non-nuclear waste .
the major contribution to informed debate about the search for site for the disposal of nuclear waste ha come from a group of geographer ( openshaw et al. 1989 ) and a gi approach to this problem can pay high dividend .
the work of carver ( see openshaw et al. 1989 : ch. 7 ) is an interesting example .
one advantage is the ability to model a range of alternative by allowing particular criterion to enter or be omitted from the polygon overlay operation .
conceptually , what is offered is little more than an automated â€˜ sieve mapping â€™ that land-use planner have used for many year , but the whole procedure is speeded up by many order of magnitude using gi software .
the author examine both near-surface site and deep repository ( which use more restricted population criterion and allow offshore location to enter ) .
data layer include geological map , conservation area , transport route , petrochemical facility and demographic data .
a different layer are added sequentially we see how the proportion of available land shrink ( pl. 10.3 ) ; for instance , when geological criterion are set in the search for a deep repository , 25 per cent of land area remains and the addition of the population density layer reduces this to 24 per cent .
however , adding a proximity to transport constraint lower this to 12 per cent ; and so on .
we can not pretend gi is a solution to the political problem of negotiation and justification but at the very least the openness of the procedure go some way towards satisfying a worried public .
gi research into site selection for non-nuclear hazardous waste ha been almost exclusively conducted in north america and ha yet to be matched in the uk .
jensen and christiansen ( 1986 ) , working in the south-eastern usa , use a raster approach ( with a pixel resolution of 20 m&sup2 ; ) and boolean overlay to â€˜ weed out â€™ particular site , such a those which are poorly drained , environmentally sensitive or inaccessible ( see also stewart 1987 ) .
environmental monitoring of site for the disposal of toxic waste is of particular concern in the usa .
the comprehensive environmental response , compensation and liability act ( cercla ) legislation of 1980 ( â€˜ superfund â€™ ) requires the cleaning up of the worst landfill site , of which some 800 had been identified by the environmental protection agency by 1986 ( foresman 1986 ) .
a major worry ha been the threat posed by groundwater pollution and some research ha been conducted on using gi to monitor this ( merchant et al. 1987 ; barringer et al. 1987 ; von braun 1988 ) .
this is an important topic since 95 per cent of rural household in the usa consume only groundwater , while half the u population consumes at least some groundwater ( merchant et al. 1987 ) .
clearly , the research demand access to exceptionally good environmental data , usually in three dimension .
a foresman ( 1986 ) suggests , we might expect remote sensing technology to play a major role .
merchant et al. ( 1987 ) for example , use the image processing system erdas to construct an index of vulnerability to groundwater pollution ; this us variable such a depth to water table , soil type , slope and so on ( see estes et al. 1987 for a similar approach ) .
barringer et al. ( 1987 ) overlay data on soil type and geology with that of groundwater quality ( hardness and corrosivity measure ) and build a linear model which show that the background environmental variable are good predictor of the water quality index .
again , a raster-based approach is used .
a further example is work by von braun ( 1988 ) who us pmap to look at exposure of organic and metallic compound in groundwater in the vicinity of tucson airport , arizona .
using plume model that predict the movement of contaminant she is able to intersect the result with data on current well location and to ass which water supply area are worst contaminated .
overlaying the result with residential location data provides â€˜ map delineating location-specific exposure for each residence in each time-period â€™ ( von braun 1988 ; 1160 ) .
in principle , it is then possible to compare such â€˜ dos â€™ with epidemiological data , once again indicating a link from a hazard event to assessment of health outcome .
attention should be drawn to foresman 's comment that the u geological survey ( usgs ) ( at the eros data centre ) have successfully linked arc/info to a model of groundwater flow ( foresman 1986 : 260 ) .
this kind of link between gi and other modelling program is to be warmly welcomed and parallel work at the nwrrl in linking gi and air pollution plume model .
natural hazard and disaster management
several study in recent paper have shown the effectiveness of gi in mitigating the effect of major natural hazard .
berke and ruch ( 1985 ) , for example , describe a gi for assessing the impact of hurricane on the texas gulf coast .
hurricane damage through high wind and tidal surge cause an immense amount of destruction and pose a major threat to many coastal community in the usa .
it ha been estimated , for example , that during the mid 1970s some 50 million u citizen were subject to hurricane wind of over 160 km/h and 6 million were subject to hurricane surge ( brinkman 1975 ) .
the system described by berke and ruch ( 1985 ) is raster-based and ha an interesting modelling component called slosh ( sea , lake and overland surge from hurricane ) .
slosh is able to estimate still water surge by grid cell for various hurricane intensity , angle of approach to the shoreline , forward movement speed and landfall location .
additional algorithm are used to model total building damage cost for each grid cell based on water-depth and speed-depth rating curve .
hobeika and jamei ( 1985 ) describe the massevacuation ( massvac ) simulation software designed to analyse and evaluate traffic evacuation plan given a natural disaster in an urban area .
the system comprises three interrelated module : community and disaster type characteristic module ; population distribution module ; network evacuation module .
the type of natural disaster determines the time period within which the road network ha to be evacuated .
the control and management strategy are directly correlated to each specific disaster scenario .
massvac is quite flexible and various user-defined road network option are available such a : traffic signal timing on the road network ; one-way traffic ; reserved lane for special vehicle such a those of the emergency service .
hobeika and jamei have tested massvac on various evacuation scenario for virginia beach city in order to determine evacuation time for flood and hurricane condition .
in general , they suggest that the model provided reasonable and reliable evacuation time .
southworth et al. ( 1989 ) have enhanced the massvac model with the addition of a colour graphic module which they call mvopl .
this module ha been developed using ibm 's graphic kernel system and provides a number of display screen .
the most interesting are those which portray population flow a scaled ribbon on the network diagram , and evacuation shelter capacity a bar diagram .
the diagram are updated periodically during the course of the simulation .
in dry part of the world , such a australia , forest and bush fire are an important natural hazard and some attention ha been given to the way in which gi might help examine their potential impact .
kessell ( 1988 ) describes a pc-based gi called preplan ( pristine environment planning language ) which is a natural area management , land-use planning and fire modelling system developed for the australian national park and wildlife service , preplan comprises four module : a simple raster-based gi ; a grid cell resource database ; a wide range of vegetation , fuel , fire behaviour , erosion and land-use model ; tabular , statistical and colour graphic output system .
with this system real-time fire growth can be modelled , taking into account change in terrain , fuel and temporal change in the weather .
kessell point out that uncontrollable fire are recurrent phenomenon in many part of australia , causing damage in the hundred of million of dollar and significant loss of life .
system such a preplan can provide the user with sound model of the behaviour pattern of fire and are therefore of enormous value in land-use planning .
one of the most widespread natural hazard is flooding , and the potential for loss of property , risk to life and general social disruption is large .
one interesting example of a gi approach to flood damage estimation is the anuflood package which wa developed in the early 1980s following detailed flood damage study for flood-prone coastal town in northern new south wale , australia ( smith and greenaway , 1988 ) .
the anuflood system requires locational and building type data on individual property , stage-damage curve relating the average damage that would result from overfloor flooding to differing depth for each property type , and lastly , flood frequency in term of flood height expressed a a probability .
output from the system is both tabular and graphical .
of particular interest is the construction of map showing the expected annual amount of damage for gridded flood-prone area .
anuflood also contains module which permit the investigation of various flood damage mitigation option , such a property height raising , levee construction and flood proofing .
environmental epidemiology
the possibility sketched above relate to emergency planning , the search for hazardous site , and risk assessment .
to what extent can gi help if an event occurs ?
what , for instance , of the health consequence ?
there is a rich tradition of epidemiological work in geography and with the increasing availability of spatially referenced medical data the scope for gi application is wide ( gatrell 1987 ) .
for instance , diggle et al. ( 1990 ) ass the hypothesis that cancer of the larynx is associated with proximity to a now-closed incinerator and develop a statistical model from the theory of spatial point process to test this .
this would have been impossible without the link provided from postcoded data ( increasingly available from source such a cancer registry ) to ordnance survey ( o ) grid reference .
such a link is provided by the central postcode directory , which match all 1.5 million unit postcode in the uk to grid reference .
when the case of laryngeal cancer are compared with a null distribution ( provided by the distribution of much more common lung cancer ) the hypothesis is given strong support .
although the proposed method is novel , it is but one of an increasingly large number of method designed to detect and model â€˜ cluster â€™ of disease ( see , for example , cuzick and edward 1990 ) .
the work of diggle et al. ( 1990 ) examined only a single point source , though it generalizes to multiple source and to linear hazard a well .
a a second example of health work linked to hazard study we may cite the research being conducted by cross ( 1989 ) on childhood leukaemia .
there are numerous hypothesis concerning the aetiology of this disease ( from ionizing radiation to viral transmission ) and cross 's approach is to collect digital data designed to test some of these hypothesis and to use arc/info to display and analyse result .
for instance , one hypothesis concern the role of electromagnetic radiation , a source of which is overhead high-voltage transmission line a noted earlier .
if these are digitized and buffer zone constructed around them ( fig. 10.3 ) we can quite easily test hypothesis about prevalence in such buffer zone a compared with prevalence outside .
however , we should bear in mind the earlier point about the resolution of the data , since such electromagnetic effect have a very weak effect beyond perhaps 50 m ( wertheimer and leeper 1982 ) .
there ha been a suspicion for many year that high rate of stomach cancer in part of wale may be linked to soil chemistry , specifically such factor a zinc/copper ratio , and to lead content in water supply .
matthew ( 1989 ) ha used data from the regional cancer registry ( morbidity rather than mortality data ) and data on trace element to examine this link .
since the latter are available for a 5 km raster the registration data are assigned to these grid square using the o grid reference .
a with any such study there are the usual problem of ecological analysis and influence of migration , together with issue of separating out the risk factor under investigation from possible confounding factor .
in the usa , morrill 's report on the beginning of a $15m. study into the possible radiation dos received a result of radiation release by the hanford ( washington state ) nuclear complex would seem to cry out for gi skill to be applied ( morrill 1989 ) .
the research project will employ plume dispersion model in order to model dispersal by wind and will need to integrate this and other environmental data with detailed demographic record .
to some extent this line of approach wa anticipated by johnson ( 1981 ) in his study of exposure to plutonium fall-out from the rocky flat nuclear weapon plant near denver ( see zeigler et al. 1983 : 47â€“8 ) .
using soil sample he constructed a contoured risk surface and then mapped excess cancer in various distance band , detecting a marked distance decay effect .
research problem and prospect
the work reviewed above suggests that the application of gi technique to hazard assessment , emergency planning and environmental monitoring is both actually and potentially an important research area with many challenging problem .
undoubtedly , the gi of the 1990s will look very different from their rather primitive ancestor .
computer power is becoming cheaper , digital data more readily available , and gi are becoming hybrid system involving other technology .
in this last section we highlight some important research area a we see them .
doubtless there are many others , such is the surge of interest in gi technology .
data availability
we should first consider the availability and quality of data concerning the population who may be at risk from the storage and transport of hazardous substance , from toxic air pollution , from flooding and so on .
such data are crucial , either for assisting the emergency service to make assessment about resource they need to deploy in such event , or for high-quality estimate to be made of risk .
within the public domain in the uk we must rely in general on data from the most recent population census , the lowest level being that for enumeration district ( ed ) .
a is well known ( rhind 1983 ) these contain on average perhaps 150â€“200 household and 400â€“500 people .
the boundary ed have not been widely digitized , unlike the higher-level electoral ward .
however , the centroid of ed are available a 100 m grid reference and artificial ed â€˜ polygon â€™ can be created if necessary .
in some instance such data will suffice ; in many case they will have to suffice a nothing better is available .
but in some area ed are very extensive physical unit and the shape will be quite distorted .
furthermore , the scale at which population estimate are often required mean that even ed are too coarse for risk assessment .
for instance , in performing it quantitative risk assessment the health and safety executive will rarely deal with distance from hazardous site in excess of 1 km ( petts 1988 ) and census data will not offer sufficient resolution for accurate population estimate to be made .
it should be pointed out , however , that the national radiological protection board currently us 1 km grid square resolution population data from the 1971 census in it radiological protection study ( hallam et al. 1981 ) .
it would seem that , despite the cost involved , they should consider investing in the very detailed digital data ( pinpoint address code ( pac ) ) provided by pinpoint analysis ltd .
this area of application would appear to be an obvious market for such a product .
a useful research exercise would be to compare the use of census and pac data in deriving quantitative risk assessment and this is an interesting research problem .
even with pac data some assumption would need to be made about number of person per household if good population estimate were to be made .
more generally , we need to consider the availability of large database for hazard study and emergency planning and this is an area ripe for major initiative .
in the uk we seem to be lagging well behind the usa in the construction of database on hazardous site and especially hazard event such a those involving toxic release .
for instance , lioy and daisey ( 1987 ) , in their work on the monitoring of toxic air pollution in new jersey , construct what they term a â€˜ microinventory â€™ for each of three town , comprising the location of chemical plant , metal processing plant , paint spray manufacturer and so on .
some attribute information is available for these .
more closely linked to gi is work by mcmaster and johnson ( 1986 ) who perform a very detailed inventory of hazardous material ( both stored and transported ) in santa monica , california .
although the raster display are very crude it is simple to answer spatial query concerning the location of people ( for instance , particular age group ) within certain distance of toxic hazard site .
we have data on some hazardous site ( e.g. those regulated by cimah ) in the uk but a much more comprehensive approach should be adopted , especially if we are serious in the search for environmental association with chronic disease .
the same is true for toxic release .
in the usa the environmental protection agency produce an acute hazard event database while the department of transportation ha a database recording detail of 25 000 release of toxic material ; such detail include locational information , type and amount of material released , mode of release and resulting injury or death ( cutter and solecki 1989 ) .
can we not use gi technology to construct â€˜ risk mosaic â€™ ( zeigler et al. 1983 ) , at a variety of spatial scale , akin to hewitt and burton 's â€˜ hazardousness â€™ of a place ?
in the uk only a handful of county emergency planning department ( cumbria , for instance ) have built up resource database in digital form and this need to be promoted .
further afield , we need to recognize that some disaster ( most obviously chernobyl ) will have implication for more than one country so that environmental monitoring should take place with some kind of international co-ordination .
there will also be movement of hazardous cargo across frontier and there are clearly problem of risk assessment and planning for emergency at or near international boundary , or at sea and airport .
a noted above , the work of fedra ( 1989 ) is a major step in this direction , and link need to be forged between initiative in the uk and the rest of europe .
in term of database on hazardous waste , attention ha already been drawn to the waste disposal plan prepared by wras .
these give detail of present site location and some indication of permitted waste , though little or no information on closed and abandoned site .
however , for detail of disposal the individual site licence must be consulted .
the former hazardous waste inspectorate operated a large database on all site , with detail of permitted waste , including allowable quantity .
the database wa created by a commercial company , aspinwall ltd .
there are numerous potentially usable database for environmental epidemiological work .
opcs , of course , is a major data repository for medical statistic and their various monitor series are of use , though only at the scale of district health authority and above ( see gatrell and lovett 1988 for an example ) .
regional health authority will from time to time produce mortality data at ward level .
however , if cancer are of interest it is more appropriate to obtain data from regional cancer registry ; these data are morbidity rather than mortality data and are therefore more valuable .
the data are postcoded and may have some occupational detail .
for example , we have received data from the regional cancer registry in manchester comprising all cancer of the larynx and lung notified between 1974 and 1984 .
this large data set , to which grid reference have now been added , comprises information about nearly 40 000 individual .
it ha now been copied into ingres , a relational database management system , permitting query to be made about subset of the data ; for instance , all male between the age of 25 and 64 suffering from laryngeal cancer .
other data source on cancer are independent registry of malignancy .
an example is that on leukaemia , managed by the leukaemia research fund centre in leeds ( alexander et al. 1989 ) .
it should be noted that there are frequently major discrepancy between the regional registry and the specialist registry , partly because the latter receive notification directly from consultant .
indeed , working directly with consultant prof invaluable in gaining access to high-quality morbidity data .
two other potential source of data are worth mentioning .
first , attention should be drawn to the new directive from the department of health to regional and district health authority to review the health of the population .
this mean in practice that district medical officer are charged with preparing an annual report , presenting and interpreting epidemiological data , identifying local health problem and evaluating service outcome .
lancaster university is already heavily involved in the work of preston district health authority in this area .
second , family health service authority ( fhsas ) , are also required to take on these role , but from the viewpoint of primary health care service .
fhsas ( which correspond to non-metropolitan county and to metropolitan borough ) hold large database on service provided by general practitioner ; for instance , uptake of immunization and screening for breast and cervical cancer .
is it , we wonder , possible to contemplate bringing together some of these data source to form a national online health information system ( nohis ) , to parallel that for employment and unemployment ( townsend et al. 1987 ) ?
this might include data on morbidity and mortality , uptake of preventative medicine , service use and availability , at both primary and hospital level .
it would be a useful management tool , particularly in view of the reorganization taking place in the national health service .
it should not be forgotten that hazard themselves have geographical distribution yet there ha been little effort in the uk to develop public domain database .
are there hazard-prone area ?
is there a regional differentiation in hazard type ?
these are , of course , commercially important question and a good deal of money is going into the collection of such data by insurance company .
one well known database is major hazard incident data service ( mhidas ) global industrial hazard database which is currently being developed by the safety and reliability directorate on behalf of the major hazard assessment unit of the uk health and safety directorate .
this system currently run in dbase ii and contains in excess of 3000 item of professionally assessed information on major hazard coded into some 24 separate field .
unfortunately , the spatial referencing is poor and with some effort this could easily be remedied .
it is , for example , little use when recording a chemical spill into an egyptian river to have entered into the appropriate field â€˜ the nile â€™ â€” after all , this river is some 6480 km long !
here , in our view , is a classic case of the absence of a geographical input rendering the data of limited value .
in some case , good hazard data exist but have simply not yet found their way into a gi .
a case in point is inland and coastal flooding .
most river authority have detailed map showing the geographical limit of flood in relation to their return period .
within the next decade , a the privatized water company develop their gi , we shall see a massive amount of data conversion in this area .
this development , together with the availability of digital terrain model , will allow quite sophisticated flood forecasting model to be developed within a gi framework .
a sea-levels rise in the next 20â€“30 year due to the â€˜ greenhouse â€™ effect , this very practical use of gi technology is likely to become commonplace and of considerable practical importance .
spatial statistic
another way in which there need to be a rapprochement between statistical method and gi is in the area of spatial sampling and estimation from spatial sample .
this is clearly an important issue in radiation monitoring a the discussion of rimnet above implies .
a an example , peirson ( 1988 ) ha reviewed the evidence on artificial radioactivity in cumbria and show map of the distribution of certain radionuclides a contoured surface .
the point worth making here is that there are many alternative scheme for interpolating from irregular point data ( lam 1983 ) .
one method in particular , kriging ( now available a part of the uniras software ) , offer not only a contoured surface but also an estimate of the standard error at any point on the map .
where this is high it suggests that an additional sampling station might be set up .
such technique are now standard in environmental science ( see streit 1981 for an application to rainfall monitoring ) and need to be promoted vigorously in hazard study ( estes et al. 1987 ) .
related to this problem is that of making population estimate for new zone that result from overlay operation .
we may want to count number living within plume , circle of fixed radius , buffer zone and so on .
existing gi technique usually base the estimate on area-weighted share , so that a new polygon created by â€˜ slicing â€™ an existing area in half is assigned half the population of that source unit .
this may be quite absurd in some case , notably where population is spatially clustered within a physically large ed .
at the nwrrl work is under way ( flowerdew and green 1989 ) to solve such problem using the em algorithm and the statistical modelling package glim , which ha been interfaced with arc/info .
operation research method and simulation
while there is some research activity in linking statistical model to gi there would seem to be scope for further application of operation research technique to gi problem .
within some sophisticated gi such a arc/info there are some useful algorithm ( such a shortest path calculation ) , but gi developer have in general failed to realize the natural link between gi function and many operation research technique .
over the last 20 year or so a large theoretical literature ha been developed to provide tool for the solution of many problem associated with emergency planning ( kolesar 1981 ) .
one important class of problem is the selection of location for the emergency service unit .
the choice of location need not be for permanent base , but can also include dynamic repositioning of unit a circumstance dictate ( kolesar and walker 1974 ) .
in any major emergency a typical problem is the assignment of resource to demand .
if resource are held at one set of location and the demand are at another and the cost of transportation are known , how do we allocate the resource most efficiently ?
in a major emergency , for example , there might be a need to supply blood product held at regional blood bank to hospital .
this , of course , is the classic transportation problem and it solution is well known .
a far a we know the microcomputer-based emergency response system devised by belardo et al. ( 1983 ) , is the only gis-like software to incorporate this algorithm .
in this system , the solution to the transportation problem is shown graphically on the road network , which is displayed on the computer screen .
a a last example of the potential role of operation research in gi and disaster management we are reminded of the logistic problem that faced the peel regional police force during the mississauga evacuation mentioned previously ( scanlon and padgham 1980 ) .
during the early state of the phased evacuation the logistical problem facing the police wa the street-by-street warning of the population to make ready for evacuation .
speed wa of the essence and manpower wa limited .
here , then , is a familiar operation research network problem : all road have to be traversed such that the journey length/time is a minimum .
this is the so-called chinese postman problem , which provides a hamiltonian circuit through the arc ( a compared with the travelling salesman problem which is a minimum route through the network node ) .
