conclusion
task analysis ha not and can not be reduced to a set of standardised procedure which the aspiring analyst could acquire in a formal training course .
the technique just described are no more than guideline in what remains the creative art of studying and describing what an experienced worker is doing or will be required to do .
the danger in being too rigid is that justice will not be done to the flexibility and complexity of human performance .
the danger of being too flexible is that the ergonomists will have an inadequate basis from which to design interface , training course and other operator aid .
if a comprehensive task description can be completed then it is legitimate to enquire why the task can not be allocated to a technical device rather than a human being .
yet without a reasonably comprehensive task description the total system performance may be unreliable or unpredictable .
there is a necessary compromise which can only be arrived at in the context of a particular system .
a mentioned earlier there are always the twin problem of how to get the information and how to present it .
it is sometimes easier to proceed on the basis of : â€˜ if i were doing the job this is how i would do it â€™ , but this can be delusory even for analyst who have past experience a operator .
for analyst who are designer creating a new task there may be no alternative , but any task description arrived at by this method should be checked against the behaviour of real operator a they gain experience .
a particular difficulty about task synthesis is that there is no easy way of confirming completeness .
a task description which is part of a design process should be fully â€” documented in term of the origin of information , the assumption made and the method used .
the standard method of producing task description is to write all the separate sub-tasks at various level on card and then assemble the card in a network .
there is a temptation to make these final diagram very large , but often it is more useful to separate into small unit with cross-referencing .
the optimum size seems to be a3 , this is easily copied and circulated but is large enough to contain considerable detail .
the procedure can be computerised , there are programme available for the easy production , manipulation and storage of block diagram with useful cross-referencing facility .
there is an inherent difficulty in that often the main dividend from a task analysis is from the act of obtaining it .
those not involved find it difficult to gain very much from a presentation of a task description .
like reference book , they have to be used rather than read .
task analysis can be very expensive in skilled manpower , but in looking at cost/value it is important to include in value the increase in mutual understanding which occurs when a multi-disciplinary team conduct the analysis .
if such a team is used the required man-hours can be optimised by using the delphi method rather than a long series of group meeting .
political , economic and social issue which arise in conducting task analysis are discussed in the context of job analysis ( p. 148 ) .
application
man-power planning
the â€˜ human resource â€™ concept of man-power a the central profitâ­m_generating asset of any organisation ha made some progress over the past twenty year .
there wa particular interest around 1970 when company were prosperous , stable and innovative ( ipm , 1972 , patten , 1971 ) .
the objective is to revise and almost reverse the traditional accountancy concept of man-power a a resource consuming factor requiring regular payment , overhead in the form of heated , lighted , equipped workspace and so on .
from the narrow accountancy viewpoint , people are a cost and it is desirable to keep this cost a low a possible .
in these term it is very difficult to justify , for example , sending a member of staff on a training course .
the training requires expenditure and so also doe the replacement for the person away .
where is the return ?
the return is actually in the improved human resource but this is not readily measurable in term which accountant use .
there have been two attempt to reduce human resource to this form of measurement ( giles and robinson , 1972 ) .
one is to acquire a human asset multiplier for each level of staff .
the multiplier for manual staff might be unity but it increase with staff level and becomes three or more for management .
the other is to add together all the personnel cost for each kind of worker from the original job advertisement through to the retirement or redundancy payment .
the first doe attempt to obtain a value , the second remains essentially a cost and not a value .
it would be unduly optimistic to assume that management skill have risen to a level such that cost undertaken can be assumed to be equivalent to value .
a le direct measure which is applicable only to the most senior management is to observe the fall or rise of the share price when a particular executive leaf or join a company .
this can be very high , for a large company a change in market value of million of pound is not unusual .
the most serious consequence of the accountancy view of man-power is that high level policy decision-making doe not give this factor sufficient weight .
for example , the main board of a company will have complete detail of cost and value of physical resource such a building and equipment but relatively scanty data about the man-power which is their most important asset .
indeed it will be described almost entirely a a liability because the cost will be known .
similarly , broad factor such a the state of morale will again only be described by negative feature such a absenteeism , stoppage , strike , low quality output and so on .
it is obvious that company output and project must reflect the man-power and it performance level , but , if this is acknowledged at all , it is only in brief verbal term within , for example , the chairman 's annual report .
the obsession , of current management , with measurement and the reduction of every variable to number for ease of computer manipulation ha worsened the situation .
unfortunately there is no easy remedy .
human resource are too complex a variable to be easily susceptible to measurement beyond the level of counting head and trade .
key variable such a the quality of staff at any level and the current morale are only expressible in general verbal descriptive term .
it is generally accepted ( p. 145 ) that â€˜ hygiene â€™ type variable such a pay and working condition can , if they are inappropriately specified , depress morale and motivation but they will not in themselves result in very high level of these parameter .
morale and motivation are influenced more by condition of work in the sense of work variety opportunity and attitude of management .
these factor are worthy of emphasis because in practice they are still dealt with very badly in many organisation and yet it take little trouble or expertise to make an enormous difference .
for example , very few senior management take the trouble to keep worker fully informed about policy and likely change .
the result is excessive reliance on rumour and continuous speculation which is not only time-consuming but is also highly deleterious in morale term .
the two human factor which can be unambiguously measured are age and sex .
the age distribution of a work force is always an interesting and important source of information .
for most purpose a rectangular distribution is the most desirable because it provides the appropriate mix of youthful enthusiasm and ageing experience .
it also make for regular change a staff retire and are replaced .
many distribution show a peak in a particular age group mainly associated with taking on a large number of young people when the organisation wa started or reorganised , this is not desirable because it lead to excessive competition for promotion at particular stage .
these comment are obviously most relevant for work force where there is little tendency to move out , this is characteristic of a surprisingly large number of organisation in european country and in japan but much le so in the u.s.a.
stable work force have their advantage in term of familiarity with the enterprise and it peculiar need but they do result in problem if the market for the product is erratic or otherwise unpredictable .
this is particularly so in high technology e.g. design and construction of ship , aircraft , power station and chemical plant , where the product unit is large and expensive and the variety of required expertise is extensive .
some peak of demand can be dealt with by using consultant , but they tend to be expensive and do not always fit well with ill-defined but important factor such a the favoured style of the organisation .
in well ordered system it may be possible to calculate the length of in-house experience required to achieve the required level of expertise in particular topic and compare this with the lead time for particular product requirement .
given time available to train new staff it is not necessary to keep more than a nucleus in that particular expertise .
on the other hand , if there is usually insufficient notice of need it may be necessary to keep staff who have period with little to do between project .
it is also desirable to have the possibility of shifting staff between design and operational duty .
this not only provides flexibility in the use of man-power , it also improves communication .
these real difficulty of man-power utilisation are such a to force many organisation to become larger and larger if they are to remain economic .
size provides a cushion to the vicissitude of market but it creates other problem .
for example , difficulty of communication and in particular , how to inculcate the feeling within each individual that he and his performance are important to the organisation .
this is considered in more detail in chapter 3 .
human resource should be fundamental to subject such a ergonomics which are essentially people-centred but the concept remains procedurally undeveloped at any level from a continent through country , region and company down to particular working system .
this is one reason why policy maker never seem to take account fully of the people affected by their decision .
the design of procedure
the increased importance of standardised informational support for both machine and operator ha led to the concept of procedure a a separate design issue within a system ( fig. 1.30 ) .
much of this information can be incorporated in computer program but there is also a need for extensive printed paper for the use of the operator .
this take three main form : checklist , routine and knowledge-texts .
the terminology in this field is not standardised .
software might be restricted , a in this book , to computer program but it is sometimes used a the generic term to include all source of information .
procedure is sometimes used for what are here called routine and sometimes to indicate all the operator 's information support except software .
the situation is further confused when some but not all the operator information support is itself computer-based .
sometimes the term firm-ware is used .
however whatever the terminology used there is a requirement for design effort .
the quality of procedure would be much improved if designer followed some simple guide-lines ( table 1.16 ) .
many document are written on the implicit assumption that the reader is much like the writer in term of expertise and style of thinking .
similarly it is assumed that the condition under which the reading will take place are the same a those of writing which are usually a clean well-lit office .
they may have to be read in the open air , in rain and darkness or within a plant under oily , cramped , poorly lit condition .
the user probably ha limited expertise and he may be working under various stress condition .
the material on which the information is printed , the typeface used , the sequence , the structure and the format generally should reflect the condition of use , ( easterby and zwaga , 1984 ) .
all these thing can be achieved if it is acknowledged that all procedure should a validated by user under the normal condition of use .
checklist
checklist are used in the pre-start mode , for routine maintenance and for fault-finding .
they normally take the form of a series of question or statement which the operator us to guide and structure his inspection of the hardware and other system component .
the sequence is dictated by the optimal order in which action should be taken , for example in starting up an aircraft .
there are many situation when the order is of no great consequence , for example a safety audit of a new machine , and in this latter case some logical structure is needed .
a mentioned above , the logic of the design may not be the optimal basis if the way the operator think and act is different .
baker ( 1984 ) suggests that four kind of shortcoming emerge when checklist are validated in the real situation .
the operator may deviate from the listed procedure because it may require excessive moving about or because he is interrupted by the requirement of other task .
systematic use of a checklist often reveals fault in work design such a inadequate access , visibility and labelling .
the checklist itself may be inconsistent , incomplete , imprecise or ambiguous .
finally the checklist may have reprographic limitation such a inadequate vertical or horizontal structure , poor typeface and insufficient linking of requirement , description , remark and tick-off point .
the international ergonomics association developed a general ergonomics checklist which is reproduced in edholm ( 1967 ) .
there is a deceptive simplicity about such list which requires extensive effort to ensure a comprehensive but coherent content .
the context is , of course , the context of ergonomics a conceived by the designer of the list .
routine
a routine is the most common form of instructional material .
the reader is required to follow a series of step which might , for example , introduce a change in the performance of a complex system .
normally routine are single chain of instruction although it is possible to have some conditional branching .
each step should be simple and complete in itself â€” preferably with some indication that successful completion ha been achieved , for example the appearance or the change of colour of a signal light , or the noise of a motor starting or a change of pressure which indicates that there ha been a change in the flow of some material .
a mentioned earlier a routine can often be regarded a a written task description .
by definition and design intention routine are inflexible .
the user is intended to behave exactly according to their instruction .
no great skill or intellectual effort is required , which is one way of saying that the human operator is being under-used , but the purpose of a routine is usually to avoid common human error such a omitting a step or reversing step .
in general the value of formal routine is precisely that the operator , left to himself , will not normally function in such a systematic sequential manner .
knowledge text
knowledge is contained in document which may vary from single sheet presentation to large manual .
a instructional material it is indirect in that the reader is not necessarily told what to do , a in a routine .
rather he is informed about the situation and the purpose of informing him is to provide a knowledge base from which he can work out his own action .
nevertheless , the relevant action remains important and the knowledge provided should be carefully and stringently selected .
it is too easy to follow the academic tradition and provide knowledge for it own sake .
the â€˜ need to know â€™ criterion is crucial because unless it is applied , the operator can be overburdened with unnecessary material .
correspondingly the material should be structured in term of how it is most likely to be used .
in this context the operator requires a guide for action and not a scientific text-book .
it is invariably better to provide material diagrammatically , systematically or at least in well-structured short paragraph rather than in flowing prose .
consider for example how readily information about the relative position of street is available from a map of a town centre rather than a verbal description of all the street .
such a map will enable the reader to work out for himself how to get from one location to another within the town .
note the enormous flexibility compared with a routine which would tell him only how to get from one particular point to another .
a second important difference is that if he miss his place in a routine he will be totally lost , whereas if he happens to go in the wrong direction while using a map he is still able to make whatever correction is necessary .
however the map doe require more intellectual effort than doe the routine not only in making decision about how to proceed but also in filtering out the required information from the massive irrelevance ( for a particular task ) which is equally available .
thus , one would expect the use of the map to be slower than the use of routine with the human performance being more versatile but le predictable .
with two-dimensional representation such a map it remains important to avoid too much homogeneity and provide substructure in the form of main road and key building or other feature such a river .
see , for example , fig. 5.3
manual which may contain map , schematic diagram and other material warrant separate consideration .
the design of manual
a manual is usually in the form of a book or booklet which is sent with the hardware from the maker to the customer .
it is part of the communication package from the designer to the user .
thus it is a particular form of job aid which provides the operator with information on a system and it performance .
the standard of manual design ha traditionally been very low for several reason .
for this purpose , the designer is too familiar with the product and he rarely take the trouble to find out what the user really need to know .
often the manual ha to be provided hurriedly at the end of a project when the budget is running out , and it is regarded a a tiresome chore still required after the essential design work ha been completed .
the problem is particularly acute for computer .
manual for car , machine tool and so on are poor but at least it is possible to observe the mechanism and their function .
most computer operator do not know or wish to know about the mechanism , and in any case these are not accessible to vision and manipulation .
the computer operator is entirely dependent on the manual , at least in the early stage of gaining familiarity with the system and it function .
he is usually very badly served although the principle of manual design are well known .
for most system the manual is best divided into part a indicated in table 1.17 .
the first part should be a general introduction which orientates the reader towards the system , it purpose , it functional structure and mode of operation and it performance limit .
this should be followed by a separate section on how to set the system up in the first place and how to check that it is working properly .
if it is fairly small it may be necessary to describe how to unpack it , fix it into position , connect it to power supply and start it up .
if it is large , the emphasis will be on the start up procedure .
the section describing the operation of the system should cover not only how the system operates but also how the user can operate it .
extensive use of line drawing , schematic diagram , algorithmic list is desirable , and where prose is used this also should be structured into short sentence with meaningful separation into paragraph .
the maintenance section should indicate what , if anything , the operator is expected to do and the symptom which indicate that more specialist maintenance skill are required .
table subdivided into faults/reasons/m_remedies can be extremely helpful .
part list including description reference number and sometimes drawing are necessary if the user is expected to deal with his own replacement .
a standard fault is to assume that the reader is familiar with the jargon which the designer habitually us .
a glossary of term and acronym used can be very helpful not only to the reader but also to the writer who is forced to consider carefully how he is using various technical term .
finally , all manual should end with an index which supplement the table of content at the beginning .
attempting to integrate under five
policy in islington , 1983â€“8
margaret boushel , clare debenham , lisa dresner and anna gorbach
during the 1980s margaret boushel , claire debenham , lisa dresner and anna gorbach worked in the london borough of islington within social service for pre-school child .
this chapter is an account of the way they perceived attempt to develop integrated provision for under five and the barrier in attitude and system which were encountered .
the first section provides an overview of provision and service in 1983 .
the next section describes attempt to create a clear under-fives policy .
the third section look at the faltering progress towards integrated service .
the fourth section examines the formidable barrier that were posed by reorganisation of social service , local authority cut and the demise of the inner london education authority .
the chapter concludes with a mixture of resignation and determination .
background : under-fives service in islington , 1983
islington is a multi-ethnic , inner city london borough with a child population of 29,000 .
in 1982 , 44 per cent of under five in islington had the use of an under-fives facility , 29 per cent on a full-time and 15 per cent on a part-time basis .
between 1982 and 1988 provision gradually increased , so that by 1988 there were 12 child 's day centre , about 70 playgroups and 350 assisted child-minding place funded partially or totally by the social service department , and 1,075 full-time and 824 part-time place in nursery school funded by the inner london education authority .
the beacon nursery , a local mencap project , offered care five day per week to child over one year of age categorised a having severe learning difficulty .
unlike council day centre , playgroups or community nursery , the beacon nursery wa free .
child were collected from home by special bus and place were available on demand .
the beacon had been in existence for many year and in the past had catered for up to thirty child with a wide range of disability and need .
by 1983 it had half that number on roll .
since 1983 number at the beacon continued to fall a it wa increasingly seen a a resource for child with severe and multiple disability .
in addition the elfreda rathbone organisation , a voluntary organisation helping people with learning disability , ran a free day nursery in the borough for a small number of child with learning difficulty .
some integrated service emerged in the early 1980s â€” one playscheme wa developed through a parent 's initiative , and an integrated adventure playground wa established by a voluntary organisation .
worker from the integrated playscheme subsequently supported a local special school in integrating under five into a playgroup .
in addition to it mainstream school , the borough included five special school providing primary and secondary education to child with special need from islington and neighbouring borough â€” one for child with moderate learning difficulty , one for child with severe learning difficulty , one for child with emotional and behavioural difficulty , one for autistic child , and one for child with physical disability .
in 1983 three of these offered nursery place to child from the age of 2 .
the child using all of these service came from a wide range of ethnic background , though rarely wa this actively and positively reflected in the type of care provided or the ethnic background of their carers .
residential unit
by 1983 residential service were designed with â€˜ care in the community â€™ in mind , but most were based in specialist setting .
for example , field end house had been open for three year a a specialist unit for child with learning difficulty , and wa designed to offer respite â€˜ round the clock â€™ care .
parent had fought for this a a local resource to prevent the need for child to be placed in long-stay placement far from home .
by 1988 field end had been joined by another specialist child 's respite care unit at leigh road .
the social service department also provided thirteen short-term and long-term residential unit for child in care ( by 1988 their number had been reduced to eleven ) , none with wheelchair access and only one with any experience of caring for child with learning difficulty .
the community health team
the community health team had major responsibility for assessing child and recommending provision .
it included physiotherapist , occupational and speech therapist , doctor , psychologist and a specialist health visitor .
they were joined in the network by specialist teacher , funded by the ilea and based in the special school , who visited child at home before they were due to start school .
this group of people met on a regular basis to coordinate service and discus professional issue .
discussion of service to individual child also took place .
this included deciding between day care or school , and parent ' attitude to different option .
often a member of the group wa delegated to advise parent on a course of action .
the community health team aim wa to ensure a place of treatment for child from the earliest possible age , to enable them to develop to their fullest potential .
they were , however , an all-white group , operating without directly involving parent and were generally committed to specialist care in the community .
they gave support to the beacon and elfreda rathbone nursery and most member saw these setting a the best place to â€˜ treat â€™ child .
because of their early contact with parent they often had considerable influence in steering parent towards specialist provision .
the special service team
in the early 1980s social worker were funded jointly by the ilea and islington social service department to work with child attending two special school and two specialist unit for â€˜ partially hearing â€™ child .
these social worker were based in the borough 's special service team .
they all worked with some under five attending the school they covered .
they were aware of a number of pre-school child who did not have adequate access to resource or support when parent and professional were becoming aware of the possible extent and implication of the disability .
when a vacancy arose within this group of social worker in 1983 it wa decided to appoint someone specialising in work with under five with special need .
this might have had the effect of drawing family into the specialist system at an earlier stage .
instead , for a number of reason , it proved to be a key step in the development of the debate about integration in islington .
first , the post wa not attached to any specialist institution .
there wa an expectation but not a contractual obligation that the postholder would work closely with the beacon nursery .
second , the worker had an alternative peer group of under-fives worker who were key figure in controlling access to all other under-fives facility .
third , the worker wa committed to the idea that treatment of a medical or disabling condition should not take precedence over the child 's social , emotional and cultural need .
there wa now a new worker in the network who also made an early relationship with family but who could present a broader choice of day care to parent .
attempting an integration policy
the labour council , elected in 1982 , had a a stated policy aim that every child whose parent so wished wa entitled to a pre-school place of some sort .
this led to the initiation of an under-fives review .
the review team , set up under the auspex of the social service committee , included councillor , senior departmental management and representative of the child 's day centre organiser and under-fives social worker in it membership .
it proposed a network of under-fives centre throughout the borough â€” an idea which the under-fives worker had been anxious to promote for some time .
day care in each area would be organised and coordinated through the child 's day centre which would also provide information , support and resource to parent .
the review had made no mention of child with special need .
the specialist social worker and another under-fives worker with a personal interest in the issue undertook a supplementary review to examine resource for these child .
they interviewed parent , specialist professional and mainstream day-care worker and they designed a questionnaire which wa completed by worker in a large proportion of mainstream council service and voluntary organisation , a well a by some child-minders .
broadly , the survey confirmed that parent of child with special need had not been given a real choice of placement , and that they had to fight hard for mainstream provision if this wa their choice .
parent wanted to be part of the decision-making and did not feel that they were .
many commented on lack of information about the range of facility on offer .
one afro-caribbean woman described how she felt that she wa never given a choice of provision for her son , and objected to being told he should be in a specialist nursery for his own good .
she fought hard to get him a part-time playgroup place in the group his older brother attended .
because the group had known her and her child for some time they agreed to â€˜ have a go â€™ and the placement went ahead .
she commented :
i had to fight every step of the way for this .
no one told me about it , and no one wanted him to have it .
i knew it wa right for him â€” this is where he learnt to join in and be like other child .
it doe n't matter that he 's not like them .
i know what is right for my child , but i 'm not supposed to say .
parent also wanted an increased availability of specialist â€˜ therapy â€™ time , but wanted it to be used in a more flexible way .
some parent were very worried that more integrated setting would lead to lack of attention for child with special need .
definition of â€˜ special need â€™ varied widely in the questionnaire response â€” some defining it a child with disability , some including child with behavioural and emotional problem and some including child whose first language wa not english .
this made the survey difficult to interpret .
the finding were documented in a report a place for all our child ( ccuf 1983 ) and an analysis wa prepared of the practical and bureaucratic problem in gaining an ordinary childcare place for a child with special need , with suggestion for how these might be overcome .
these idea were informed by a paper which had already been produced by an active member of the local mencap group , who had previously tried to generate the debate within the community health service through the mencap committee .
the conference : â€˜ a place for all our children'
a a result of this work the coordinating committee for under five ( ccuf ) , an umbrella organisation of statutory and voluntary sector group and individual providing a service to under five , decided to organise a conference to look at the issue of integration of special need child within mainstream provision .
prior to the conference various group met to produce short contribution to the debate .
these included parent , under-fives worker , specialist worker , child 's day centre organiser and playgroups organiser .
each of these group came up with a list of proposal which were sent to everyone attending .
about 230 people attended the conference , held in april 1985 .
workshop facilitated by the speaker and the conference organising group examined integration policy , overcoming practical problem for child in integrated provision and available source of information .
the conference made the following recommendation :
1
for a formal , universal information system , covering local and specialist resource .
2
for child 's need to be considered with parent and their wish acknowledged and recognised .
3
for employment of extra staff where needed , and employment of peripatetic special need worker .
4
for availability of money for equipment , toy and adaptation .
5
for enhanced payment to child-minders caring for child with special need .
6
for priority status for child with special need in order to facilitate access to ordinary provision .
7
for training facility for staff in ordinary provision , specialist provision and child-minders .
8
for greater communication and sharing of resource between ordinary and specialist provision .
9
for therapeutic and professional staff to reflect the cultural background of the child , and that for all child , including those with special need , their day-care environment and activity to express a consciousness of the multicultural society in islington .
it wa made clear that all of the above area needed funding from health , education and social service before they could be implemented .
finally , it wa felt that unless the attitude of adviser and information-givers became more positive towards integration , none of the recommendation could be effective .
( d ) warfare and crusade
the poet marbod of rennes celebrated ( in latin ) the vengeance which would befall a wicked castellan and his troop :
the poem highlight two important twelfth-century development : the use of force in punishing aristocratic crime ( the fruit of princely peace-keeping ) , and the increasing significance of siege-engines in warfare .
intellectual claimed a formative influence in the second too : john of marmoutier declared that monk from his own monastery had read vegetius ' de re militari to geoffrey le bel when he wa investing the castle of a rebellious castellan , and ascribed his success to their advice on firebomb .
but common sense may have been more important than learning in perceiving the vulnerability of most castle to â€” battering-rams and catapult ; for even in 1100 they were , a a rule , primitive structure .
siege-engines demanded on the one hand skilled operator , and on the other a plentiful supply of unskilled labourer to move the necessary material .
this effectively restricted their use to the wealthy and to those whose claim to assistance under the ban wa incontrovertible .
equally , the improvement in fortification needed to withstand their effect â€” thick stone wall , inner a well a outer defence â€” required income greater than those of simple lord .
the net effect wa that , given dedication , prince â€” louis vi , geoffrey le bel , alphonse-jourdain â€” could subdue rebellious castellans within their own demesne , and could build castle of their own which were impregnable to all but lengthy and sustained attack .
henry ii 's castle at chinon , where the treasury of anjou wa situated , wa the model for it generation , with it hall , upper chamber , tower , and curtain walling , set on a high point above a strategic crossing of the loire .
it could be copied only either by those of nearly equal wealth or by those who could concentrate their resource chiefly on defence â€” prince , great bishop or , in the south especially , town .
all others became vulnerable .
john of salisbury 's comment that not only the town but also the fortress of gaul feared henry i of england wa revealing of the new situation .
similarly , campaigning in the field imposed an increasing financial burden .
the treaty between robert ii of flanders and henry i of england , renewed by their successor , stipulated that each knight supplied to the english king should bring three horse with him â€” this at a time when a horse represented a major investment .
then knightly armour grew more elaborate and costly throughout the century .
and though some knight might be supplied free by feudal service , household one had to be maintained by their lord , mercenary one needed pay , and those with money-fiefs constituted a regular charge on the treasury .
besides , away from their home base , knight were useless on their own .
in open battle and in the plundering expedition that constituted the normal form of aggressive warfare , they needed the backing of archer , both mounted and on foot , and of numerous infantryman .
the usual source of supply wa town militia â€” suger mention those of rheims , chã¢lons , laon , soissons , orlã©ans , etampes , and paris in the royal army of 1124 .
but they might have to be supplemented by mercenary from flanders and the low country , where the rapidly expanding population forced large number of young men into military career .
in the eye of the faithful , these â€˜ brabanters â€™ , with their freebooting and lack of discipline after the end of campaign , were a dangerous a heretic ; in 1171 frederick barbarossa and louis vii agreed to expel them from their land .
their value in battle , a shield behind which the knight could shelter before they launched their charge , ensured their continued employment by those who could afford them â€” notably henry ii of england .
but the hatred they aroused meant that they were liable to be brutally slaughtered if captured afterwards .
inevitably , therefore , war on the grand scale a fought by prince bore little resemblance in practice to the feud of lesser men .
the divergence wa sharpened by the application of a theoretical distinction , the fruit of augustinian theology , amplified a the century went on by canon and roman law reference ; prince pronounced their campaign just war ( fought against aggressor with the aim of re-establishing peace ) or public war ( declared by a competent authority in the public interest ) , while they condemned the war of lesser men a infraction of the peace .
geoffrey martel had perceived the propaganda potential of public war a early a the mid eleventh century ; later it enabled henry the liberal of champagne to claim the military service of all lord within his county , a reapplication of the carolingian ban .
so it could be a powerful weapon .
yet the logic of the definition suggested that it would benefit especially king , whose competence to declare war could not be impugned : thus thibaud of blois failed to persuade the monk of marmoutier that they owed him service when he fought against louis vi ; they claimed discretion in the matter ; and in 1184 , the mighty philip of alsace hesitated to commit to battle the army he had summoned against philip augustus , through fear that it rank might melt away .
the contrast with the united front louis vi and his troop presented to the emperor henry v in 1124 wa marked .
a royal banner now bestowed respectability on a cause ; king ' war were assumed to be for the sake of peace , even when cripplingly expensive and increasingly savage .
this go some way to explaining why the complicated campaign within toulouse in the second half of the century were largely fought out under the banner of the angevin , aragonese , and french king ; and why the revolt of henry ii 's son had widespread ramification .
the twelfth century wa characterized , therefore , by fewer but more protracted war , which smouldered for long period between short , sharp bout of conflict , like the war that covered must of the reign of henry i of england and louis vi of france , the one inaugurated by the great rebellion of 1173 , which outlived both louis vii and henry ii , or that against catalan claim in the south .
into these large number of local conflict were subsumed .
the pattern caused problem , in that the circumstance which might trigger off renewed fighting were unpredictable ; yet the moment an opportunity offered , prince had to be prepared for invading their enemy ; there wa no time for lengthy preparation .
the usual tactic wa to march troop across the enemy 's territory , plundering and looting ; to take fortified place if the task wa easy ; to obtain ally among the lord of the locality ; and to retreat without fighting a battle if at all possible â€” in effect an application to a wider canvas of the some technique of warfare which had earlier made castellans formidable in the immediate environs of their castle .
it necessitated the rapid mobilization of auxiliary to join a core of highly-trained troop kept constantly , at the ready â€” the prince ' military household , which became the linch pin of the system .
john of marmoutier 's life of geoffrey le bel offer a glimpse of how strong bond were created between the prince and his follower within the household , and how a high level of training wa maintained at all time .
geoffrey wa brought up from infancy with the son of neighbouring angevin lord , on whose companionship he relied .
when it came time for him to be knighted by henry i in rouen , he set off for normandy with a crowd of these young men about him .
in rouen , the occasion wa celebrated with pomp , elaborate dress , and a great feast , and then the youth all demonstrated their military skill at a grand tournament , at which geoffrey naturally won the highest honour .
it wa within this kind of environment that chivalry flourished , that young men learned to identify , their vassalage towards their lord a a symbol of their honour , that they debated the relative merit of roland 's and oliver 's way of displaying loyalty ; and that they learned the finer point of jousting .
tournament were disapproved of by ecclesiastic , for they could be both bloody and dangerous .
yet they supplied real need : .
that of an instruction in the martial art compatible with the maintenance of a prince 's peace ; that of an outlet for competitive instinct within the claustrophobic confines of a court ; and that of display to maintain princely social status .
so they flourished everywhere .
they offered opportunity for men of skill to acquire great position in the world â€” arnoul of ardres caught the eye of countess ida of boulogne through his exploit ; and they also conferred lustre on the great â€” geoffrey le bel and philip of alsace were throughout france for their distinguished performance in these show battle .
all this formed a background to the first century of crusading ; and it go some way to explaining the more secular aspect of the magnetism which drew french knight to take up the cross in their thousand .
urban ii , launching the first crusade at clermont in 1095 , presented it a a natural evolution of the peace movement : warrior were to forget their petty feud and squabble in a campaign to promote the interest of holy church .
the war , declared by one whose moral authority wa unimpeachable , wa to free the eastern church , to end muslim guardianship of the holy place , to restore jerusalem to it proper state .
a prince saw it , it wa a just war in the fullest sense of the term ; those who were slaughtered in it battle were regarded a among the martyr of the church .
when raymond of toulouse , robert ii of flanders , robert curthose , stephen of blois , and hugh of vermandois responded to the call , past and present member of their military household fell almost automatically into rank behind them , to test themselves against terrible danger , but also to enjoy the companionship in arm , the adventure , the deed of daring which were the stuff of the chansons de geste .
that they shared , along with all other participant in the crusade , a fervent devotion , a conviction that their sin would be forgiven , is certain .
yet for them crusading necessitated no large leap of the imagination , no millenarian vision ; it wa simply a challenging and a deserving extension of their everyday activity .
in french eye , the hero of the first crusade wa robert ii of flanders .
while raymond of toulouse remained in outremer , stephen and hugh disgraced themselves in escaping from the siege of antioch , and robert curthose , no matter how gallant , at least in his lifetime failed to impress , robert ii 's prestige soared to the sky .
he became robert of jerusalem to his contemporary ; his deed were recorded in the song of antioch ; his pious bravery totally expunged the memory of robert the frisian 's usurpation ; and after his death his son baldwin vii harped on his father 's achievement to justify his substantial use of coercion against the enemy of the church .
outside flanders , robert 's sister 's marriage to roger of sicily wa a mark of his new position in the world ; like louis vi 's sister 's marriage to bohemond of antioch , it stretched french kinship connection into the new norman colony , ensuring that traveller from the homeland would find a welcome in those distant part .
robert 's example wa a potent one .
after the next change in dynasty , thierry and philip of alsace strengthened their shaky claim to the comital position by attaining for themselves the prestige attached to being the outstanding crusader of their generation .
thierry 's remote connection with the family of godfrey de bouillon , along with his marriage with sybilla of anjou , fulk v 's daughter , gave him some claim to the throne of jerusalem ; a a consequence , the aristocracy of outremer viewed him and philip with mixed feeling , expecting much of them , yet fearing their ambition .
in the end , it wa not in outremer but in the newly conquered byzantine empire that the flemish crusading achievement wa crowned with the elevation of count baldwin ix to the imperial throne in 1204 .
but in the twelfth century , the house of alsace could afford the high cost of their expedition â€” thierry went to jerusalem four time , philip twice â€” and the count ' repeated absence in fact promoted administrative reform ; the baillis and the grote bref were a much the product of crusading a the english and norman judicial system and exchequer were the product of the king-duke 's divided attention .
but if , on balance , the flemish count benefited materially from crusading , â€” a , arguably , did the angevin â€” they were far from typical .
for other french house a commitment to outremer often represented a sacrifice of domestic political interest .
the achievement of raymond iv st gilles in consolidating his authority within toulouse were threatened by his departure in 1095 , especially since it wa known that he had no intention of returning home .
then his example lured his elder son bertrand to tripoli in 1112 , and his younger son alphonse jourdain there in 1147 .
had not his grandson raymond v fulfilled his crusading obligation in his early youth , thereby freeing himself from the necessity of interrupting his career in the county , the principality of toulouse might have disintegrated altogether .
william ix 's crusade of 1101 brought about serious financial problem in aquitaine which caused rapid devaluation in the hitherto stable currency ; and robert curthose 's decision to mortgage normandy to william rufus in order to cover his crusading expense exacerbated a conflict over the duchy which wa not settled until the death of his son william clito in 1128 .
all in all , it wa not surprising that suger , mulling over the precedent of early crusading , tried to dissuade louis vii from embarking on his jerusalem journey in 1145 .
yet the appeal of crusading wa , if anything , even stronger to those aristocratic house just below the level of the princely : it wa , after all , the house of boulogne which produced the first two ruler of jerusalem .
the count of nevers , the lord of montpellier , the lord of amboise and lusignan , the trencavels , were fervent devotee of the ideal ; so the problem of absence , shipwreck , death abroad plagued them a much a their prince .
on occasion , their determination to sacrifice for christ played directly into their overlord 's hand , a when the count of la marche , who had no direct heir , sold his county to king henry ii in 1177 .
when it did not , accompanying their prince in a joint crusading venture , a the lord of amboise accompanied fulk v or hugh vi de lusignan william ix , created new personal link .
the political spin-off from crusading are therefore more difficult to calculate than at first glance they might appear .
nevertheless it is clear that the chief gainer wa louis vii .
the irony of this lay in the fact that the second crusade wa counter-productive in outremer , embittered relation with byzantium , and resulted in the death of enormous number of it participant , particularly those drawn from the nonâ­aristocratic class .
yet it consolidated the king 's position within france , partly through the skilled regency of suger , but also because , in taking on himself the leadership of the expedition , louis re-emerged a a credible ruler of men .
his noble gesture appeased those churchman who had been appalled by his early obstinacy over the archbishopric of bourges .
the disaster that befell his army paradoxically heightened his personal charisma : even the baron of outremer continued to look on him a their one potential saviour .
his friendship with raymond v of toulouse , a fellow warrior , reversed the trend of history since the middle of the tenth century , in bringing the prince of the far south back into the king 's mouvance .
the crowd of burgundians who accompanied the king learned to respect him ; on their return , they sought the arbitration of the royal court in their dispute , and they welcomed louis 's intervention within the county that fringed the duchy of burgundy .
a william of st denis put it ,
even a early a 1147 it wa apparent that the crusading movement had provided a new sense of aristocratic cohesion in the realm .
the great joint expedition , the combined leadership , although not without friction , had been justified by their outstanding achievement , the conquest and maintenance of jerusalem .
they were the gesta francorum , the deed of the frank , or the gesta dei per franco , god 's deed performed through the frank , a two of their earliest chronicler entitled them .
but what wa missing before 1147 wa a clear sense of who the frank were .
the second crusade , however , wa launched with st bernard 's ringing appeal to the count and baron of brittany :
in taking his place at the head of the second crusade , the rex francorum decisively hastened the transformation of the frank into the french .
revival under law , 1911â€“1914
few contemporary would have expected balfour 's retirement to lead to a unionist recovery .
the party wa in a mutinous condition in november 1911 and there wa no apparent successor who would have even half of balfour 's political gift .
nor wa it clear that balfour 's departure wa universally desired : at the national union conference a few day later , leo maxse wa booed off the platform , and constituency party everywhere passed resolution regretting balfour 's decision .
a great many crocodile tear were shed , for , since unionist had such an elevated view of the principle of leadership , they were unable to accept that they had just driven their own leader from office .
the shock caused by balfour 's going thus did something to steady the party and it certainly produced a real determination to settle the affair of the party with le friction than had been fashionable of late .
the main contender for the leadership were austen chamberlain and walter long , representing the two different establishment of the edwardian unionist party â€” the opposite world of birmingham and wiltshire â€” but the situation wa complicated by the intervention of two outsider , edward carson and andrew bonar law .
two strong group emerged behind long and chamberlain , about equal in number , but there were few backer for carson or law .
carson thereupon fell out of the contest â€” or rather announced that he wa not standing , for no official nomination had been made .
the party officer and whip were solidly behind chamberlain , but law drew off some of his support among tariff reformer and , urged on by edward goulding and max aitken , he refused to withdraw unless his continued candidature would allow long to win .
against chamberlain it wa remembered that he wa a liberal unionist ( a wa lansdowne , who led the unionist in the lord ) and that he had not been entirely loyal to balfour since the referendum pledge .
long wa supported by the bulk of the english county member but by few of the party 's men of talent .
by the morning of friday 10 november it wa clear that neither chamberlain nor long would secure a majority and that some supporter of each of them would not contentedly follow the other .
a party meeting wa to be held on the following monday and the whip were making preparation for a vote to be taken .
chamberlain provided the way out by suggesting to balcarres that both he and long should withdraw and so allow law to be elected unanimously ; he also turned down balcarres 's remarkable advice that he should allow long to be elected since long would make such a mess of thing that chamberlain would be bound to succeed within the year .
faced with chamberlain 's proposition , and without a majority of his own , long had no option but to agree .
bonar law , backed by fewer mp than any of the other three contender at the beginning of the week wa now to be the unanimous choice .
robert sander reported the election meeting on 13 november with evident satisfaction :
the carlton meeting on monday went off quite admirably .
harry chaplin in the chair a very great success .
a good many men came into the room by no mean inclined to acquiesce in the proposed arrangement .
walter long proposed bonar law .
his speech wa one of the best thing i have heard .
manly and rather touching .
the country gentleman at his best .
he spoke and evidently felt strongly of the degradation that it would have been to the parry to have elected a leader by secret ballot .
austen followed .
when austen sat down chaplin suggested that he had better put the question at once and on getting an affirmative shout did so .
a clever way of stifling discussion and few could have done it so well .
motion carried unanimously .
bonar law then sent for â€¦
his speech struck me a the feeblest of the day .
he wa evidently moved and nervous .
the meeting separated in great content with the conservative party all round .
it wa certainly creditable .
the urgency of the event following balfour 's resignation wa due to the need to find a new leader before the national union conference .
so within a few day of his grudging acceptance by his parliamentary colleague , law 's leadership wa ratified by the throng of party notable at leeds ; here he received a reception more typical of what wa to come in the future .
in 1911 law wa known â€” in so far a he wa known at all in the parliamentary party â€” a a spirited debater .
a. ) .
p. taylor ha noted that law wa trained , if he wa trained for anything in his early career , to be a debater .
in glasgow he had picked up many of the skill that a more highly-born politician might have acquired in the oxford union or at the bar , but skill that were very different in appearance .
his concern wa solely with effectiveness rather than style ; he rarely spoke with more than brief note and left it to the occasion to furnish the word .
( when he became chancellor of the exchequer this practice caused consternation at the treasury , when he used only a page of note to assist him in moving complicated resolution on wartime finance ; he relied successfully on his memory to provide detail and figure . )
a an extempore speaker he wa able to tailor his word to his audience 's reaction and so convey the impression of personal conversation even when addressing thousand .
in parliament he wa wont to abandon an argument half-completed if he sensed that his point had been made ; the speech that appear in hansard were touched up by his staff , and his speech in the country were given to the press in advance .
in debate he wa a formidable opponent , and one that asquith wa never able to master , for all his forensic skill .
again law succeeded by unorthodoxy ; he wa quick to grasp the essential of an opponent 's argument and quite prepared to be rude or direct if the situation demanded it .
in the long debate over marconi in 1913 and over the curragh mutiny in 1914 law wa able to nail asquith 's evasion where a more polished stylist might have been foiled .
occasionally , in the onrush of pursuit , he gave commitment that were later regretted , but these were a small price to pay .
the unionist recovery wa built around law 's leadership , but what asquith called the new style of obstruction , threat and offensive behaviour did not originate with law .
the beginning are to be found in the diehard campaign against the parliament bill .
it wa in june 1911 that the first scene of disorder were mounted in the house of common , led by lord hugh cecil .
in august 1911 the diehard peer used language a violent a any that law wa to use later , and in the crucial decision on the house of lord law sided with the hedger rather than the ditchers .
ulster 's opposition to home rule long pre-dated law 's leadership and he had rarely even spoken on irish affair in parliament before becoming leader .
before he succeeded balfour then , law wa not associated with any of the feature that were to characterize his party leadership ; his contribution wa to use the leadership in the service of all of these cause , to make himself the spokesman for the most spirited section of the party .
law conceived of the role of the leader in an exactly opposite sense from balfour : he sought to foster unity by placing himself at the head of the discordant element where balfour had thrown the leadership on the side of restraint .
their respective position explain this in part : a ex-prime minister and a considerable figure in his own right , balfour had the confidence to believe in his own judgement even when isolated from the bulk of the party ; a the tertium quid in the recent contest , law had neither the weight nor the confidence to take such a detached view .
his strategy wa rather to canalize the enthusiasm of the diehard into party channel , to articulate their view , and to create unity from the sense of movement .
in his characteristic phrase , i am their leader , i must follow them â€” but he determined to follow them from the front .
a a result of this he wa labelled a a diehard and a bigot , when he actually distrusted the diehard and wa himself distrusted by the bigot .
much of his leadership wa thus a form of pragmatic extremism , extreme action and the threat of more extreme action to come , but used in the cause of more limited objective .
he certainly held deep conviction a to the absolute wrongness of the liberal policy that he opposed , but at least a part of his apparent hostility wa assumed for the occasion , a hard line that might secure a better compromise in the end .
so in his first major speech a leader in parliament he wasted little time on the customary compliment and warned the government that he intended to play hard .
in private conversation he told asquith i am afraid that i shall have to show myself very vicious mr asquith this session .
i hope you will understand .
in later conversation about ulster , law wa frank enough to point out that bloodshed in ulster would be the best way of ensuring an electoral landslide for the unionist .
there wa then an open toughness about party tactic ; before the buckingham palace conference in 1914 he had central office work out the electoral effect of excluding nine , six or four county of ulster from an independent ireland the concern of a calculating pragmatist not of a bigot .
from the party viewpoint law 's method were successful but dangerous ; expectation raised when the party wa in full cry were not easily set aside for an eventual compromise , and the only outcome that would fully vindicate the method wa the recovery of power .
the basis of recovery wa then law 's fighting capacity a leader .
in parliament there would be a carefully organized campaign of resistance that would at least slow the government down and raise unionist morale .
improved morale would also make possible organizational advance outside parliament , all linked to the issue of ulster , and with the sight fixed firmly on the next election , an election that would make or break the union , the empire and law 's leadership of the party .
all wa linked to the sense of a new departure , a new style , a new regime in politics and a new team at the helm : a well a a new leader , the party had by the middle of 1912 a new chief whip , party chairman , party treasurer , principal agent , press adviser , and an almost entirely new team of whip and organizer .
party organization provided the easiest advance because it wa the only area entirely under the party 's control .
no general election wa fought by the organization set up after 1911 , but there is little doubt that there wa a massive improvement ; criticism that were commonplace in 1910 were not to be heard by 1913 .
the space available to central office wa increased by the acquisition of st stephen 's house a well a st stephen 's chamber , which had been occupied since 1874 ; the new office cost $3,000 a year in rent alone .
the quality of the senior staff wa improved by the appointment of three specialist .
john boraston took over a principal agent in 1912 , having been a professional organizer all his career and latterly chief agent to the liberal unionist council .
he joined william jenkins , appointed chief organizing agent in 1911 and previously district agent for the midland liberal unionist .
the party wa indeed handed over to birmingham .
finally , malcolm fraser , ex-editor of the standard and of the daily express became first honorary press adviser and subsequently wa employed to run the press bureau at the colossal salary of â£1,200 .
steel-maitland , boraston , jenkins and fraser made up a team of expert , all of sufficient status to deal with politician who might call into the office ; the work of the office wa departmentalized for the first time and the head of department brought together into a supervising board .
the influence of central office toned up the rest of the organization .
a new canvas system wa introduced in 1911 so that all county constituency would operate the same system and so that duplicate record could be kept at area level .
this wa important at a time when outvoters were a larger group in many constituency than the size of the majority and when the outvoters were overwhelmingly unionist .
in 1914 a register of car owner wa compiled , also important when some voter could cast several vote in an election when polling went several day .
by november 1912 only fifty-one constituency in great britain were without candidate , a much better position than the position at dissolution in either 1909 or 1910 ; few of these fifty-one constituency were winnable , but in any case most of them had candidate by the end of 1913 .
a programme of speaker for a general election wa also ready by 1913 , with both constituency and politician told in advance what they might expect .
the financial mismanagement that had reigned before 1911 wa ended with the employment of professional accountant .
expenditure on central office , district office and the london office doubled from â£32,466 in 1909â€“10 to $68,957 in 1913â€“14 ; the total expenditure of the party organization from central fund also doubled from $73,000 to over â£150,000 .
to pay for this outpouring , a great drive wa mounted for additional income -by farquhar and steel-maitland .
in 1912 ordinary subscription still brought in only â£12000 a year and it proved difficult to raise them to anything like what wa needed .
the method adopted wa to canvas for capital donation that could then be invested to bring in a regular income , and both balfour and law were used to raise the wind .
steel-maitland told balfour that farquhar and i are endeavouring to raise more money .
i think we are likely to succeed â€” thanks in part to the very generous example set by lord rothschild .
but i would b , e very glad if i might see you on your return to ask you to help me in two case .
systematic collection were made from peer and from the city ; by the outbreak of war the invested fund amounted to $671,000 â€” twice the sum in 1911 and worth four year expenditure â€” and there wa a special cash deposit of â£120,000 for the coming election .
in three other area , a much political a organizational , advance were made towards the management of the press , the coordination of the heterogeneous collection that made up the party , and the fostering of modern attitude in the local party .
under malcolm fraser the party began to make effective use of it support in fleet street .
it had been recognized that liberal had been more adept at handling the press because they were more ready to stoop to newspaper practice , a sandars had reported in 1909 :
i told northcliffe that if his people would make enquiry at our central office , he would find that our nightly regiment of speaker wa at least a well worth reporting a the radical contingent , but that i realized that from the press point of view , no doubt , our speaker did not play up to the reporter by> handing them their speech in advance , and by other trick of the kind to which the radical orator have recourse .
this wa the essence of the problem : the press could only be managed by someone who knew their method and who would not expect them to come round to central office to search for news .
the uoc found that it is patent that some feeling exists among the unionist press that , while in the past they have done much for the party , the party ha not assisted in their work .
in many quarter it is asserted that the unionist press are treated with greater courtesy by the radical than by their own side .
fraser dealt with this problem without difficulty and explained to law in 1913 that the importance of getting important speech in advance is shown in concrete manner by your wallsend speech on october 29th .
the verbatim report reached most of the newspaper in place such a aberdeen at one o clock in the morning of the 30th .
he went on to quote the response that had been received from editor :
manchester dispatch : i hope you will continue this form of enterprise .
you fully know a an old pressman the difficulty of dealing with a big speech late at night â€¦
daily express : i am much obliged to you for the precis of mr bonar law 's speech last night .
it wa a great help to u .
the speech came over the wire so late and the sheet were so confused by the post office â€” which is usual in these case â€” that without the precis we could not possibly have got the leader through in time for the first edition .
so in the supply of information to newspaper predisposed to support the party , much wa achieved merely by employing a specialist .
the other side of press management , depending on informal contact , is le easy to ass .
in 1911 steel-maitland invited blumenfeld of the daily express to central office for a two-way exchange of idea and advice .
he also used law himself to impress editor who were on le familiar term with fraser , a with marlowe of the daily mail , and law took a direct interest in negotiation with the most difficult of all the unionist editor , j. l. garvin of the observer .
law personally conducted the negotiation with gardner sinclair over the sale of the observer and the pall mall gazette in 1914 ; this negotiation also demonstrates that , a well a producing information and nursing editor , the party had to work hard to keep some of the newspaper in business .
sinclair did buy the observer and the pall mall in the end , but not before he had been given a $45,000 guarantee from party fund .
such investment had a double purpose , for they could bring in income and provide a political bonus .
one such scheme involved the purchase of the daily express by max aitken with party fund , but the party wa also propping up the globe , the standard , and a wide range of provincial daily .
all these transaction were secret , for there would be little use in keeping a paper alive if it were known to be owned by a political party .
the paper were therefore bought through nominee or bank loan backed with party capital .
it wa usual to extract some form of guarantee in exchange for party support but the secrecy prevented such undertaking from being enforced .
sinclair thus gave an undertaking that his paper would always remain unionist , but steel-maitland doubted if such a promise had any value .
the later history of the daily express suggests not : when the express became hostile in the 1920s , younger raged when i think of the large sum of money this office ha put into that gutter print it make my blood boil .
the justification for pouring out large sum without a certain return wa the even greater cost of any real alternative .
the support for the express in 1912 provides for the introduction of â£50,000 or $60,000 to retain the only half-penny paper the party posse in london , and no le a sum than $700,000 to â£1 , 000,000 would be necessary to start a similar paper if the daily express were allowed to go .
the struggle therefore went on to keep the unionist press alive , and in 1912 and 1913 both aitken and northcliffe stood by the party and gave valuable support in their paper .
the coordination of competing party body wa carried out with surprising ease .
the national union wa merged with central office in 1911 and fusion with the liberal unionist followed in 1912 .
joseph chamberlain retained his preference for independence but allowed himself to be overruled by austen and by the overwhelming wish of the liberal unionist council to merge .
to cater for all susceptibility , the party became the national unionist association of conservative and unionist association â€” a decision that allowed some local party to go on calling themselves conservative , but the name liberal wa at last dropped .
the liberal unionist were admitted to the carlton club and a longstanding source of tension wa eradicated .
this wa in itself a striking commentary on what had already been done : liberal unionist who had resisted amalgamation under balfour and acland-hood in 1911 acquiesced meekly under law and steel-maitland in 1912 .
other organization could not be absorbed but could still be brought within the central office orbit .
so in 1911 steel-maitland offered to join the halsbury club in order to guide it on to safer line â€” and wa immediately accused of disloyalty by walter long .
long helped though by placing both steel-maitland and boraston on the executive committee of the union defence league for the same purpose .
steel-maitland also explained to lord robert cecil that it ha been arranged that the national conservative union should send out leaflet produced by several of the ancillary organization , such a the tariff reform league , the anti-socialist union , the primrose league , the london municipal society etc .
the central office attitude wa flexible , for outside body would always exist and could even be made use of :
they represent a time when either their object were not the same a those of the official organization , or the cco wa not enterprising enough to suit them .
but some caution should be observed .
it may be wise , while retaining or regaining control , to trade under different name .
in some case additional subscription may be obtained , in others full responsibility may be avoided .
with open ally like the primrose league , the case for collaboration wa strong ; for the first time in thirty year of existence the primrose league formally linked itself with the party in 1914 .
on the other hand , the independence of the tariff reform league wa a positive advantage : after january 1913 the party supported the tariff policy but wa not including them in it immediate programme , so that central office could continue to distribute tariff propaganda from the trl without compromising party policy .
in general , effort to ensure better coordination were a matter of the patient building up of contact , although the improved party morale and the greater efficiency of central office had removed their justification for existence .
3 .
steady current
3.1 .
the basic equation
in this chapter we shall be concerned with phenomenon in which the main role is played by the current of charged particle .
all our variable can be function of space but are independent of time .
it is easy to present the relevant equation ; we have all of eqns ( 1.1 ) -(1.7) but have to substitute a/at = o , yielding formula .
there is a difficulty with eqn ( 3.6 ) .
it is correct for most of the chapter with formula taken a a constant but break down for ferromagnetic material , which will be discussed in section 3.11 .
all the other equation are all right but will not necessarily provide the simplest starting point for solving a given problem .
we shall , therefore , introduce a number of alternative formulation .
first , we could search for some analogue of the potential function which proved so useful in electrostatics .
we found there that by choosing a scalar function in the form formula we could automatically satisfy the other equation for the electric field strength , formula .
can we do the same thing for the magnetic quantity ?
no , but we can do a similar thing .
since it is the equation formula that need to be satisfied , we should choose the potential a a vector ( called , not without logic , the vector potential ) , defined by the equation formula .
substituting the above equation into eqn ( 3.1 ) we get formula , or using the vector relation ( eqn ( a.6 ) ) we obtain the modified form formula .
this equation can be further simplified to formula by choosing ( in the physicist 's jargon this is called choosing the gauge ) formula .
can we do that ?
one ca n't usually assign some arbitrary value to the divergence of a vector function .
in the present case , however , we do have some freedom of choice .
the definition of a by eqn ( 3.8 ) is not unique .
if we add to a the gradient of a scalar function , the resulting vector a' still give the same magnetic field because formula .
hence a suitable choice of will ensure that formula .
so we are left with eqn ( 3.11 ) .
if the current density is specified , eqn ( 3.11 ) will provide the solution for a from which b can be determined .

there is nothing easier .
