we have now a number of equation describing the same thing .
which equation should we use in a practical case , the equation for the vector potential , ampã¨re 's law , biot-savart 's law , or attack directly maxwell 's equation ?
it 's hard to tell .
experience with practical calculation help , but even after year of work one usually remains this side of infallibility .
i regret to say that there are no general guideline available .
if one manages to find the simplest method at the first attempt that can mostly be attributed to good luck .
can we claim then that the vector potential is a useful a it scalar counterpart in electrostatics ?
for computational purpose the answer is an unambiguous no .
it is always laborious to find the component of a vector , so we are not much better off with a than with h or b. it turn out however that a is a more basic quantity of physic than b. since b is given by the curl of a it is possible that a is finite while it curl is zero .
interestingly , under these condition a ha an effect on certain quantum-mechanical phenomenon
it would be unfair both to the vector potential and to quantum mechanic to say that none of those formulation have engineering application ( in fact the most sensitive magnetometer built to date is based on that kind of theory ) , but by and large engineer would n't lose much sleep if the use of a were banned with immediate effect .
the vector potential is not a popular variable among engineer , maybe because it is not directly measurable .
there are no instrument capable to measure the magnitude or direction of a.
in my opinion a is a useful thing if used with moderation .
it helped u to derive biot-savart 's law , it will come handy later in solving certain radiation problem , and it often lead to nice formula , e.g. for the magnetic flux crossing a surface , defined a formula that may be rewritten in term of the vector potential a follows : formula where c is the curve enclosing the surface .
we have now collected a good number of formula which will serve u well in the following section .
before going on , just a few word about the classification of steady current .
it may be roughly divided into two part : magnetostatics and the rest .
we shall discus the rest first ( section 3.2â€“3.5 ) , and that will give u some idea of the relative significance of electric and magnetic field .
section 3.6â€“3.16 are concerned with magnetostatics , where electric field are assumed to be zero and the interrelationship of j , h , and b are studied .
since we have so many variable , and since in each physical configuration only some of them appear , we will record ( just for this chapter ) the non-zero variable in each section heading .
3.2 .
the defocusing of an electron beam ( j , e , d , p , h , b )
we have so far talked about positive and negative charge , about point charge , and distributed charge .
i shall now introduce the concept of an elementary charge , 1.6 ã— 10 -19 c , carried by an elementary particle called the electron .
this is not a necessity .
it is possible to study electromagnetic theory without ever mentioning the word electron , but since it ha become such a household word and is used so often we can just a well make use of it .
in the present section we shall consider a cylindrical electron beam of radius a in which the charge density is uniform ( p = po ) and all electron travel with velocity v.
we are not going to enquire into the detail how such beam can be produced ( it belongs to the subject of physical electronics ) ; we shall accept the fact that the beam exists and will try to work out the force on the outermost electron .
there is now cylindrical symmetry and a net charge per unit length formula , yielding for the radial component of the electric field formula .
the magnetic field may be obtained from ampã¨re 's law ( eqn ( 3.21 ) ) a follows formula , where the line integral is taken over the circle of radius a ( fig. 3.2 ) , i is the total current of the beam and formula is the azimuthal component of the magnetic field in the cylindrical coordinate system specified by formula] .
from eqn ( 3.26 ) we get formula] .
the electric force on an electron of charge e travelling at the edge of the beam ( r = a ) is it is in the radial direction pointing outwards .
the magnetic force is perpendicular both to the direction of motion ( +z axis ) and the direction of magnetic field ( formula] direction ) .
the vectorial product v x b give an inward force in the radial direction .
hence the net force on the electron is formula] .
noting that formula] and anticipating the relationship formula] to be derived in section 5.7 , we get the following formula for the force formula] , where c is the velocity of light .
it may be seen from the above equation that the magnetic force is negligible in comparison with the electric force unless the velocity of the electron approach the velocity of light .
thus the conclusion is that , owing to the repulsive force between the electron , a cylindrical electron beam is unstable .
using a technical term borrowed from electron optic we could say that the electron beam get defocused .
the other conclusion ( a far a it is permissible to generalize from a single example ) is more important in principle .
it is concerned with the relative magnitude of electric and magnetic force .
since charged particle rarely travel close to the velocity of light we may conclude that the magnetic force are by order of magnitude smaller than the electric force .
why is it then that we have no difficulty in practice in observing magnetic field ?
the reason is , of course , that there are two kind of electric charge and their effect usually cancel , so the small magnetic field ha a chance of getting observed .
and that brings u to the ext example .
3.3 pinch effect ( j , h , b )
in contrast to our previous example we shall now investigate a cylindrical beam consisting of two kind of charge carrier : negative electron and some positive particle , which i do not wish to be more precise about at the moment .
we shall assume that the two kind of particle have equal density and move in opposite direction .
a a result there is no net space charge and hence no electric field .
the current , on the other hand , do not cancel because they represent opposite charge moving in opposite direction ( remember , minus one time minus one is equal to plus one ) .
the magnetic field strength is given again by eqn ( 3.27 ) ; we only need to substitute for the current in + ip where the subscript n and p refer to negative and positive particle respectively .
hence the magnetic force on the electron at the edge of the beam ( at r = a ) is formula] , and on the positive particle is formula] .
note that the force is inwards , and in the same direction for both particle .
let u distinguish two case .
both particle are mobile
in that case both of them will move inwards under the effect of magnetic force .
but if the diameter of the beam is reduced , eqns ( 3.33 ) and ( 3.34 ) tell u that the force are even larger .
what happens then ?
if everything wa uniform then the beam diameter would go on decreasing .
in practice however the beam is not uniform and doe not posse perfect cylindrical symmetry .
under these condition the motion of the particle is fairly complicated , and of course our model is unable to predict the detailed behaviour of the particle .
nevertheless a few qualitative conclusion may be drawn without doing any further mathematics .
if the cross-section happens to be smaller at a certain place , then the force are larger there than at the neighbouring cross-sections , so the beam will be further constricted , etc. , leading to the so-called sausage instability ( fig. 3.3(a) ) .
if the magnetic field happens to be larger at one side than at the other side , then the beam will be deflected towards the weaker field which make the field even weaker , etc. , leading to the so-called kink instability ( fig. 3.3(b) ) .
the positive particle is immobile
in practice this mean that the positive ion are part of the crystal lattice .
there is then no magnetic force on the ion , only on the electron .
so the electron want to move inwards but can not because the ion hold them back by virtue of their electrostatic attraction .
naturally , if the ion attract the electron the converse is true a well , i.e. owing to the magnetic force on the electron , there is also an inward force upon the ion .
so the whole crystal structure try to contract or in other word the material is under pressure ( see example 3.2 ) .
in any practical situation this pressure is small and can be neglected when calculating the force on current-carrying conductor .
3.4 .
flow pattern and ohm 's law ( j , e , d )
up to now we have not enquired into the question of how the current arose .
we just assumed that certain charge carrier moved with certain velocity .
now we shall say that in a class of material the current density at any point is proportional to the electric field ; in mathematical form formula] , where formula] is the conductivity of the material .
the above equation is spite of it utmost simplicity represents an extremely good guess .
it is valid for nearly all material up to quite high electric field .
will there be a magnetic field ?
yes , of course , whenever there is a current there is a magnetic field a well .
but the effect of the magnetic field upon the motion of the electron is nearly always negligible ( one exception causing the pinch effect is mentioned in the previous section ) .
thus we may safely ignore the magnetic field when determining the line of current flow .
we are then left with only one equation formula] or formula] or formula] , depending on our preference .
eqn ( 3.38 ) tell u that there is some analogy ( see example 3.3 ) with the electrostatic case treated in chapter 2 .
we may , in fact , reinterpret any of the diagram of fig ( 2.25 ) -(2.31) by assuming that the whole space is filled with a material of conductivity formula] and the field line are now the line of current flow a well .
is the analogy perfect ?
not really , because ( 1 ) a current flow there is some potential drop in the electrode itself , ( 2 ) zero conductivity for part of the space can not be electrostatically modelled since there are no dielectric with er = 0 , and ( 3 ) when current flow through two material of different conductivity there is generally a surface charge at the boundary ( see example 3.4 ) .
it is not worth discussing any of these complication because one is rarely called upon to work out line of current in a conductor .
we shall rather return to a very simple geometrical configuration for deriving ohm 's law .
we shall take a piece of cylindrical material of length l and cross-section s and apply a voltage between the end .
the electric field is then formula] , the current density formula] , and the current formula] .
from circuit theory formula] , where r is the resistance of the material .
comparing eqns ( 3.41 ) and ( 3.42 ) we get formula] .
since formula] is by definition the resistivity we get the result you learned in school that the electrical resistance is proportional to the resistivity and the length of the sample and inversely proportional to it cross-section .
a more general definition of resistance valid for varying cross-sections may be easily arrived at , but it is hardly worth the trouble .
the essential thing is that the relation formula] is equivalent to ohm 's law .
risking the dismay of circuit engineer some theoretician do , in fact , refer to eqn ( 3.35 ) a ohm 's law .
prologue
post-impressionism : royal academy of art , london 1980
he signed the friend ' book in his elegant handwriting : alexander wedderburn , january 22nd , 1980 .
she had said , peremptory a always , that he wa to go straight to room iii , where the miraculous stuff wa to be found .
early .
so here he wa , a distinguished public man , also an artist of a kind , stepping obediently through room i ( french 1880s ) and room ii ( british 1880s and 1890s ) , on a lead grey morning , to the pale grey , classical , quiet place where the bright light shone and sang off pigment so that the phrase , miraculous stuff , seemed merely accurate .
on one long wall hung a row of van gogh , including an arles poet ' garden he had n't seen before , but recognised , from small photograph , from charged description in the painter 's letter .
he sat down and saw a bifurcated path , simmering with gold heat round and under the rising , spreading blue-black-green down-pointing vane of a great pine , still widening where the frame interrupted it soaring .
two decorous figure advanced , hand-in-hand , under it suspended thickness .
and beyond , green grass and geranium like splash of blood .
alexander wa not worried about whether frederica would turn up .
she wa no longer in the habit of being late : her life had schooled her to temporal accuracy , perhaps to being considerate .
he himself , at sixty-two , felt , not quite accurately , that he wa now too old , too settled , to be put out , by her or by anyone else .
he thought with warmth of her certain approach .
there had been a pattern , an only too discernible repetition in the event and relationship of his life into which she had ruggedly refused to fit .
she had been a nuisance , a threat , a torment and wa now a friend .
she had suggested that they look at van gogh together , setting up another form of repetition , deliberate , contrived and aesthetic .
his play , the yellow chair , had first been presented in 1957 ; he did not like to think too closely about it , a he did not like to think too closely about any of his past work .
he stared at the serenely impassioned garden made out of a whirl of yellow brushstrokes , a viridian impasto , a dense mass of furiously feathered line of blue-green , isolated black pot hook , the painfully clear orange-red spattering .
he had trouble finding an appropriate language for the painter 's obsession with the illuminated material world .
he would have been lying if he had recorded only the more accessible drama of the painter 's electric quarrel with gauguin in the yellow house in arles , the distant necessary brother who supplied paint and love , the severed ear delivered to the whore in the brothel , the asylum fear .
at first he had thought that he could write a plain , exact verse with no figurative language , in which a yellow chair wa the thing itself , a yellow chair , a a round gold apple wa an apple or a sunflower a sunflower .
sometimes he still saw the brushstrokes , a it were , in this naked way , so that his earlier thought of this garden had to be undone , the idea of black wing to be stripped from the painted leafage , the vulgar idea of blood splash washed off the notation of geranium .
but it could n't be done .
language wa against him , for a start .
metaphor lay coiled in the name sunflower , which not only turned towards but resembled the sun , the source of light .
van gogh 's idea of thing had also been against him .
the yellow chair , besides being brushstrokes and pigment , besides being a yellow chair , wa one of twelve bought for a company of artist who were to inhabit the yellow house , the white wall of which should blaze with sunflower a the window of gothic cathedral blazed with coloured light .

one thing always linked to another thing .
